The true adept Zhang Ziyang said: 'At this stage of refinement, the nine palaces stir with wind, the Crimson Palace shines with moonlight, the Cinnabar Field blazes with fire, the Sea of Grain ripples with clarity. The spine turns like a wheel, the four limbs become as steadfast as mountains, the pores rise like one freshly bathed, the bones and channels sink like one in deep slumber. The spirit rejoices as if in conjugal union, the hun and po linger like mother and child.' This is its realm, not a mere metaphor.

What has been described above may be called the ultimate depiction."

The yin image should descend in front, while the yang light may be planted behind.

Though the operation of yang fire and yin tally arises naturally, human effort also exerts a subtle transforming influence—this must not be overlooked.

From the *weilü* it ascends to the *niwan*; this path along the spine is called “advancing the yang fire.” From the *niwan* it descends to the *qihai*; this path along the front of the chest is called “retreating the yin tally.” Ascending is advancing; descending is retreating.

Moreover, when ascending behind, one feels the body suffused with rising heat; when descending in front, the heat gradually settles into cool stillness. Thus, the flourishing of heat is “advancing the yang fire,” and the calming of heat is “retreating the yin tally.” Though the two explanations differ in meaning, their principle is one and the same. Within this lies many subtleties—these should be studied.

Atop the mountain and beneath the sea, after rain comes a clap of thunder.

The Perfected Lü Chunyang’s *Lyrics of Pacing the Toad Palace* says: “The earth’s thunder shakes the mountain’s rain.” The *Hundred-Character Stele* says: “Yin and yang arise in reversal; the universal transformation is a single thunderclap.” Master Shao Kangjie’s poem says: “Suddenly at midnight a thunderclap—ten thousand households, a thousand gates open in turn.” The Perfected Zhongli Quan says: “The adept gathers the primordial qi; all night the thunder rolls without cease.” Master Peng Helin says: “Those on the Nine Flowers Peak know—one night of wind and thunder shakes ten thousand mountains.” The alchemical classics speak of thunder in many places; they cannot all be recounted. Their source lies in the *Zhouyi* hexagram “Return of the Earth’s Thunder,” yet in truth they all describe the phenomenon of the primordial qi accumulating over time, its power growing mighty, and responding to the moment with activation.

When this qi arrives, all the gates and orifices of the body open at once: the ears hear the sound of wind, the back of the head trembles, the eyes flash with light, the nose twitches—these and other signs should be known in advance to avoid alarm and confusion when they occur.

However, for women practitioners to reach this stage, it must be after the cessation of menstruation. The state described in Sun’s poem occurs before the slaying of the dragon and thus may not yield the same effect. Here, the term “thunder” likely refers only to the qi surging upward from the sea of blood to the two breasts during practice. The arising of this qi is called by alchemists the *huo zishi* (“living midnight”). The “mountaintop” symbolizes the two breasts and the *shanzhong* region; the “sea bottom” symbolizes the uterine sea of blood. “Rain” symbolizes yin qi; “thunder” symbolizes yang qi.

*Editor’s note: This annotation is in error. Practitioners at this stage will naturally come to understand.*

**Slaying the Dragon, Fourth Stage**

When stillness reaches its extreme, movement is born; yin and yang intertwine in embrace.

The “dragon” is a woman’s menstruation. “Slaying the dragon” means using the method to refine and sever menstruation, ensuring it never returns.

If one asks, “Why is menstruation called the dragon?”—from the Tang dynasty onward, all alchemical texts and oral transmissions have used this term, suggesting a deeper significance that need not be elaborated here.

If one asks, “Why must women practitioners first sever menstruation?”—this is the unique transmission of the immortals’ school, the supreme mystery, incomparable to any world religion, philosophy, or theory of physiology and hygiene. The difference between women’s and men’s practice lies precisely here; women’s success is swifter than men’s for this very reason. To depart from this path and seek another is to abandon all hope of attaining immortality. If one fails to refine the immortal body in this life, facing death empty-handed and claiming posthumous enlightenment or liberation is self-deception—such talk must not be believed.

Some may argue: “If menstruation is an obstacle to practice and must be refined away, then elderly women whose menstruation has naturally ceased should be spared this effort and achieve success more easily than the young.” Yet those who are virgins with no prior menstruation, if born with innate wisdom and awakened to the mysterious work, will naturally find success even more readily. Once old age arrives and menstruation dries up, the vital force is depleted—a world of difference from the virgin state. How can they be treated alike? The method requires generating something from nothing, restoring circulation in those whose *tiangui* has already ceased. Only then, returning from having to non-being, can one follow the established path for young women, gradually severing it step by step. This is far more difficult—how could it be easier?

Thus the ancients admonished: “Add oil while it is still early; join life before it is too late.”

When stillness reaches its extreme, movement arises; when movement reaches its extreme, stillness returns. When yang reaches its peak, yin emerges; when yin reaches its peak, yang arises—this is the natural circulation of principle and *qi*, nothing strange in it. *The Daodejing* (Chapter 15) states: *“Who can make the turbid gradually clear through stillness? Who can make the tranquil gradually come to life through long abiding in movement?”* The first line speaks of how, when one attains stillness, the turbid *qi* within the body gradually transforms into pure *qi*; the second line speaks of how, after stillness has endured for long, movement within the body gradually stirs once more. *The Daodejing* (Chapter 16) states: *“Attain the ultimate of emptiness, guard stillness with sincerity; the myriad things arise together, and I observe their return.”* The first two lines speak of stillness at its extreme; the latter two speak of the arising of movement. *“Return”* refers to the hexagram *Fu* (Return). Yin symbolizes stillness, yang symbolizes movement; beneath five yin lines, one yang returns—this too speaks of *“stillness at its extreme gives rise to movement.”* *“Model”* refers to a pattern, that by which things are formed. *“Mutually modeling”* expresses how yin and yang are rooted in each other, mutually completing one another in inseparable interdependence.

“Seizing the jade tiger in the wind, capturing the golden crow in the moon.”
*“Wind”* refers to human breath. As the alchemical classics say: *“The postnatal breath stirs a gentle wind,”* and *“Blowing and exhaling rely on the wind of Xun.”* Both convey this meaning. Daoist texts often pair *“tiger”* with the west (metal) and *“dragon”* with the east (wood). Whenever they speak of lead, metal, or tiger, they refer to the same thing—nothing more than a metaphor for the prenatal yang *qi* that arises when stillness reaches its extreme. *“Moon”* has two meanings: in the context of cultivating *xing* (nature), it refers to the state of not a single thought arising, called *“moon”* for its purity, flawlessness, and solitary radiance; in the context of cultivating *ming* (life), it refers to the moment when prenatal yang *qi* stirs, likened to the phases of the moon—new moon, crescent, full moon—turning without error. *“Golden crow”* is a synonym for the sun; the sun is *li* (fire), fire is mercury, mercury is spirit. When gathering prenatal *qi*, one must rely on postnatal breath as the pivot, hence *“seizing the jade tiger in the wind.”* The word *“jade”* describes its gentle, harmonious quality. As the Perfected One Shi Xinglin said: *“When the wind first rises amid ten thousand whispers, the moon begins to round over a thousand peaks.”* This is precisely the scene. In the Dao of the Elixir, where there is wind, there must be fire; when *qi* moves, spirit responds. Thus Lü Chunyang, the Perfected One, said: *“Lead too is born, mercury too is born; born mercury and born lead are refined together.”* Lead and the moon are metaphors for yang *qi*; mercury and the golden crow are metaphors for yin spirit. When yang *qi* stirs, yin spirit necessarily responds simultaneously, hence *“capturing the golden crow in the moon.”*

Observe closely the moment of *yinyun*; attend carefully to the paths of sequence and reversal.
*The Book of Changes* states: *“Heaven and earth intermingle in *yinyun*, and the myriad things are transformed into harmony.”* *“Yinyun”* describes how the *qi* of heaven descends to earth, and the *qi* of earth ascends to heaven, warmly brewing like the moment before rain, when the sky is overcast for miles, the air thick with the union of spring’s vitality—yet before thunder rolls or rain falls. Once thunder and rain have been released, it is no longer *yinyun*. The moment of *yinyun* in the human body follows the same principle. But what exactly is its manifestation? There are unspeakable subtleties here, not to be written on paper. A discerning woman, if she receives true transmission, may seize the moment to apply effort; otherwise, she risks missing it entirely. Though there remains the possibility of creating the opportunity oneself, it will never match the ingenuity of the natural moment. At this time, if one follows the movement in sequence to practice the human Dao, conception may occur; if one reverses the movement to practice the immortal Dao, the medicine may be gathered and the elixir returned. Yet the meaning of sequence and reversal extends further. Outward expression of vital movement is sequence; inward gathering of vital movement is reversal. Vital *qi* descending and transforming into menstrual blood is sequence; vital *qi* ascending without transforming into blood is reversal. Thus the Daoist texts say: *“Men cultivate to cease leaking essence; women cultivate to cease leaking menses.”*

Where the Magpie Bridge is crossed again, the elixir *qi* returns to the furnace.
*The Mirror of Entering the Medicine* states: *“Cross the upper Magpie Bridge, cross the lower Magpie Bridge; heaven responds with stars, earth responds with tides.”* Later elixir classics all derive their use of *“Magpie Bridge”* from this. In the practice of refining the elixir, one must first turn from the lower Magpie Bridge up the spine, striking through the Jade Pillow to reach the Mud Pill Palace; then turn from the upper Magpie Bridge down the twelve-storied tower of the chest to return to the Ocean of Qi. The *“upper Magpie Bridge”* lies within the Mountain Root between the brows; the *“lower Magpie Bridge”* lies between the coccyx and perineum. When the elixir *qi* reaches the upper Magpie Bridge, one feels a radiant orb flashing between the eyebrows, hence *“heaven responds with stars.”*

When the elixir *qi* ascends from the lower Magpie Bridge, one feels a steaming warmth surging within the Sea of Blood, hence *“earth responds with tides.”* The phrase *“crossing the Magpie Bridge again”* refers to both the upper and lower bridges. *“Returning to the furnace”* means returning to the Yellow Court and no further, for the Yellow Court is also called the Earthen Furnace.

*(Note: The upper and lower Magpie Bridges have other interpretations, which are not discussed here.)*

**Fifth Stage: Nurturing the Elixir**
Bind the tiger and return to the true cavity; lead the dragon to gradually augment the elixir.
The *“tiger”* is *qi*; the *“dragon”* is spirit; the *“true cavity”* lies roughly between the breasts. *“Binding the tiger and returning to the true cavity”* refers to what Master of Supreme Yang, Chen Zhixu, called: *“For women cultivating immortality, one must first accumulate *qi* in the breasts.”* *Qi* is divided into precelestial and postcelestial: refining postcelestial *qi* employs methods of regulating breath and concentrating spirit; gathering precelestial *qi* requires acting when the generative *qi* stirs within the body. *“Leading the dragon”* is nothing more than concentrating spirit to unite with *qi*. When spirit and *qi* merge, the hun and po are restrained, and the elixir coalesces.

Celestial Master Zhang Xujing said:
*“The moment primordial spirit strays, draw it back;
Spirit returns to the body, and *qi* naturally follows.
Thus morning and night without cease,
The sacred embryo of the red child will form.”*
This expresses the meaning of *“leading the dragon to gradually augment the elixir.”* Here, the *“dragon”* differs from the *“dragon”* in *“slaying the dragon.”*

The nature must be as still as water,
The heart as tranquil as a mountain.

True Man Zhang Sanfeng said: *“Concentrate spirit and regulate breath; regulate breath and concentrate spirit—these eight characters must be practiced as one, in layers yet unbroken.”* To *“concentrate spirit”* is to gather the already-cleared heart and draw it inward. When the heart is not yet clear, do not forcefully close the eyes. First, one must self-exhort and self-encourage; only when clarity, coolness, and serenity are attained may one gather it into the *qi* cavity—this is called *“concentrating spirit.”* Then, as if seated atop a high mountain gazing upon all peaks and rivers, or as if a heavenly lamp illuminates the nine depths and nine obscurities—this is what is meant by *“concentrating spirit in emptiness.”*

Regulating breath is not difficult: when the heart-spirit is still, breath follows naturally, and I merely guard its spontaneity. Regulate breath to gather the golden crucible; settle spirit to guard the jade pass.

True Man Zhang Sanfeng said: *“In seated meditation, one must embrace spirit with *qi* and tether intent to breath, revolving gently within the Cinnabar Field, gathering without scattering. Then the internal *qi* and the external *qi* intermingle in the Cinnabar Field; day by day and month by month, it fills the four limbs and flows through the hundred vessels, striking open the twin passes of the spine to ascend to the Mud Pill Palace, then circling back down to the Crimson Palace and returning to the Cinnabar Field. Spirit and *qi* guard each other, breath upon breath interdependent—the path of the river chariot is opened. At this stage, half the effect of laying the foundation is achieved.”* He also said: *“To regulate breath, use postcelestial respiration to seek the true person’s breath. Yet in regulating postcelestial breath, one must let it regulate itself, only then can precelestial breath be aroused. I merely embody emptiness and guard stillness. When true breath stirs, the mysterious pass is not far. Proceeding thus, the foundation can be attained with ease.”*

Guangchengzi said: *“Embrace spirit with stillness, and the form will right itself. Do not weary your form, do not agitate your essence—only then can you attain longevity. Eyes seeing nothing, ears hearing nothing, heart knowing nothing—your spirit will guard your form, and your form will endure. Guard your inner, close your outer; excessive knowledge leads to ruin. I preserve the One to dwell in harmony, thus my body has endured twelve hundred years without decline.”*

*(Note: Zhang Sanfeng’s method of regulating breath is the most detailed, while Guangchengzi’s discourse on settling spirit is the most refined; thus, they are cited as commentary.)*

The preceding line speaks of martial fire, hence "golden crucible"; the following line speaks of civil fire, hence "jade pass." Daily, one may augment by a grain of millet, and white hair returns to youthful complexion.

*The Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* states: “Chaos envelops emptiness, emptiness encompasses the Three Realms. Tracing to its root, a single grain is no larger than a millet.” It further says: “Grain upon grain, from the subtle to the manifest.” This is the meaning of “daily augmenting by a grain of millet.” In essence, it is nothing more than gradual gathering, gradual refinement, gradual coagulation, and gradual congealment—there is no actual millet-like form to be sought.

*The Seal of the Unity of the Three* says: “Golden sand enters the five viscera, dispersing like mist or rain. Steaming and permeating the four limbs, the complexion becomes radiant and fair. White hair all turns black, fallen teeth regrow in their original place. Old age reverts to youthful vigor, the aged crone becomes a maiden. Transforming form to escape worldly calamities, one is called a true person.” This is the meaning of the poem’s final line.

Some say: “White hair on the head with an infant’s face is called ‘crane’s hair returning to youthful complexion.’” This is mistaken. If a cultivator practices primordial work, even white hair must turn black. If the hair remains white and only the face appears rosy, this is merely postnatal effort or the practice of absorption and supplementation—immortals are not like this. The common saying of immortals having “crane’s hair and a child’s face” is the talk of outsiders.

**Sixth Stage of Embryonic Breathing**
To hasten the elixir’s completion, first remove illusory realms.
“Illusory realms” refer to all worldly circumstances that entangle a person, pressing in from all sides, clinging without end, from which one cannot escape even in death. In the next life, the same or worse may await. Thus, the Dao cultivator must sever worldly ties to achieve swift results. Those who study the Dao for decades without progress have all failed to transcend worldly burdens.

*Note: The preceding explanation is correct, but it does not convey the true meaning of “illusory realms.” It was simplified for beginners. In truth, “illusory realms” refer to the various phenomena arising from yin demons within the body, seizing opportunities to manifest—some arousing desire, some inducing terror, some provoking anger, some evoking sorrow, some misleading one into mistaking them for spiritual powers, some luring one onto deviant paths, even causing mental confusion, self-mutilation, or delusional claims of encountering sages. All such phenomena are illusory realms and must be swept away. Without the Dharma eye, they are difficult to discern, which is why students must seek a teacher. Those who study the Dao for decades without encountering demonic obstacles have simply not practiced earnestly.*

With every thought, guard the numinous medicine; with every breath, return to Qian’s origin.
“The numinous medicine” is wondrous existence; wondrous existence is true breath. “With every thought, guard the numinous medicine” means the heart abides in breath. “Qian’s origin” is true emptiness; true emptiness is the heart of Dao. “With every breath, return to Qian’s origin” means breath abides in the heart. Though beginners in cultivation may achieve heart and breath interdependence, it does not last long before they separate again. At the stage of embryonic breathing, heart and breath remain interdependent without end.

“Qian’s origin” refers to the moment before the first line of the Qian hexagram is drawn, not the first line of Qian. *The Illumination of Dao* says: “Observe Qian before its line is drawn—what form does it take? With a single line, the ten thousand images arise.” Thus, is not Qian’s origin the image of the Great Ultimate before yin and yang divide?

Qi returns to the Three Isles; spirit forgets and merges with the Great Void.
“The Three Isles” metaphorically represent the upper, middle, and lower cinnabar fields in the human body. Laozi said: “Returning to the root is called stillness; stillness is called recovering life.” This is the meaning of “qi returns.” The human body originally comes from the Great Void, but upon entering form, it becomes obstructed and cannot merge with the Great Void. Only those who have attained the Dao can forget all forms; once forms are eliminated, they merge with the Great Void.

Tian Yinzhi was a Daoist who said: “Those who cultivate the truth cannot achieve sudden enlightenment and must proceed gradually. First, fasting and purification—cleanse the body and empty the heart. Second, peaceful dwelling—reside in a quiet chamber. Third, visualization—gather the heart and restore nature. Fourth, sitting in forgetfulness—forget the body and the self. Fifth, spiritual liberation—penetrate all dharmas with the spirit.” The entire text is over a thousand words, and I cannot quote it in full; these are its essentials. Sima Ziwei’s *Treatise on Sitting in Forgetfulness* is also worth reading. Such practices are exceedingly difficult and cannot be mastered in a short time, but where there is a will, there is a way—it depends solely on one’s resolve.

Whether coming or going, there is no place that is not true suchness.
“True suchness” is a Buddhist term. Buddhist scriptures say: “The Tathāgatagarbha contains two aspects: the gate of arising and ceasing, and the gate of true suchness.” When the heart is free from arising and ceasing, it is true suchness. To turn away from true suchness is to enter arising and ceasing. It is also said: “True means real and not illusory; suchness means constant and unchanging.”

**Chapter Seven: Firing with the Talisman**

When in the state of continuous fetal breathing, one must discern the mechanisms of movement and stillness. The interplay of yin talismans and yang fire, the dynamics of qi in motion and repose—these have already been addressed in previous stages and need not wait until fetal breathing is perfected. However, before reaching the stage of elixir formation, the movement of qi often surges upward to the nipples (in men, it descends to the reproductive organ). Once the elixir is formed, the two nipples contract tightly like those of a maiden, and though internal movement persists, it can no longer manifest externally, remaining purely internal. Such movement occurs at intervals—sometimes once every few days, sometimes several times a day—depending on the diligence of one’s practice.

Before movement arises and after it subsides, all is still. The yang radiance should advance further, while the yin po-soul must be guarded against flight. Movement pertains to yang; stillness pertains to yin. When yang qi stirs, the primordial spirit moves with it; where qi travels in the body, the spirit follows. The activation of yang qi is called "advance," and by subtly aiding it with the spirit, it grows ever more vigorous—hence "further advance." When yang reaches its peak, yin arises; when movement reaches its extreme, stillness returns.

Man’s hun-soul belongs to yang and tends upward; the po-soul belongs to yin and tends downward. When it is time to ascend, do not force descent; when it is time to descend, do not force ascent. "Guard the yin po-soul against flight" means that if qi assumes a state of tranquil stability, the spirit must assist in maintaining that stillness to prevent restlessness and agitation.

Within the pool, the pearl holds its light;
Atop the mountain, the moon casts its glow.
The "pool" below symbolizes the region of the blood sea and womb; the "mountain" above symbolizes the area between the nipples and the chest center. The pearl’s light is concealed and restrained; the moon’s light is radiant and bright. "Within the pool" and "holds its light" describe the image of deep, hidden stillness; "atop the mountain" and "casts its glow" describe the mechanism of outward, manifest movement.

Do not slacken for even a moment through the six periods;
Nourish the medicinal sprouts to fatten them.
The "six periods" do not refer to the six divisions of day or night but to the six metaphorical phases of internal, silent cultivation. Ancient texts also speak of the "six seasonal nodes," but one must not rigidly adhere to celestial timing, lest a living method become a dead one. What, then, are the six periods of the human body? They are nothing other than the ebb and flow of spirit and qi, the ascent and descent of yin and yang.

"Do not slacken" means that thoughts must not arise, intention must not scatter; maintain a single thread without interruption or discontinuity. Only after completing this phase of practice may one act freely.

**Chapter Eight: Gathering the Medicine**

Half the mystery is realized,
The elixir’s head congeals like dew.
At this stage, half of the immortal’s entire practice is complete, for the internal elixir has formed. "Dew" is water from the earth that, heated, transforms into vapor and disperses into the air; at night, encountering cold, it clings to the most heat-dissipating objects and condenses into dew. The Dao of the elixir follows the same principle—it may be grasped through spiritual insight but cannot be conveyed in words.

Though life may be secured,
How can the form be refined?
Having formed the elixir, the essence, qi, and spirit of the entire body are fully consolidated, ensuring longevity—but one has not yet achieved transcendence. At this stage, one may be called a *human immortal*.

There are five ranks of immortals:
- *Ghost immortals*, who do not depart from the ghostly realm, possessing spiritual communication and enduring existence, unlike ordinary ghosts.
- *Human immortals*, who do not depart from the human realm, eating and dressing as others do yet free from the afflictions of aging, sickness, and death.
- *Earth immortals*, who do not depart from the earthly realm, untouched by cold or heat, hunger or thirst, and though they may not yet project their spirit, they are unburdened by the needs of clothing, food, or shelter.
- *Divine immortals*, who possess supernatural transformations, moving freely, shedding their physical forms, standing alone and unbound—dispersing into qi, coalescing into form.
- *Celestial immortals*, who, building upon the foundation of divine immortality, pursue further practice to transcend the world we inhabit, entering another realm beyond the measure of mortal sentiment.

The nose’s observation connects to pure yang;
Divine lead permeates the body with numinosity.
These two lines describe the concrete practice of transcending the mundane and entering the sacred—without this path, one cannot project the yang spirit. In the present age, aside from a few dedicated adepts, not only does no one practice this method, but even those who comprehend its principles are rare. Were I to venture my own interpretation, I fear it would be met with incomprehension and dismissed as delusion. Thus, I cite the ancient transmitted method of *"Refining the Form in True Emptiness"* to elucidate its profound meaning.

The *Method of Refining the Form in True Emptiness* states:
"Before a person is born, with each inhalation and exhalation, qi connects to the mother; after birth, with each inhalation and exhalation, qi connects to heaven. Heaven and humanity share a single qi, linked and flowing, mutually inhaling and exhaling like the pull of a saw. Heaven bestows it, and I can draw it in—obtaining qi, it flourishes and gives life. Heaven bestows it, then reclaims it—losing qi, it dissipates and brings death. Thus, the sage observes the Dao of heaven, grasps heaven’s movements, and at the time when the sun’s chariot has not yet risen from the Valley of Light, gathers the spirit in tranquil sitting, emptying the self to await it. Within, thoughts are relinquished; without, all entanglements are forsaken. Suddenly, heaven and earth are forgotten, the bodily form is pulverized (Daoist terms like 'pulverizing emptiness' or 'pulverizing the bodily form' merely signify forgetting the physical—one must not take 'pulverize' literally)."

Within the great void of nature, there gleams a single radiant yang—like dew, like lightning—which surges into the mysterious gate, pierces the long valley, and ascends to the mud-pill palace, where it transforms into sweet nectar and descends into the five viscera. I then stir the wind of Xun to meet it, driving out the evils lodged in the three barriers and nine apertures, sweeping clean the filth of the five organs and six bowels, refining the body and forging the essence, burning away dross and dispelling haze, drawing forth the foul and turbid frame to transmute it into a body of pure yang. With long and patient accumulation, the form is transformed, and one becomes immortal.

The *Song of Breaking Delusion* says:
*"Truly, after a hundred days of guarding against peril,
The blood turns to cream, the body shines like silver;
Truly, after a hundred days without loss,
Jade nectar flows, luminous light is born."*

The *Emerald Void* says:
*"Golden light suffuses the body, the marrow exudes fragrance,
Golden sinews and jade bones become wholly yang;
Refining until the red blood flows white,
Yin qi dissolves, and the body is naturally sound."*

Qiu Changchun says:
*"As long as you breathe, ever mindful,
The bodily frame is exchanged, jade liquid flows."*

Zhang Ziyang says:
*"Heaven and humanity share a single qi from the beginning,
Yet the bodily form obstructs its free flow;
Only when refining reaches the point where form and spirit merge in darkness
Does one know that form is none other than true emptiness."*

The methods of refining the form number six in total: the first is refining the form with jade liquid, the second with golden liquid, the third with the great yin, the fourth with the great yang, and the fifth with inner contemplation. Yet all these, though not the great Dao of emptiness, ultimately cannot unite with the great void. Only this one secret—called *refining the form through true emptiness*—though termed "active practice," is in truth non-action; though called "refining the form," is in truth refining the spirit. It is cultivation of the external that encompasses the internal.

Following this method for a hundred days, the seven po dissolve, the three corpses vanish, the six thieves lie hidden, and the ten demons flee afar. Refining for a thousand days, the four great elements of the body become like a crystal pagoda—translucent within and without, radiant inside and out, the flower of the heart blazing, the numinous light manifest.

Thus the *Scripture of Spirit Generation* says:
*"When body and spirit unite as one, it is the true body.
Body and spirit conjoined, form follows the Dao’s flow.
Concealed, the form is stabilized by spirit; revealed, spirit merges with qi.
Thus one treads water and fire unharmed, faces sun and moon without shadow.
Life and death lie in one’s own hands, entry and exit without obstruction,
Whether preserving form to abide in the world or shedding the frame to ascend as an immortal."*

Note: The work of refining the form through true emptiness encompasses a vast scope, not merely glossing this single verse. Even the later methods of refining the spirit, ingesting elixirs, fasting, wall-gazing, and spirit projection all fall within its application, differing only in stages according to the depth of practice.

*"Nurturing must be done with care; when fullness is achieved, one soars."*
"Nurturing" here means to temper and nourish. "Fullness" means qi is sufficient and the medicine potent. "Soaring" refers to the image of the great medicine surging through the passes. If taken to mean ascending into the sky, the time has not yet come.

*Refining the Spirit, Ninth Stage*

The Śarīra of former lives,
One day enters my embrace.
"Śarīra" is a Buddhist term; here it symbolizes the primordial spirit. "Former lives" refers to the time before this body existed. Our primordial spirit remains unchanged through kalpas, while what changes is the spirit of consciousness. Through the practice of refining the form with true emptiness, the spirit of consciousness is gradually refined away, and the primordial spirit gradually emerges. It is like polishing a mirror: once the dust and grime dissolve, radiance appears. Thus we know that all spiritual powers are inherently possessed by our original nature, not acquired from without.

This verse, "One day enters my embrace," seems to refer to the aspect of qi. Yet at this stage, qi and spirit are inseparable—speak of spirit and qi is within it; speak of qi and spirit is within it. Lü Zu’s *Song of Tapping the Trigram* says:
*"From the lead pool bursts forth golden light,
Mercurial fire flows like pearls into the imperial capital."*
"Lead pool" and "golden light" refer to qi;
"Mercurial water" and "flowing pearls" refer to spirit.
"The imperial capital" is the central cinnabar field, also called the Crimson Palace or Spirit Chamber, the domain of the heart. The heart is the sovereign of the body, hence the "imperial capital." The phrase "enters my embrace" carries the same meaning.

Hold it as one holds a fragile vessel,
Tender as one cradles an infant child.
Laozi says: *"To hold and fill it is not as good as stopping in time."* He also says: *"Those who preserve this Way do not wish to be full."* And again: *"Great fullness seems empty, yet its use is inexhaustible."* From this we may understand the intent of the next line in the verse. Laozi says: *"Concentrate qi to attain suppleness—can you be like a newborn babe?"* And: *"I alone am still, without sign, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile."* And: *"In life, humans are soft and weak; in death, stiff and strong."* From this we may grasp the meaning of the following couplet.

The earthly gate must be firmly sealed,
The heavenly pass must first be opened.
All references to "earth" pertain to the lower part of the body;
all references to "heaven" pertain to the upper part.
In cultivation, the greatest taboo is the leakage of essence and qi;
thus all lower orifices must be gathered and tightly sealed.
As the body’s essence and qi gradually accumulate and fill, unable to leak downward, they must surge upward to the brain. At this time, one hears the sound of wind in the ears, sees flashes of light before the eyes, feels tremors at the back of the head, and surges in the navel—many extraordinary phenomena arise.

The seventeenth-generation master of the Dragon Gate lineage, Hong Jiaosui of Guangxi, transmitted a *Song of the Golden Elixir* that has not yet circulated widely. I recall it contains these lines:
*"Ten thousand horses gallop, assaulting both ears,
Meteors and lightning strike the brows.
If you reach this point, do not be alarmed—
Firmly hold the heart and spirit, do not waver."*
This describes the phenomena of sealing the earthly gate and opening the heavenly pass.

Wash and cleanse the yellow sprout,
On the mountain’s peak, earth-shaking thunder.
Lü Zu, in instructing the Immortal Maiden Zhang, wrote in *Lyrics to the Toad Palace*:
*"Earth-shaking thunder on the mountain’s peak,
To wash and cleanse the yellow sprout as it emerges from the soil."*
"Yellow sprout" is another name for the Great Return Elixir. Here, "mountain’s peak" likely refers to the muddy pellet palace atop the head. The third poem in this sequence also says:
*"Mountain’s peak and ocean’s depths,
After the rain, a single thunderclap."*
Judging by the surface, there seems no difference;
in reality, however, the effects are vastly distinct.

The function of "washing and cleansing" is nothing other than stillness and stabilization. In the Dao of the Elixir, after a minor stillness, there must be a minor movement; after a great stillness, there must be a great movement. The deeper the power of stillness and stabilization, the greater the effect of the ensuing tremor. When the magnitude of this tremor reaches its fullness, it can directly burst open the crown gate and exit—yet this is only possible after a great stillness. Now, regarding the power of stillness and stabilization, we can take control ourselves, progressing from brief to prolonged, from shallow to deep. As for the effect of the tremor, however, it follows its natural course and cannot be forced by human effort, seeming beyond our control. Nevertheless, minor stillness invariably leads to minor movement, and great stillness invariably leads to great movement—this reaction never fails. The reason ordinary people lack such effects is their inability to achieve stillness and stabilization. The reason practitioners fail to attain great effects is that, though they know stillness and stabilization, their power in this regard remains insufficient. Those in the Buddhist tradition who study meditation may also achieve stillness for days on end, yet ultimately lack such effects because they merely know how to sit in meditation but not how to refine *qi*.

**Note:** Here, "śarīra" serves as a substitute term for the Internal Elixir, though it does not carry the original Buddhist meaning of relic. Whether *śarīra* and the Golden Elixir are the same or different, and what distinctions exist between the outcomes of Buddhist and Daoist cultivation, are questions of great urgency—yet none of the scriptures from any school have addressed them. While the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra* speaks of the "ten kinds of immortals," this is merely the Buddhist perspective. Outside of Buddhist scriptures, no Chinese texts—ancient or modern—mention these ten kinds of immortals, so they cannot be taken as definitive. The Chinese disposition has always favored harmony: not only do Confucianism and Daoism share a common origin without conflict, but even toward the foreign religion of Buddhism, there is no discrimination. Instead, mutual integration prevails, a tolerance far broader than the exclusivist doctrines of other faiths. The Venerable Qinghua’s discussion of *śarīra* is particularly impartial. He states: Buddhism takes seeing the nature as its essence and does not value essence and *qi*. All things are born and perish, but nature is neither born nor perishes. After nirvāṇa, the original nature becomes perfectly luminous, transcending the three realms and forever escaping the cycle of rebirth. After cremation, the remaining essence and *qi* condense into *śarīra*, like a pearl emerging from an oyster—separate from spiritual nature. That it can radiate brilliant light is due to the concentration of essence and *qi* within it. The three treasures of the human body—essence, *qi*, and spirit—are originally inseparable, yet Buddhism uniquely seeks to illuminate the mind and see the nature, refining wisdom by extracting the light of spirit alone, leaving essence and *qi* to coalesce into form, discarded and unattended. Nevertheless, because all leaks have been sealed and the power of meditative concentration is profound, the essence and *qi* within the body are no longer ordinary. Thus, *śarīra* can transform, appear and disappear, and display varying hues of light.

From this, we may infer: what Buddhism calls the unborn and undying is spirit, that is, nature. *Śarīra* is essence and *qi*, that is, life. After a Buddha’s passing, the spirit has already transcended form, yet essence and *qi* remain bound within the world. Daoism, by contrast, cultivates both nature and life, merging essence, *qi*, and spirit into one. Through the fire phases of the celestial cycle, it refines a body beyond the body—spirit resides within it, essence resides within it, *qi* resides within it, and they cannot be separated. Thus, after ascension, whether the flesh transforms into *qi* or the corpse releases the spirit, no *śarīra* remains. If, by chance, *śarīra* is left behind after seated transformation, the practitioner’s daily work must have leaned toward Buddhism, emphasizing nature over life.

Those who cultivate both nature and life fuse the body’s essence, *qi*, and spirit into a luminous, vibrant whole. Bones and flesh dissolve, pores merge, blood becomes silvery nectar, and the body flows like fire, coursing through the four limbs and hundred joints, illuminating the realm of purity and emptiness. Thus, they can ascend or descend unpredictably, appear and vanish without trace. The difference between Buddhism and Daoism is this: Buddhism emphasizes refining nature, with solitary spirit shining beyond root and dust—this is called "nature’s longevity." Daoism emphasizes refining *qi*, with the entire body becoming purely yang and golden light radiating—this is called "*qi*’s longevity." Ultimately, at the supreme source, nature is *qi*, and *qi* is nature. What is the same is their reality; what differs is merely their names.

**Ingestion Tenth**
The Great Foundry shapes mountains and marshes,
Within them lies the sentiment of creation.
"Great Foundry" originally meant forging the five metals; here, it metaphorically describes the mighty work of creation. Heaven and earth serve as furnace and crucible, yin and yang as water and fire. All phenomena are forged from this, for all things share a single Supreme Ultimate. Mountains and marshes are but one thing among the myriad, yet within them lies creation—each thing possesses its own Supreme Ultimate. Mountains and marshes communicate *qi*, with *zhèn* and *duì* interacting, and the sentiment of creation becomes manifest.

For those who cultivate immortality, the key lies in gathering the pure, numinous *qi* of empty space within the body. Then, one’s spirit is combined with this *qi* and refined and nurtured. Over time, spirit and *qi* merge into one, and the Great Elixir is formed. The latter stages of practice are best conducted in the mountains, for the pure, numinous *qi* there is superior to that of cities. Yet even in the mountains, one must carefully select the terrain—whether building a thatched hut or dwelling in a cave—choosing a place sheltered from wind and gathering *qi*, with the mountain’s vein flowing behind, protective barriers on either side, a node at the center, and an open vista in front. This is the land where the generative *qi* of heaven and earth accumulates.

As the sun and moon rise and set, the wheel of creation turns. The Daoist who sits in meditation amidst this ceaseless motion draws in this boundless, pure, numinous *qi* to nurture the primordial spirit—how could one not undergo a transformation of bones and rebirth of the body? At dawn, one welcomes the *qi* of the solar crow; at night, one absorbs the essence of the lunar toad. Just as the clam receives the moon’s radiance to form its pearl, and the earth receives the sun’s essence to yield gold and jade, so too may a person gather the essences of sun and moon to congeal the immortal elixir and transmute the mortal frame.

As for the method of gathering these essences, at this stage, one naturally comprehends—there is no need to cling to outward forms, lest it hinder perfect adaptability. The "Method of Gathering Solar Essence and Lunar Radiance" described in the *Yijin Jing* pertains to the highest level of martial cultivation, not the profound mysteries of the immortal arts.

When the elixir is ripe for gathering, the years weigh lightly upon the body. To gather the numinous *qi* of heaven and earth and congeal the elixir, one must recognize the waxing and waning of yin and yang. To seize the profound mechanism of creation and transform the body requires the effort of three years and nine cycles.

Where the primordial spirit travels, the myriad pores radiate light. This speaks of the luminosity that manifests from every pore in the body. As the alchemical classics say: *"One morning, the work is complete, unknown to others—on all sides, palaces of night-shining jade appear."* This conveys the same meaning. The reason for this luminosity may lie in the body’s abundant electrical vitality. In the world, thunder and lightning naturally emit light, enduring through long ages without the slightest diminution of their essence. If even such mindless substances possess such marvelous potency, why doubt the immortal body?

**Chapter Eleven: Abstaining from Grains**
Having feasted on numinous *qi*, the lungs and viscera grow wondrously cool and pure—this is the true practice of cutting off cooked food. The ability to do so arises because the body is filled with numinous *qi*, naturally eliminating the desire for food, not merely enduring hunger on an empty stomach.

*"Forgetting the spirit without attachment, merging with the Ultimate to part from emptiness."*
"Forgetting the spirit" means that though wisdom is present, it is not employed—flaunting cleverness easily gives rise to demonic obstacles. "Without attachment" refers to having no form or appearance to cling to. "Merging with the Ultimate" means uniting with the Great Ultimate, where spirit and *qi* merge as one, and yin and yang intertwine. Thus, one does not fall into inert emptiness, hence "parting from emptiness"—meeting emptiness, one distances oneself from it. The third line speaks of non-attachment to form, the fourth of non-attachment to emptiness; with both form and emptiness forgotten, one abides in perfect samadhi.

At dawn, one seeks mountain taro; at dusk, one gathers marsh orchids. Taro is an ordinary food, known to all.

"Zhi" resembles a mushroom, with a cap above and a stalk below, its texture firm and smooth. *Bencao* records six varieties—green, red, yellow, white, black, and purple—all of which, when consumed, lighten the body and prolong life. Yet the divine scriptures list dozens or even hundreds of *lingzhi* names, though these are not obtainable by ordinary people.

If one mingles with the smoke of the kitchen fire, the body will never tread the Jasper Pool. The immortal’s form prizes purity and ethereality; if one does not abstain from cooked food, the turbid *qi* of the mundane will mingle within, and how can transcendence be hoped for? The "Jasper Pool" is the abode of female immortals. *Collected Biographies of Immortals* states: "The palace of the Queen Mother of the West is bordered on the left by the Jasper Pool and encircled on the right by the Emerald Waters."

**Facing the Wall, Twelfth**
All affairs are said to end—
still, one sits in the small niche.
The practice of "facing the wall" began with Bodhidharma. During the reign of Emperor Wu of Liang, Bodhidharma dwelled at Shaolin Temple on Mount Song, sitting motionless facing the wall for nine years as if a single day. Thus, later Daoist practitioners of stillness cultivation all spoke of "facing the wall." Today’s Buddhists, however, no longer uphold this practice, merely reciting the name of Amitābha.

Once the barrier of grain avoidance is passed, not only can cooked food be forsaken, but even *zhi* and taro may be left uneaten. When ancient immortals reached this stage of cultivation, most chose to dwell in mountain caves, having others seal the entrance with massive stones to ward off wild beasts and the disturbances of human affairs, with no need for guardians. Yet this method may not suit the present age. A common approach is to build a few thatched huts in a quiet mountain forest for fellow cultivators to reside in. Then, one constructs a small niche from wood, just large enough for a single person to sit, with a soft, thick cushion. A door is set at the front, while the other three sides allow airflow without drafts, best covered with bamboo-slat curtains like those used on sedan chairs. Seated within, one disregards the passage of days and months until the yang spirit departs the body, marking the completion of the work. However, someone must keep watch day and night to guard against unforeseen dangers. If one does not wish to sit for long, temporary emergence is permissible.

At this stage, the body’s *qi* is full, eliminating the desire for food; the spirit is whole, dispelling the need for sleep. Outwardly, the breath ceases, the pulse stills, the body remains warm, and the eyes emit a radiant light. The inner workings of the body differ entirely from those of ordinary people and cannot be judged by conventional physiology. Such phenomena are not uncommon even today, for I have personally witnessed them. Yet none could discern what spiritual powers they possessed—perhaps this is what the alchemical classics mean by "wisdom present but unused"?

**Note:** From the third line of this verse to the end of the fourteenth, all describes inconceivable states, hence no annotations are provided. A certain lady once suspected I was deliberately withholding secrets and wrote to question me. Yet in my thirty years of seeking the Dao, I have never beheld the form of the yang spirit or how it departs. Even my teacher’s transmission did not touch upon this, merely stating, "When the time comes, you will know." Thus, regarding the various effects after the spirit’s departure, lacking direct experience, I dare not speak idly. If a practitioner truly masters the work of facing the wall, what fear is there of not knowing the matter of the spirit’s departure? I ask that you remain patient and await personal verification in the years to come.

**Departing the Spirit, Thirteenth**
Beyond the body, another body—
not formed by illusion.
**Note:** To leave this verse entirely unannotated would leave readers unsatisfied, yet to comment on each line would be difficult to put into words. Thus, I excerpt several passages from the records of past masters to illustrate the scene of the spirit’s departure and the further work thereafter. For full details, one must broadly study the alchemical classics and engage in true inquiry and realization—this text cannot encompass it all.

*Record of Elder Qinghua* states: "The omens before the yang spirit’s birth include light radiating outward from the navel wheel and fragrance issuing from the nose and mouth. After its departure, golden light shines in all directions, the pores gleam like crystal, like the sun rising over the sea or a pearl emerging from the deep. Fragrant mist fills the room, and with a thunderous crack, gold and fire intermingle as the yang spirit exits through the *niwan* [mud-pill palace]. After departure, all depends on one’s prior work. If the yang spirit is formed purely from primordial spiritual *qi*, it remains unstained by circumstances, unmoved by phenomena, gathered or released at will, coming and going freely. Upon re-entering the *niwan*, the body becomes fiery hot, golden light once more emanates from the pores, and fragrance again suffuses the air. In an instant, it returns to the Yellow Court, as if absent yet present, unknowing yet aware—this is the true state. If the mind has not attained emptiness and clarity, the embryo formed will not be the sacred embryo, and the spirit that emerges will carry impurities. At the sight of the fearful, terror arises; at the sight of the desirable, attachment arises. It may linger and forget to return, falling into demonic paths. Though the body appears to die, those unaware may think it an immortal’s transformation, yet if the yang spirit departs and does not return, the outcome is dire indeed."

Someone asked: "If the mind is not yet pure but the spirit has already departed, what is to be done?"
The master replied: "If unavoidable, there remains the practice of refining emptiness."

Though the embryo spirit has emerged, it must be tightly reined in to complete the work of refining emptiness before releasing it again. Then, within the true light of the Dharma-realm, it may roam freely at will, vast and transformative. Refining emptiness requires a vast and boundless heart—no self, no other, no heaven, no earth. In the clarity of empty qi, within the primal chaos, one discerns neither "I" nor "not-I," neither emptiness nor non-emptiness. Creation turns and revolves, undivided when divided, unmerged when merged—this is called refining emptiness. For the emptiness of the yang spirit merges with the Great Emptiness, fusing seamlessly, such that form and spirit are both wondrous, uniting with the Dao in truth. This is the work after the spirit’s emergence, the task before the division of the body.

Someone asked: "What is the difference between yang spirit and yin spirit?"
The master replied: "If the yin has not been fully refined and the spirit emerges too soon, it is called yin spirit. When it departs, one may see a river of white light in the eyes, and the spirit exits through them; or hear the sounds of bells, chimes, flutes, and pipes in the ears, and the spirit exits through them. Because the yang qi is not yet robust, it cannot break through the Celestial Pass and thus takes a side path of convenience. After emerging, it too roams freely and joyfully, traversing streets and alleys, approaching waters and ascending mountains. It can assume form but not divide it; it can wander among humans but cannot soar or transform. Yet when the scorching summer sun hangs overhead, the yin spirit fears and avoids it. Thus, though it bears the wind of immortality, it has not escaped the realm of ghosts."

Someone asked: "Can the yin spirit be refined into a yang spirit?"
The master replied: "It can. Those who study the Dao and refuse to settle for the lesser vehicle must, after the yin spirit has emerged, undertake further refinement. They shatter the original form of the yin spirit, pour it into the golden tripod and jade furnace, and rekindle the fire. When the fire and timing are sufficient, the yin will be exhausted and the yang purified, and the true person will manifest."

Someone asked: "How can the original form of the yin spirit be shattered?"
The master replied: "Forget the body, empty the mind. In the vast emptiness, let no thing arise. Then one may exchange the mortal embryo for the sacred embryo, transform the mundane self into the true person, and the task is complete."

Someone asked: "After attaining a body beyond the body, what further work remains?"
The master replied: "An excellent question! There are two paths in this Dao: the lesser adept surrenders the body and departs swiftly; the greater adept merges with the body and departs slowly."

When the yang spirit pierces through the crown, it roams freely and joyfully in the Great Void, soaring tens of thousands of miles in an instant, treading upon clouds and mist, gazing down upon mountains and seas, transforming endlessly at will. Looking back at the illusory body, it is but a clod of dung and earth—better to cast it aside. Thus, one sheds the bones in the wilderness, leaves the form behind, and departs far away: this is the conduct of those who surrender the body and depart swiftly.

If a person of resolve seeks no hasty results and willingly undertakes the slow and patient work, the yang spirit may emerge yet remain unemerged, the illusory body may be abandoned yet remain unabandoned. Preserving the primordial numen, refining it through a thousand fires and ten thousand smeltings, one forgets the spirit as if it were the Great Void, yet with pure fire tempers it, transforming together with it. Flesh, bones, and form all turn to fine dust: this is the conduct of those who merge with the body and depart slowly.

Both paths are set forth here for one to choose. Should those of aspiration not take the higher way as their model?

From *The Recorded Sayings of Master Chongxu*, someone asked: "The emergence of the yang spirit need not necessarily entail a body beyond the body, as you have already made clear. But if there is truly no visible form, how can it be called the emergence of the spirit?"

The master replied: "The luminous nature of the original essence is neither existent nor nonexistent, neither non-nonexistent nor existent. How can its concealment and manifestation, its forms and appearances, be confined to one mode? Of old, the Perfected Liu Haichan emerged as a white vapor; the Patriarch Wang of Xishan emerged as a flowering tree..."

The Perfected Ma Danyang emerged with thunderous reverberations; the Primordial Lady Sun Bu’er emerged amid fragrant winds and auspicious qi. Though these several cases presented visible forms, they were not the human body. Moreover, Master Lan Yangsu of Nanyue departed with a clap of the hands and a great laugh; the Perfected Qiu Changchun himself said: when the spirit emerged, three times he struck through the Heavenly Gate, gazing straight down upon the forest of myriad phenomena, seeing mountains, rivers, and the great earth as if on the palm of his hand. These two cases showed no visible form, yet neither were they the body—why then cling to the notion that there must be a body beyond the body for the spirit to emerge?”

Someone asked: “Why do these differences arise?”

The master replied: “At the moment one emerges from samādhi, if by chance a thought stirs and aligns with the mechanism of emergence, there is none that does not manifest in accord with that thought. Thus, if the thought is not directed toward transforming the body, there need not be any visible form; if the thought is directed toward transforming the body, there need not be an absence of visible form. My words here are spoken only for those who have attained the Dao and become immortals within the lineages of our ancestral perfected ones—Zhong, Lü, Wang, Qiu, Li, and Cao. This is household talk for household members, not meant for the ears of heterodox practitioners or vulgar youths. Even if they hear it, it will be of no use to them. Those of later generations who receive the Dao within the lineage of our ancestral founder, the Perfected Qiu Changchun, must take heed and remember, lest they be startled or doubtful when the moment arrives.”

The aforementioned perfected ones are, respectively: Zhongli Quan (Zhengyang), Lü Dongbin (Chunyang), Wang Zhe (Chongyang), Qiu Chuji (Changchun), Li Niuwan, and Cao Xuanyang (Changhua). Perfected Cao represents the seventh generation of the Dragon Gate lineage, transmitting to the eighth generation, Wu Chongxu (Shouyang); Perfected Wu, in turn, transmitted to the ninth generation, Liu Huayang (Taichang). These words refer precisely to the lineage of the Wu-Liu Immortal School.

**Ascension (Fourteenth)**
The auspicious hour arrives, and one emerges from the valley,
In an instant, ascending to the divine Empyrean.
“Ascension” is what the vulgar call “ascending to heaven in broad daylight.” The *Cantong Qi* states: “Diligently practice it, day and night without rest; after three years of refining and ingesting, one becomes light and roams afar. Crossing fire, one is not scorched; entering water, one is not drenched. One can appear or vanish at will, abiding in eternal joy without care. When merit is complete, one ascends on high, receiving the registers and charts.”

Such accounts have existed since antiquity, yet in the present age, they remain unseen and unheard, and in theory, lack evidence. If one relies on the biographies of immortals through the ages, the tales come as readily as counting household treasures, and listeners may delight in them without tiring. Yet others may suspect them of being fabricated facts promoting superstition. Only when an ordinarily skeptical person—one who does not believe in the Dao of immortals—provides corroboration, whether in speech or writing, can doubt be dispelled.

Consider, for instance, the poem *Xie Ziran* by Master Han Tuizhi of the Tang:
In Guozhou’s Nanchong County, there lived a girl, Xie Ziran;
Naïve and ignorant in youth, she had only heard of immortals.
Light of life, she learned their arts, dwelling on Jinquan Mountain;
She spurned worldly splendor and renounced her parents’ tender love.
One day, seated in an empty chamber, clouds and mist arose within;
As if hearing the strains of sheng and yu, descending from the unseen heavens.
Eaves and pillars flickered in radiance, hues of five colors linked in succession;
Onlookers stood aghast, hesitating, none daring to draw near.
In an instant, she ascended lightly, drifting like smoke in the wind;
Vast and boundless the eight directions, no trace or echo to be found.
The village head reported the matter; the prefect marveled and sighed.
He drove his carriage, leading officials, while the common folk vied to lead the way.
Entering the chamber, they saw nothing—only robes and shoes, shed like a cicada’s molt.
All declared it the work of immortals, a tale so luminous it must be passed down.

The prefecture of Guozhou lies in present-day Shunqing Prefecture of Sichuan. This poem spans three hundred and thirty characters in total, with the first half narrating events and the latter half offering commentary. Nearly every derogatory term is heaped upon her—cold maiden, dull-witted child, mountain demon, phantom, gloom, desolate wind, unholy trickster, specter, netherworld, ghost, inanimate stone, monstrous transformation, fox spirit, malevolent curse, forsaken soul, deep grievance, uncanny marvel, lament—sparing no effort in slander. Clearly, Master Han harbored no belief in the existence of immortals. Yet his concluding argument amounts to no more than this: *"Human life follows constant principles; men and women each have their proper roles. Cold clothing and hunger are met by weaving and tilling. Below, this sustains descendants; above, it serves ruler and kin. To stray from this Dao is to abandon one’s true self."*

Alas! Such a perspective differs little from a frog at the bottom of a well or a louse in a monk’s robe—how could it satisfy our aspirations? The value of immortals lies in their achievements, surpassing the mundane a myriadfold, transcending all worldly suffering, and breaking every fetter of mortal existence—not merely in the name "immortal." Call them "immortals" if you will, or "demons and specters" if you prefer; what matters is the truth of the matter. The ascension of Xie Ziran was witnessed by all in her time; even the obstinate Master Han could not deny it. Yet, clinging to Confucian orthodoxy, rejecting Buddhism and Daoism, his stern demeanor crumbled—how could he bear to recant? Thus, he cloaked these divine signs in foul names, which is hardly surprising.

Reading the *Yongcheng Record of Assembled Immortals*, which recounts the wondrous life of the Perfected Woman Xie Ziran in meticulous detail, one hesitates to accept it as truth. Yet upon reading the lines of this poem—*"In an instant, she ascended lightly, drifting like smoke in the wind; entering the chamber, they saw nothing—only robes and shoes, shed like a cicada’s molt"*—one realizes the accounts of ascension are no deception. O later scholars, can you not take heed?

*Song of the Numinous Source Dao*
By Cao Wenyi of the Song Dynasty

I shall speak to you now of the truth:
The stem of life has ever dwelt in the true breath.
Illumine the body—longevity abides in emptiness that is not empty;
The numinous mirror embraces the heavens, cradling the ten thousand things.

The Supreme Polarity unfolds its wonders, and man attains the One.
Having attained the One, guard it well and never lose it.
Let the palace be empty and still, that spirit may dwell therein;
The numinous house, scorched and parched, dries up the blood and fluids.

A single sorrow, a single joy, a single thought or care,
A single indulgence, a single toil—each wears the body thin.
Morning’s harm, evening’s loss, unheeded and unknown,
Scatter and disorder the spirit, leaving it without support.

Gradually worn away, slowly declining,
The primordial qi is exhausted, and spirit departs.
They say sitting in meditation is also meditation—
Such may befit the sage, but not the common man.

Tender and fragile as a budding sprout, it must be nurtured;
Rooted in deluded perception, it shifts and changes.
Dawdling, one fails to clear the brambles—
When has fine grain ever sprung from a barren field?

After nine years, the work is complete, the fire and timing perfected.
Responding to things without intent, the spirit transforms swiftly.
Intentless intent—this is the true heart;
Forgetting both movement and stillness, one transcends desire.

Spirit is nature, qi is life.
When spirit does not stray outward, qi naturally settles.
These two, originally one—who is closer?
Lose them, and what remains to grasp as the root?

Merge them into one, then forget even that oneness—
You may then emerge and submerge with the primordial transformation.
Piercing metal and stone is no longer difficult;
Sitting in liberation or standing in death—all is swift and fleeting.

This Way is easy to know but hard to practice.
Forget the practice, and the Way is complete.
Do not mistake breath-holding for the true task—
Counting breaths or following diagrams is not the way.

Of late, lay down the toils of the external world;
Yet within, the heart remains entangled—what difference is there?
Observe the infant in the womb—
Does it scheme or calculate in secret?

Concentrate the qi, attain softness, and spirit lingers long.
The true breath comes and goes, naturally at ease.
Gently, unbroken, it returns to the primordial life—
Without drawing from the numinous spring, it flows of itself.

Thirty-six thousand days make the great work,
Within which lie the rhythms of yin and yang.
Steaming and fusing, the meridians and sinews transform;
Everywhere, radiance shines—nothing is obstructed.

The Three Corpses depart the yin abode of the corpse;
The myriad lands come to pay homage at the Red Emperor’s palace.
I ask you: whence comes the Perfected One?
From the beginning, he has dwelt only in the numinous terrace.

Once, clouds and mist deeply obscured him;
Today, we meet, and the eye of the Way opens.
This is not the work of a day or a night—
It is my original truth, not some mere technique.

Firm as metal and stone in the biting cold,
Repelling the demons of yin with the power of wisdom—
All arises from emptiness and tranquility, refined and focused.
This is the land of purity in the dream of Hua Xu.

What foundation was first laid?
At the place of non-doing, all is done.
Banish the images that arise in thought;
In dreams, the spirit toils to hold fast.

Neither movement nor stillness—this is the great essential;
Neither square nor round—this is the ultimate Way.
When the primordial harmony circulates within, truth is realized;
Seeking it in the breath without is never complete.

If the primordial qi does not abide, the spirit cannot rest—
A worm-eaten tree, rootless, its branches and leaves wither.
No need to speak of tears, saliva, essence, or blood—
Trace them to their source, and all are one.

This thing has no fixed place;
It shifts and changes with the heart’s intent.
In the body, when heat is felt, it becomes sweat;
In the eyes, when sorrow is felt, it becomes tears.

In the kidneys, when desire arises, it becomes essence;
In the nose, when wind is felt, it becomes mucus.
Flowing and circulating, it moistens the entire body—
In the end, it does not depart from the water of spirit.

The water of spirit is hard to describe; few recognize it.
It sustains all life through the true qi.
Know only tranquility, free from thought and care;
Fast, quiet the heart, and moderate your speech.

A single taste of ghee and ambrosial nectar—
Hunger and thirst vanish, revealing true simplicity.
When the work is complete in time, you shall be free and at ease.
At first, the refining and forging are truly arduous.

Yet within this arduousness, there is no arduousness—
Leisurely, one need only nurture the primordial spirit.
Alas, the heart will not allow leisure—
At this point, even restraint depends entirely on the self.

I once suffered bitterly, more bitter still—
Eating bark, wearing grass, solitary and still.
Though I knew the great Way, I could not practice it—
Fame and reputation were the great afflictions of my body.

How does it compare, practicing in leisure,
To sitting in great stillness, utterly at peace?
Though form and spirit are said to be two, hard to unite,
One must first complete life before completing nature.

Do not chase fame or pursue profit;
Cut off human attachments, and all affairs cease.
The resolve lies with you—why linger?
Who else can control or restrain me?

What is the worth of a reputation that shakes the heavens?
Or writing essays as swiftly as a horse gallops—are these truly noble?
Wealth, fine clothes, food—all are of no concern;
Piling up jade and gold—what use is it?

Craftsmanship, literature, poetry, and prose—
All hinder the path of cultivation.
Like thin mist or light smoke,
Drifting idly beside falling flowers, following willow fluff—

Floating, leisurely, between heaven and earth,
Yet in the end, unable to become rain or dew.
Which is closer—fame or the self?
Half a lifetime’s years, spent in great procrastination.

Of late, I rely on spirit and qi for my practice,
Yet if spirit and qi are not at peace, the toil is in vain.
Pity this fine foundation—
A golden hall, a jade chamber, without a master.

If only the master could dwell there long,
Placed in emptiness, in idleness—
The wondrous existence within nothingness is hard to grasp;
To nurture the infant, one must rely on the mother.

Seal away clever speech, set aside brilliance,
Gather in the spirit, and feign foolishness.
With a steadfast heart and single intent, follow the path ahead—
The great Way will never betray a person.

*Plain Explanation of the "Song of the Numinous Source Dao"*
By Chen Yingning

**Reader’s Note:**
1. Though the *Song of the Numinous Source Dao* was written by a female Perfected, it is not exclusively a manual on female alchemical formulas. All who cultivate the Way—regardless of gender, age, or status—may practice this method with great efficacy, free from harm. It is among the most stable and universally accessible paths in the pursuit of immortality. Previously, scholars paid little heed to this work, leaving it buried for many years—a great pity. I have long wished to provide a plain explanation and publish it for circulation, but the opportunity never arose. Now, at the request of the Academy of Immortal Studies for teaching materials, the annotation is finally complete. With the Alchemical Scripture Publishing Society’s commitment to dissemination, publication has at last become possible.

Thus we may know that the success or failure of all worldly affairs hinges upon their proper timing and karmic conditions—nothing occurs by mere chance.

Second, the excellence of this text lies in its complete absence of cryptic language. From beginning to end, it expounds the truth with utter clarity, unlike other alchemical scriptures that fill their pages with terms like *Dragon and Tiger*, *Lead and Mercury*, *Heavenly Stems and Earthly Branches*, *River Chart and Luo Scripture*, *Five Phases and Eight Trigrams*, leaving practitioners thoroughly bewildered. Though this commentary offers no particular distinction, it employs plain speech over literary language, adhering closely to the original text’s meaning while striving for simplicity. It is written so that even those with only basic literacy may grasp it at once, and further, may relay its teachings to those devoted to the Way yet illiterate. In this manner, the vow of universal salvation may gradually be fulfilled.

Third, some question why this text omits the female practice of *Severing the Red Dragon* and the male practice of *Refining Essence into Qi*, fearing that its teachings on life-cultivation (*minggong*) remain incomplete. Yet one must understand that the methods for women to halt menstruation and for men to seal the essence-gate each encompass both rapid and gradual approaches, as well as forced and natural techniques. The methods described in other texts are forced and rapid; those in this text are natural and gradual. When executed well, forced and rapid methods yield swift results, but if mishandled, they give rise to many afflictions and may even lead one astray. Natural and gradual methods, when practiced well, produce the same effects. If practiced poorly, they at most yield no results—never will they cause harm. By comparison, such methods are the most stable and free from pitfalls. Thus, the Perfected Master Cao transmitted this song to later generations not out of ignorance of *Severing the Dragon* or *Refining Essence*, nor from a desire to withhold secrets.

Fourth, one may ask: “Two-thirds of this text expounds profound principles, while one-third urges the severance of worldly attachments. Where, then, are the actual alchemical formulas?” The answer is this: For those who cultivate the Way, the most difficult tasks are comprehending profound principles and breaking free from worldly entanglements. Should one achieve these two, even if one has not yet devoted oneself to dedicated practice, one has already attained the necessary qualifications. When the day comes to earnestly apply the method, results will manifest with ease. Otherwise, even if you recite the formulas by heart, they will be of no use. If you insist on knowing where the formulas are concealed, I shall point them out to you. Within this text, four lines contain the most essential formulas:

1. *“When the spirit ceases its outward scattering, the qi naturally stabilizes.”*
2. *“Concentrate the qi to attain suppleness; the spirit will long abide.”*
3. *“Merge into oneness, then forget even that oneness.”*
4. *“When primordial harmony circulates within, one attains the true.”*

Once practice reaches this stage, the great work is complete—no further formulas need be sought.

Fifth, this text is not without its shortcomings, yet these are common to nearly all Daoist scriptures, not unique to this one. Surveying the Daoist texts of past and present, one finds they generally address three themes: extravagant descriptions of the wondrous, concealment of formulas, and exhortations to practice. Yet none attend to the practitioner’s living conditions—what dietary, residential, or daily-life conditions must be met to properly engage in cultivation. Nor do they consider whether failing to meet such conditions might hinder progress. This is because the social circumstances of ancient China differed vastly from those of today. The challenges faced by modern practitioners may have been unimaginable to the ancients. Those who seek to cultivate the Way in this age must carefully assess whether their environment and social conditions are conducive, lest they mistakenly attribute their lack of progress to the inefficacy of the method.

Sixth, this text propagates the great Dao, reveals the numinous source, directly points to *xing* and *ming*, and focuses solely on spirit and qi—hence its avoidance of terms like *Lead* and *Mercury*. Master Wang Dongting once remarked: “This song contains not a single word about Lead; all it speaks of is the true Mercury.” In my humble view, while the *spiritual water* mentioned herein may be likened to the true Mercury, the text also states: *“Spiritual water is hard to describe; few recognize it. All life arises from the true qi.”* This *true qi* refers to Lead, not Mercury. Moreover, cultivating the Way differs from refining elixirs. Were we to reverse the terms and speak of *cultivating elixirs* or *refining the Way*, others would surely mock our lack of linguistic precision, thereby clarifying the distinction between the two. For those who cultivate the Way, the return of postnatal spirit and qi to the prenatal *xing* and *ming* signifies the completion of merit—no further discussion of Lead or Mercury is necessary. Only in the *Three Primes* elixir method must one emphasize Lead and Mercury. Many Daoist texts conflate the cultivation of the Way with the refinement of elixirs, offering sweeping critiques that mislead later learners. Furthermore, Wang also observed: “All writings by female Perfected Ones speak of Mercury but not Lead, of Water but not Fire.”

For female Perfected Ones, their bodies belong to the *kun* phase, thus they cannot speak of yang Fire but only of yin tally. However, my humble observation notes that Sun Bu’er, the Perfected Sovereign, in her *Verses on the Stages of Female Elixir Practice*, includes the line *“Divine Lead permeates the body with numinosity,”* explicitly mentioning Lead. Moreover, the seventh poem in her collection is titled *“Tally and Fire,”* clearly referring to both yin tally and yang Fire. Thus, Wang’s assertion cannot be taken as an absolute principle.

Seven: In ancient times, those who studied the Way necessarily received oral transmission from a master, which is why Daoist texts of various schools contain no introductory procedures for beginners. Modern learners often regard this as a憾事. Some years ago, I encountered *The Five Great Health Cultivation Methods* by Hong Tai’an of Fuzhou, a work of clear and systematic instruction that may serve as a reference for beginners. Written on the Mid-Autumn Festival of the twenty-seventh year of the Republic of China (1938), by Chen Yingning of Wanjiang, at the Shanghai Academy of Immortal Studies.

During the Xuanhe reign of Emperor Huizong of the Song dynasty, there was a Lady Cao who enjoyed considerable renown as a talented woman of her time. Emperor Huizong, by nature fond of the Dao and partial to those skilled in poetry and prose, held her in high regard, for her mastery of Daoist learning ranked among the finest, and her poetry and essays were indeed of no mean quality. He summoned her to reside in the capital (at the time, the Song capital was Bianjing, present-day Kaifeng County in Henan Province) and accorded her special favor. She was later bestowed the title *Wenyi Zhenren* ("Cultivated Recluse Perfected One").

*(Yingning’s note: The term* Zhenren *was a posthumous honorific used by later Daoist practitioners, not an imperial bestowal.)*

*(Yingning’s note: Cao Wenyi was a distant descendant of Cao Liyong, the prime minister during the reign of Emperor Zhenzong of the Song. At the age of twenty-one, she left home to become a Daoist nun and wandered across the land. In her later years, she was summoned to reside in Bianjing and was granted the title* Wenyi Dashi *"Cultivated Recluse Great Master." After her ascension, the court further bestowed upon her the posthumous title* Xiyuan Guanmiao Xiansheng *"Mysterious Master of the Abbey of Rare Origin." For details, see the second volume of* Bianjing Gouyi Ji *(*Records of Strange Happenings in Bianjing*), compiled by Song Lian of the Ming dynasty. When I first wrote these annotations, I had not yet come across this record, so I regrettably omitted the full account of her life.)*

This *Song of the Great Dao of the Numinous Source* was composed by the Perfected Cao Wenyi during that period for the edification of Dao seekers. Having circulated to the present day, it has endured for approximately eight hundred and twenty years (reckoning from the early years of the Xuanhe reign). The *Stages of Female Alchemy in Verse* by Sun Bu’er appeared several decades later, while all other extant texts on female alchemy postdate Sun Bu’er, likely originating in the Ming and Qing dynasties. Though the *Scripture of the Yellow Court* was transmitted by Lady Wei of the Jin dynasty, it cannot be considered her own work. Similarly, while Xie Ziran and He Xiangu attained the Dao during the Tang, no writings of theirs survive (poems and prose produced through planchette writing at spirit altars cannot be attributed to their authors). Thus, we may affirm that among all formally composed alchemical texts written in the hand of female Perfected Ones throughout history—(Yingning’s note: Given the distinction between non-action and action-based methods, this work can only be termed a *Daoist text* rather than an *alchemical scripture*. The initial classification was an oversight, now duly corrected.)—this text stands as the most ancient.
