The text comprises one hundred twenty-eight lines in total. The principles expounded and the practices discussed are not confined to women alone but may equally apply to men. Below, the original text is presented in sequence, with each short line annotated in vernacular as follows:

I, Cao Wenyi, now declare to all present and future seekers of immortality and cultivators of the Dao: the true root of life’s stem lies ever in the *true breath*.
*I*: Cao Wenyi referring to herself.
*All present and future seekers*: those who study immortality and cultivate the Dao in her time and thereafter.
*True root*: the genuine and certain foundation.
*Life’s stem*: the most vital locus of human life. As the stem connects flower, leaf, melon, or fruit to the branch, so this point, once severed, causes flower and leaf to wither instantly and fruit to cease its growth.
*True breath* differs from *ordinary breath*. Ordinary breath is coarse; true breath is subtle. Ordinary breath is shallow; true breath is deep. Ordinary breath is swift; true breath is slow. True breath is the source of ordinary breath; ordinary breath is the manifestation of true breath. True breath may transform into ordinary breath, and ordinary breath may likewise return to true breath. For example, the spring water flowing from a crevice in a mountain stone is true breath; the surging, wind-tossed current of a great river is ordinary breath.

Illumine the body to attain eternal life—empty yet not empty, the numinous mirror embraces heaven’s countenance and holds all things.
*Illumine the body*: to turn the light inward and reflect upon one’s own nature.
*Eternal life*: the perpetual existence of the nature-body.
*Empty*: the nature-body is fundamentally empty. Yet because this nature-body encompasses all, true emptiness and wondrous being appear simultaneously, and thus it is also said to be *not empty*.
*Mirror*: a reflective surface. *Numinous mirror* refers to the nature-body.
*Embraces heaven’s countenance and holds all things*: the numinous mirror contains and encompasses heaven and earth, all myriad things.

The second line speaks of *ming* (life-destiny); the third and fourth lines speak of *xing* (nature).

The Supreme Ultimate unfolds its wonders; humanity receives the One—guard it well, lest it be lost.
The *Book of Changes* states: “The *Yi* possesses the Supreme Ultimate, which generates the Two Modes.” The *Daodejing* states: “These two emerge together yet bear different names; together they are called the Mystery. Mystery of mysteries, the gateway of all wonders.” This is the meaning of the Supreme Ultimate unfolding its wonders.
*The One* is the Dao. To *receive the One* is to attain the Dao. Laozi says: “The Dao generates the One.” Master Zhou says: “Non-Ultimate and yet Supreme Ultimate.” Laozi says: “The One generates the Two.” Confucius says: “The Supreme Ultimate generates the Two Modes.” Thus we may understand: the Dao is the Non-Ultimate; the One is the Supreme Ultimate; the Two are the Two Modes; the Two Modes are yin and yang; yin and yang are *xing* and *ming*; *xing* and *ming* are spirit and *qi*.
The Dao cannot be spoken or seen. Whatever can be spoken or seen is either the Two or the Three. For example: above and below, left and right, front and back, large and small, long and short, thick and thin, many and few, light and heavy, cold and hot, hard and soft, auspicious and inauspicious, beneficial and harmful, good and evil, right and wrong, empty and full, being and non-being, *xing* and *ming*, spirit and *qi*, yin and yang—all these paired opposites are the Two. That which lies between these Two is the Three. Once the Three arises, it may evolve into thousands and tens of thousands. Thus Laozi says: “The Three generate the myriad things.” Since the myriad things arise from the Dao, we humans, called the most numinous of the myriad things, are also born of the Dao. Without the Dao, there would be no world, nor would there be humanity.
That *humanity receives the One* means each person receives an infinitesimal portion of the Dao in its entirety. Yet this portion is exceedingly minute. If we were to lose even this tiny measure, we might not even attain human form in the next life, gradually descending into lower, baser creatures. Therefore, the author urges all to cherish this human birth, ever vigilant and cautious, safeguarding this Dao, lest it be lost.

Palaces lie vacant and serene—spirit naturally abides; the crucible of the numinous palace scorches the blood dry.
*Palaces lie vacant and serene*: a metaphor for the human body free of vices, harmful habits, deluded thoughts, and scattered intentions. If so, the primordial spirit naturally dwells securely within, never adrift or lost, never wandering without return. Yet in this world, people’s hearts are ceaselessly disturbed by the seven emotions and six desires, never knowing a moment of cool clarity.

When passions and desires stir, the yin fire follows in motion. As the yin fire moves, the qi, blood, and fluids of the entire body suffer harm, leaving one sallow and emaciated, the countenance withered and gaunt. The root of this affliction lies in the inability of every heart to see through and let go. Thus it is said: *the crucible of the numinous palace scorches the blood dry.*

The origin of human consciousness and thought is called the *numinous palace*. A single sorrow, a single joy, a single reflection, a single indulgence, a single strain—each wears away the body. When things go awry, one grieves; when fortune smiles, one rejoices. Faced with unsolvable difficulties, one broods. Fearful of not gaining what is desired, and once gained, fearful of losing it, the mind is ceaselessly taxed. Nine-tenths of the circumstances we encounter are setbacks; moments of success are rare. Within the fleeting decades of life, all is consumed by worry and suffering.

To relax the body and mind is indulgence; to tense them is strain. Alternating between indulgence and strain, the body cannot endure such ceaseless agitation and naturally decays into irreparable decline. *The form gnawed and ruined* describes the inner corruption of the body, like wood devoured by worms.

*Morning’s harm, evening’s loss*—blindly unaware of the peril, the spirit dissipates in confusion, unmoored. If asked how to live well in this world, or how to cultivate the Dao and attain liberation, they have not the slightest certainty. Gradually worn thin, slowly depleted, the primordial qi is exhausted, and the spirit departs.

As this gradual erosion unfolds, the harm to our bodies goes unfelt, sparing us sharp pain. As aging creeps in by degrees, the decades of a human life slip by all too easily, unnoticed. Unaware, our hair turns white; unnoticed, our skin wrinkles. Unaware, our blood dries up; unnoticed, our sinews stiffen.

Primordial *qi* is the original harmonious *qi* of the beginning. It is also called precelestial *qi*. Strictly speaking, it is the vital force that gives birth to heaven, earth, humanity, and all things. Though the vital force in the cosmos is boundless, the portion each person carries from the womb is pitifully small. From childhood to old age, over decades, the vital force stored within the body is exhausted, and our soul must then bid farewell to our flesh. When form and spirit part, how can one not die?

They claim that even mediocre Chan practitioners and sages can achieve this—yet it is not so for ordinary beings. *Chan* may be understood as *samādhi*. Those who strike lofty tones all know how to say: "In walking, there is *samādhi*; in sitting, there is *samādhi*; even in sleep and repose, there is *samādhi*—no need for any effort." If one were to practice diligently morning and night, they would dismiss it as excessive attachment, lacking in spontaneity and natural insight. Some might mock it as "polishing a brick to make a mirror." Yet such words may only be spoken to those of the highest caliber, not to ordinary people. Sages may act thus, but common folk can never attain it.

The tender sprout must be nurtured in restraint, for when root and perception fall into confusion, change comes all too easily.

When plants first emerge from the soil as seedlings, they are called "sprouts." Their substance is fragile and delicate, unable to withstand harm. They must be cultivated with proper methods and protected with care, only then may they one day grow into useful timber. This illustrates how the vital qi within the human body, if its foundation is unstable, is most easily lost. One must find a way to contain it within the body, preventing its constant dissipation outward—only then may one’s lifespan be extended.

The eyes, ears, nose, tongue, body, and mind are called the six roots. The functions they give rise to are the six perceptions. When roots and perceptions are disturbed by worldly dust, they fall into confusion, readily shifting from good to evil, from uprightness to depravity. Without the utmost diligent effort, one fears there will be no favorable outcome.

If one idly lets time slip by without clearing the brambles, who has ever heard of fine crops growing in a wasteland?

In a fallow field, thorns and brambles grow thick. If one is lazy and negligent, idle and indolent, failing to cut away the brambles in the field, good rice will never grow. These two lines compare how, if the deluded thoughts and bad habits in the human mind are not eradicated, progress in cultivation will be difficult, and good results will not easily appear.

When nine years of practice are complete and the firing process is perfected, one responds to things without intention, and spiritual transformation comes swiftly.

Nine is the supreme number among yang numbers. "Nine years" signifies pure yang—it does not necessarily mean precisely nine years. "Practice complete" means the work is perfected. "Firing process perfected" means that, by this stage of cultivation, one may bring a phase to its conclusion. "Responding to things" means engaging in worldly affairs to benefit others. "Without intention" means acting in accord with conditions, not deliberately seeking merit. "Spiritual transformation comes swiftly" means that when one applies their whole spirit to teaching and transformation, the effects naturally manifest quickly. As the *Mencius* says: *"What one passes through is transformed; what one abides in is spirit."* This conveys the same meaning.

The heart without intention is the true heart; forgetting both movement and stillness is liberation from desire.

The "heart without intention" refers to the essence of the heart free from thoughts. Ordinary people’s minds never rest from thoughts for even a minute. They mistake these thoughts for the true essence of the heart, but this is incorrect. You must understand that the heart without thoughts is the true heart, while the heart with thoughts is false. Once one recognizes the true heart, every movement and stillness becomes an expression of Heaven’s mechanism, allowing one to reach the state of forgetting both things and form—this is what is called "liberation from desire."

Spirit is nature, while qi is life—when the spirit does not stray outward, qi naturally settles.

Ancient alchemical classics often say: *"It is nature and life, not spirit and qi."* This is directed at those of deep cultivation and advanced attainment. Here, *"Spirit is nature, qi is life"* is stated for ordinary practitioners. Each serves its purpose and is not contradictory.

For ordinary people recognize only their own physical bodies. Beyond the body, they never pay attention to spirit and qi. If one can teach them to recognize the functions of these two words—spirit and qi—it already marks a great advancement over the average person. The true reality of "nature and life" must be left for them to contemplate in the future. In the initial stages of cultivation, one cannot do without spirit and qi. One must first gather one’s spirit within the body, and only then can qi settle.

Originally, these two are most intimately close—what could be more dear?
These two things are spirit and qi. They are inherently inseparable. If spirit leaves qi, it has nothing to nourish it; if qi leaves spirit, it has nothing to guide it. Without qi to nourish spirit, spirit will flee; without spirit to guide qi, qi will dissipate. To lose one is to lose both. What then remains to serve as the root of our body or the handle of our self?

Merge them into one, then forget even that oneness—thus may one emerge and submerge in unison with the primordial transformation.
To "merge them into one" means to practice such that the heart and breath depend on each other, spirit and qi unite. To "forget that oneness" means that after achieving unity, one must not cling rigidly to it but should let go of even this state of unity. Only when one can unite and then forget that unity will the qi within the body naturally rise and fall in harmony with the mechanism of primordial transformation. When the transformative mechanism should manifest, it manifests; when it should conceal itself, it conceals—without the slightest assertion of self.

Piercing metal and penetrating stone present no difficulty; sitting liberation or standing departure occur in an instant.
Ordinary people’s spirit is confined by the body and cannot directly reach external matter. Those who have succeeded in cultivation can separate their spirit from the body and command matter beyond it—thus piercing metal and penetrating stone present no difficulty. "In an instant" means within the blink of an eye. "Sitting liberation or standing departure" means that whether sitting or standing, if the spirit wishes to leave the body, it can do so in an instant, unconstrained by the body.

This Dao is easy to understand but difficult to practice—only when action is forgotten is the Way complete.

Even if one forces oneself to practice diligently, completion remains elusive. One must progress from forced effort to spontaneity, from spontaneity to utter integration, and from integration to effortless release—only then is it true that "when action is forgotten, the practice of the Way is complete."

Do not mistake breath-holding for the true work, nor counting breaths or following diagrams as the proper method. *Xi* (息) refers to the breath through the nostrils. *Bi xi* (闭息) means temporarily holding the breath. *Shu xi* (数息) means counting one’s breaths—from one, two, three, four, up to dozens or hundreds. *An tu* (按图) means practicing according to diagrams, either by fixating the mind on a single cavity within the body or by moving the hands and feet through various postures. None of these methods constitute the Great Dao. For breath-holding strains through force, counting breaths wearies the mind, and following diagrams fosters attachment—all far removed from the natural Great Way.

How is recent abandonment of external distractions, only to be entangled by internal concerns, any different?

Master Cao’s meaning is this: those who cultivate the Dao in these recent times have already set aside all worldly distractions beyond the body—why then cannot they set aside the distractions within, still clinging to so many concerns in the heart? I ask you: how does being entangled by the hundred affairs of the inner self differ from being bound by the myriad dusts of the outer world?

Consider the infant dwelling in the womb—during those ten months in the mother’s belly, did it ever secretly scheme or calculate? Since you seek to learn the Dao, why not first learn from the infant?

*"Focus the qi to attain suppleness—can you be like the infant?"*
Thus says the *Dao De Jing*, Chapter Ten.
*"Focus the qi"* means to concentrate the mind single-pointedly upon the qi in one’s practice.
*"Attain suppleness"* means to refine one’s work to the utmost softness, without the slightest rigidity or haste.
If one can achieve this, the spirit will long abide within the body, never straying outward.
*"When the spirit does not stray outward, the qi naturally settles."*
Once the qi settles, the true breath will arise of its own accord.
*"Leisurely, leisurely"*—these words describe the manner of the true breath: deep and enduring, gentle and unhurried.

*"Unbroken and winding, it returns to the root of life—without drawing from the numinous spring, it ever flows of itself."*
*"Unbroken"* signifies the ceaseless, subtle continuity.
*"Winding"* suggests its meandering, unbroken progression.
The *"root of life"* is the very source of human existence.
This line depicts the movement of the true breath within the body—though it reaches every limb and bone, it naturally returns to its origin.
The *"numinous spring"*—later called *"spirit-water"*—flows downward on the earth’s surface, never upward; to use it, one must draw it with vessels.
Yet the numinous spring within the body requires no drawing—it circulates naturally throughout the entire form.
Wherever the true breath arrives, the numinous spring arrives.
For saliva transforms into qi, and qi transforms into saliva, filling the whole body—thus its wondrous function.

*"Thirty-six thousand marks the great work—within it lie the rhythms of yin and yang."*
In the modern calendar, a day and night comprise ninety-six *ke*; in the ancient calendar, a hundred *ke*.
Master Zhang Ziyang’s preface to *Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* states:
*"A year has twelve months, a month thirty days, a day a hundred ke—one month totals three thousand ke, ten months thirty thousand ke. Whether moving, standing, sitting, or lying down, maintain unbroken awareness. Once the fetal qi congeals, the infant manifests; the Mysterious Pearl takes form, the Supreme Unity unites with the Real. Within these thirty thousand ke, one may seize thirty thousand years of celestial time—why? Because each ke of practice contains a year’s seasonal rhythm. Thus thirty thousand ke can seize thirty thousand years. Hence, a year’s twelve months total thirty-six thousand. Even the dullest of men, by practicing this, may ascend to sagehood—yet how is it that the common folk use it daily without knowing?"*

This passage is quite clear and may serve as commentary here.

Cao Wenyi lived during the Xuanhe era of Emperor Huizong of the Song, 790 years before the Republican era. Zhang Ziyang lived during the Xining era of Emperor Shenzong of the Song, approximately 840 years before the Republican era. The two were separated by no more than fifty years, and thus their doctrines share considerable similarity.

*Steaming fuses, joints and vessels transform—*
*In every part, radiance shines, nowhere obstructed.*

These two lines describe the effects of the practice. *Steaming* refers to evaporation, *fusing* to dissolution; *joints* are the body’s articulations, *vessels* its blood channels, and *transform* the process of change. First comes evaporation, then dissolution. Through constant dissolution, hardness is prevented, allowing gradual transformation. This practice is called "Golden Elixir Bone-Transmutation." *Radiance shining in every part* echoes the *Sun Bu’er’s Scripture of the Female Elixir*, which states, "Where the primordial spirit travels, all orifices emit light." *Nowhere obstructed* means the entire body is fully unblocked, with no closures anywhere.

*The Three Pengs depart the yin-corpse’s abode—*
*Myriad realms pay homage at the Crimson Emperor’s palace.*

The *Three Pengs* are the Three Corpses. Daoist texts state: "The upper corpse, named Peng Ju, dwells in the head, causing foolishness and lack of wisdom; the middle corpse, named Peng Zhi, resides in the chest, stirring vexation and preventing clarity; the lower corpse, named Peng Jiao, lodges in the abdomen, inciting cravings for food, drink, and sensual desires." They are also called the Three Corpse Spirits or Three Corpse Worms. The *Great Clarity’s Central Scripture of the Yellow Court* contains the lines: *"Alas for the twelve towers of the Mysterious Palace—who knew they would become the abode of the Three Worms?"* This means that our pure and refined bodies are infested by the Three Corpse Worms, rendering them filthy and wretched—a great pity.

Daoist methods for slaying the Three Corpses include talismans, incantations, observing the Gengshen day, or ingesting elixirs, but none offer a thorough resolution. Here, through internal refinement, one circulates the primordial harmonizing *qi* to fill the viscera, steaming and fusing the joints and vessels, transforming the sinews and bones. This forces the Three Corpses to abandon their lair and flee. Once the corrupt departs, the pure arrives.

All nations paying homage—this metaphor describes how the essence, *qi*, and spirit of the five viscera, six bowels, four limbs, and hundred bones all converge in the Crimson Palace. The Crimson Palace pertains to the region of the heart; the heart belongs to fire, its color is red, and physicians call it the "Palace of the Sovereign Lord," hence its name, the "Palace of the Red Emperor."

I ask you: whence comes the True Person?
From of old, he has dwelt only in the Numinous Terrace.
The True Person is the true self. Our physical body, subject to birth and death, cannot be called the true self—it is but a provisional self. Setting aside the corporeal form, what remains is that intangible thought. Can this be called the true self? No, for even that thought arises and vanishes inconstant, not subject to our mastery. Only when we further set aside these fleeting thoughts and discover instead an indestructible entity—eternal across ten thousand kalpas—do we find the true self, also known as the True Person. This True Person, though never before seen, is now met for the first time. Yet whence does he come? In truth, he has always resided within our Numinous Terrace, never once departing, nor has he entered from without.

*Of old, thick clouds and mist obscured him—
Today we meet as the Dao-eye opens.*

For years, clouds and mist have veiled the True Person’s visage. Though he has always dwelt within the Numinous Terrace, we could not recognize him. Now, as our practice reaches a certain depth, the Dao-eye opens. With the Dao-eye opened, it is as though clouds and mist are swept away to reveal the blue sky, and the True Person thus appears. The "clouds and mist" are metaphors for our seven emotions, six desires, deluded thoughts, and scattered notions.

*This is no work of a morning or an evening—
What I find is my original truth, not some artifice.*

This practice cannot be accomplished in a day or a night; it requires the passage of many years. Nor is it a matter of clever techniques or seeking unexpected gains—it is merely the rediscovery of our original true face.

*In the biting cold, firm as metal and stone,
Driving back yin demons, wisdom and power grow.*

The *Analects* states: *"Only in the cold of winter do we know that the pine and cypress are the last to wither."* "Biting cold" refers to the year’s most frigid season. "Wither" describes trees shedding their leaves. That the pine and cypress wither last means that while other trees have withered and fallen by this time, the pine and cypress remain verdant. This illustrates how those who cultivate the Dao possess enduring strength, able to endure hardship and tribulation without wavering in their resolve. "Firm" shares the same meaning as "steadfast." Even the unwithering pine and cypress are deemed steadfast, yet metal and stone are even more so. Thus, here metal and stone serve as metaphors for the resolve of the Dao cultivator. With a will as unyielding as metal and stone, one naturally repels yin demons. Once yin demons are wholly dispelled, wisdom and power simultaneously increase. "Wisdom" is insight; "power" is fortitude. Without fortitude, one may glimpse the Dao but cannot fulfill it. Without insight, one risks misrecognizing the great Dao and straying into side paths. Only when both wisdom and fortitude are complete can regret be avoided.

*From emptiness and tranquility, returning to refinement and focus—
This is the Pure Land of Huaxu.*

To be free of deluded thoughts and desires is emptiness; to be unstained by any craving or vice is tranquility. To investigate thoroughly and comprehend fully is refinement. To accept and uphold with unwavering constancy is focus. The *Liezi* states: *"The Yellow Emperor, napping by day, dreamed he wandered in the land of Huaxu. In that land, there were no teachers or rulers. Its people had no desires."*

"Unknowing of closeness to self, unknowing of distance from things—thus free from love and hate.
Unknowing of opposition, unknowing of compliance—thus free from benefit and harm."
This is, in truth, a parable, akin to what moderns call a utopia.
If one’s heart could attain perfect purity, it would be as though one had arrived in the land of Huaxu.

At first, how does one establish a foundation in non-action where nothing is left undone?
In worldly affairs, whatever one undertakes, one must first establish a foundation before any achievement is possible.
The cultivation of the Dao is a great endeavor; one must stabilize the foundation to advance step by step.
When skill is refined and attainment profound, all things naturally manifest the Dao.
Outwardly, it may seem as though nothing is done,
yet in reality, essence is complete, qi is complete, spirit is complete—without the slightest deficiency.
Laozi’s *Daodejing*, Chapter Three, states: *"Act through non-action, and nothing is left ungoverned."*
Chapter Thirty-Seven again says: *"The Dao constantly takes no action, yet nothing is left undone."*
This passage’s *"at the place of non-action, nothing is left undone"* likewise follows Laozi’s meaning.

Within thought’s realm, images must be uprooted;
in dreams, the spirit must be firmly held.
These two lines describe the method of establishing the foundation.
When one sits in meditation, one must sweep clean the heart’s stray thoughts.
Once sitting practice is mastered, stray thoughts can be entirely extinguished.
Then, even in sleep and dreams, one does not forget the work of cultivating the Dao,
remaining as composed as in ordinary meditation, fully self-possessed.

Non-movement and non-stillness are the great essentials;
non-square and non-round are the supreme Dao.
If practice leans toward movement, it risks excessive restlessness;
if it leans toward stillness, it risks excessive desiccation.
If temperament leans toward square, it risks rigidity;
if it leans toward round, it risks slickness.
Only by avoiding both extremes and attaining harmony is the great Dao realized.

Refining primordial harmony within completes true breath;
seeking without only prolongs unending toil.
If one can circulate within the body the primordial, harmonious qi without cease, one may attain the Dao.
But if one clings forever to external breathing, refusing to relinquish it, one will never achieve liberation.
If primordial qi does not abide, spirit cannot settle;
a worm-eaten tree, rootless, withers its branches and leaves.
Primordial qi is the primordial, harmonious qi mentioned above.
If it does not abide—if it cannot long remain within the body but leaks outward—
excessive dissipation depletes the body’s primordial qi.
As the primordial qi wanes, the primordial spirit, lacking nourishment,
cannot remain within the body and must flee.
Like a tree infested by borers, its roots damaged, its branches and leaves naturally wither.
When the body’s primordial qi is harmed by the seven emotions, the six desires, hunger and satiety, cold and heat, mental strain and physical toil, the body cannot endure.

Set aside talk of snivel, spittle, blood, and essence—
tracing to their source, all are one.
The fluid produced by the nose is called snivel; that from the mouth, spittle;
that from the heart, blood; that from the external kidneys, essence.
Though these four bear different names, their fundamental origin is the same.
To *reach the root* is to see through to their source.

To *trace to their source* is to investigate their origins.
These substances have never held fixed positions—they transform in response to circumstances and the mind’s intent.

The various fluids of the human body are not partitioned into rigid domains, confined to one place without movement.
Rather, they arise in the moment, shaped by external stimuli and internal impulses.

When the body senses heat, they become sweat;
when the eyes sense grief, they become tears.
The fluids within the skin, stirred by high external temperatures, transform into sweat and emerge through the pores.
The fluids within the eyes, moved by overwhelming sorrow, transform into tears and flow from the tear ducts.

When the kidneys sense desire, they become essence;
when the nose senses wind, they become snivel.
The fluids within the external kidneys, aroused by lustful thoughts, transform into essence and issue from the urethra.
The fluids within the nasal membranes, chilled by cold winds, transform into snivel and flow from the nostrils.

Flowing freely throughout the body, they moisten the whole—
yet in the end, they never depart from the *spiritual water*.
*Vertical* refers to the body’s upper and lower.

Horizontal refers to the body’s front and back, left and right. *Flowing and turning* describes its circulation throughout the body. *Moistening the whole* means it reaches and nourishes every part without exception. That such transformations and effects arise at all is due ultimately to the workings of *spiritual water*.

This treasure of *spiritual water* is so profound in principle that words can scarcely describe it. Though the learned abound in the world, few recognize it. Know that sweat, tears, snivel, spittle, essence, and blood all derive from *spiritual water*, and *spiritual water* itself arises from *true qi*. Without *true qi* in the body, *spiritual water* will inevitably dry up. Once *spiritual water* is exhausted, the eyes cannot see, the ears cannot hear, the nose cannot smell, the tongue cannot taste, the generative organs cannot reproduce, and the limbs and joints cannot move. At this stage, death is not far off.

Note: What scholars of alchemical classics find most perplexing is that the same term may be used interchangeably across different methods. For instance, the term *spiritual water* is explained here in this way, yet in other alchemical texts, though the term appears identically, its meaning may differ entirely. Consider Zhang Ziyang’s postscript to *The Understanding Reality Classic*: “The essence of the Golden Elixir lies in *spiritual water* and the *Flowery Pond*.” Again, in Zhang Ziyang’s *Four Hundred Characters on the Golden Elixir*, he writes: “When lead encounters mercury, it is called the *Flowery Pond*; when mercury enters lead, it is called *spiritual water*.” This refers to *spiritual water* in the Human-Realm Elixir method. In Zhang Ziyang’s *Secret Formula of the Golden Medicine*, he states: “Golden water is the mysterious water that partakes of the metal’s qi, also called *spiritual water*. The secret of elixir refinement is simply to guide *spiritual water* into the *Flowery Pond*—then all is accomplished.” In Xu Zhenjun’s *Stone Casket Record*, the *Sacred Stone’s Mysterious Pointer* reads: “Lead sand is kneaded into clods, sealed with the Six-One method and cherished. Fired for a day and night, the flame dies, the smoke clears, and the earth turns to ash. The ascending lead is inverted into the ash pond, where fire melts it into *spiritual water*.” This is *spiritual water* in the Heaven-Realm Elixir method. The *Mirror Casket* states: “If one knows true mercury, within yellow gold lies *spiritual fire*; if one knows true lead, within white gold lies *spiritual water*.” Bai Ziqing’s *True Formula of the Earth-Realm* says: “The *Flowery Pond’s spiritual water*—*spiritual water* is true gold. Flashing with primordial radiance, it releases the Qian-metal’s potency.” This is *spiritual water* in the Earth-Realm Elixir method. Lingyangzi’s *Secret Canon of the Grotto-Heaven* writes: “Yin-yang lead and mercury become *spiritual water*; its workings never depart from lead. Who recognizes the *spiritual water* of the elixir furnace? It is the moon’s essence and golden nectar.” Wu Chongxu’s self-commentary in *Songs of Immortal Cultivation* notes: “‘Dark advancement’ refers to the subtle infusion of *spiritual water* and *spiritual fire*, pertaining to the work of refinement. ‘Bright advancement’ refers to the overt infusion of *spiritual water* and *spiritual fire*, pertaining to the work of transcendence.” Zhu Chibo’s *Lamp of Gold and Fire* states: “Raw lead contains only *ren-water* and *kui-water*; once refined into white gold, *spiritual water* emerges within.” This is *spiritual water* in the art of aurifaction.

The term *spiritual water* in the various alchemical classics listed above differs as vastly from that in *The Song of the Great Dao’s Spiritual Source* as heaven does from earth. Langranzi’s poem reads:
*“The spinal river-carriage pierces the crown gate—
this is the swiftest path of true cultivation.
Frequently swallowing the *Flowery Pond’s spiritual water*,
the Purple Mansion’s Primordial Lord ascends directly.
Ever let qi surge through the joints,
and naturally essence fills while the Valley Spirit abides.
One day attaining the path of longevity,
grateful for the guidance once received.”*
Here, the term *spiritual water* refers specifically to the saliva in the mouth—its narrow sense. In *The Song of the Great Dao’s Spiritual Source*, *spiritual water* encompasses all coarse and fine fluids of the body—its broad sense. Though the meanings overlap, they are not entirely the same. If a scholar knows only one and not the other, clinging to first impressions and assuming identical terms imply identical methods, they mislead both others and themselves.

The *Celestial Origin Elixir Method* emphasizes ingestion but not transmutation. The *Terrestrial Origin Elixir Method* enables transmutation and may further refine the elixir for ingestion, thereby ascending to the *Celestial Origin*. *Yellow and White Arts* achieve only transmutation and cannot advance beyond. The *Human Origin Elixir Method* employs yin and yang of the same kind; though terms like *lead, silver, cinnabar,* and *mercury* appear, they bear no relation to the five metals or eight minerals. These distinctions mark the four elixir methods.

The guiding principle of *The Song of the Great Dao’s Spiritual Source* is Daoist cultivation—not elixir refinement, nor Chan meditation or contemplative stillness. Scholars must discern its purport with clarity, allowing no ambiguity.

Most extant elixir scriptures and Daoist texts suffer from vagueness, their principles muddled, often inducing weariness in readers. Having deeply grasped their flaws, I prioritize analysis, seeking to apply scientific rigor to immortal studies, thereby guiding later comrades. For this reason, my writings often diverge from predecessors’ views, a necessity I hope readers will understand.

Furthermore, in *Yangshan Semi-Monthly* (Issue 41, page 6), the closing couplet of *The Jade Flower Palace’s Immortal Scribe’s Descent-Poem* reads:
*“To cherish past affinities and awaken future insight,*
*Early preserve true fluid to ferry decay and decline.”*
Here, *true fluid* precisely aligns with the original meaning of *spiritual water* in *The Song of the Great Dao’s Spiritual Source*. Only by preserving *true fluid* can one ferry decay and decline—just as retaining *spiritual water* sustains life. This principle stands as unshakable as ironclad truth.

Since the Perfected One Cao Wenyi once selflessly revealed the foremost method of cultivation for posterity, you, having encountered her verses even eight centuries later, may as well hear her voice and see her person. Thus, I earnestly urge you to diligently practice, lest you squander your years. Otherwise, upon rebirth, you may not again chance upon such an opportunity.

Only know to embrace tranquility and dispassion, freeing the mind from thoughts and cares,
Observe fasting and still the heart, restraining speech.

The phrase *"only know"* means to follow solely the method described below, disregarding all else.
*Tranquility* is inner stillness; *dispassion* is regarding worldly vanities with indifference.
*Thoughts* are the workings of the mind; *cares* are anxieties.
*Fasting* was an ancient preparatory practice before sacrifices to heaven, earth, or spirits—
such as bathing, changing garments, abstaining from wine, meat, and music.
*Stilling the heart* is to quiet wayward thoughts; *restraining speech* is to guard one’s words.

A single taste of ghee and sweet dew—hunger and thirst dispelled, true simplicity revealed.
The first transformation of milk is *curd*; the second, *raw butter*; the third, *clarified butter*; the fourth, *ghee*.
Ghee may be considered the essence distilled from milk.
There is a variety of plantain called *sweet-dew plantain*, whose flower buds contain a nectar of exquisite sweetness—this is *sweet dew*.
It may be regarded as the essence distilled from the plantain.
*"A single taste"* signifies no other—
for the work above is pure and still, and thus the effects arising within the body are sweet and cooling.

Hunger craves food, thirst craves drink—
both reveal a lack within the body, a need for replenishment to sustain it.
If the body were whole, without lack,
hunger and thirst would vanish of themselves, and one could enter *great samādhi*,
naturally beholding the original face.
All silk untainted by dye is called *plain*;
our true face is pristine, flawless, unstained by a speck of dust—
thus it is called *true simplicity*.

In time, when the work is complete, one will roam free in boundless ease,
yet at the outset, the labor is truly arduous and bitter.
With *martial fire*, it is called *forging*; with *civil fire*, *simmering*.
What is *martial fire*?
Rousing the spirit, sweeping away stray thoughts,
sitting upright, body aligned, breath and mind interwoven.
What is *civil fire*?
Utter relaxation, inner light contained,
subtle as a thread of silk, still as if nothing were there.

Amid the toil, one must yet remain effortlessly at ease—
all to nurture the *primordial spirit*.
Though the work demands perseverance,
it is not a matter of straining mind or force, of restless hands or feet.
Thus Laozi’s *Dao De Jing* offers this secret:
*“Use it without exertion.”*
At first glance, “exertion” and “non-exertion” seem to contradict—
but know that *“non-exertion”* means no forced labor, no clinging,
no pulling shoots to hasten their growth.
*“Exertion”* means no idleness, no slackening,
no fits and starts like sun and frost.
*“At ease”* is the mark of non-exertion.
Only when at ease can one preserve the *primordial qi*;
only by preserving *qi* can one nurture the *primordial spirit*.

Alas, the human heart ever seeks rest yet cannot attain it.
Though circumstance may bind, preventing full release,
half the struggle lies in habits forged over countless kalpas—
hard to shed.
The practitioner is often burdened by this very thought.
At such a juncture, whether to indulge or restrain,
to let go or seize control, rests entirely with the individual.

I once endured suffering upon suffering—
coarse food, tattered robes, solitude, and silence.
As Immortal Cao recounted of her own practice:
in those days, she endured hardships beyond measure.
What she ate and wore were cast-offs from others.
Her surroundings were desolate, her world still.

The heart knows the Great Dao cannot be practiced—reputation, deeds, and the body become grave afflictions.
Though the mind clearly recognizes the Dao as supreme, alas, it cannot be put into action.
The reason it cannot be practiced lies in three burdens: first, empty fame; second, worldly affairs; third, the physical form.
The burden of empty fame is that the capable are often overworked;
the burden of worldly affairs is the entanglement of power and duty;
the burden of the body is the necessity of food, clothing, shelter, and movement.

For example, when at leisure to cultivate, how could any effort compare to sitting in perfect serenity?
Those who cultivate the Dao fear nothing more than lacking leisure.
Even when leisure is attained, they are often encumbered by myriad minor techniques and peripheral practices.
For instance, when body and mind have already reached a state of tranquility,
rather than pursuing various secondary methods, it is far better to relinquish all and focus solely on the practice of *great stillness*.
The character *zheng* (争) is equivalent to *zen* (怎), and *zhengsi* (争似) means "how could it compare to."
(Note: In the second poem of Zhang Ziyang’s *Wuzhen Pian*, the line reads: *"The great elixir is not sought—how could it be encountered?"*
In the thirteenth poem: *"How could it compare to planting lotuses in fire?"*
In the first *quatrain*: *"How could one attain the golden elixir without understanding life?"*
In the eighth: *"How could it compare to true lead merging with the sacred mechanism?"*
In the fortieth: *"How could the golden crow seize the hare?"*
In the sixty-fourth: *"How could one be taught to see the workings of concealment and revelation?"*
All instances of *zheng* are to be understood as *zen*. The idioms used in Song-dynasty writings differ from those of today. This annotation is provided for the reader’s convenience.)

Though form and spirit are said to be difficult to perfect together, one cannot first complete *ming* without completing *xing*.
The essence of the Great Dao lies in perfecting both spirit and form.
Perfecting spirit is commonly called *xing* cultivation;
perfecting form is commonly called *ming* cultivation.
For those who cultivate the Dao, achieving the unity of form and spirit is the highest attainment.
If this is not possible, one may first cultivate *xing* to perfect the spirit,
and when the opportunity arises, cultivate *ming* to perfect the form—this is also acceptable.
The following passage describes the method for completing *xing* and perfecting the spirit.

Avoid chasing fame and profit, sever all worldly ties—then nothing will disturb you.
By refusing to compete for reputation and gain, and cutting off social obligations,
one may reach a state of unburdened tranquility.

Resolute action lies with the individual—what binds others need not hinder me.
Who could restrain me if I choose to act?
To forsake fame and profit and reject social entanglements may seem difficult,
but where there is a will, obstacles are not insurmountable.
If I am truly determined, who can control me?

Obstruction signifies hindrance; control signifies interference.
What use are soaring fame and swift prose?
"Praising the heavens" describes one’s lofty reputation;
"leaning on a horse" describes the speed of one’s writing—
yet neither serves the Dao.

Glory and fine garments, food and comforts—all are met with indifference.
Piling up jade and amassing gold—what good do they do?
The first line speaks of single-minded devotion to the Way, unmoved by glory or comfort.
The second line asks: if the wealthy refuse to cultivate the Dao,
what use is their wealth when old age, sickness, and death arrive?

Crafty compositions and florid verses—
mastery of them only obstructs the path of cultivation.
To become a man of letters is of little use,
and may even hinder one’s progress.

Like thin mist and drifting smoke,
idly lingering by fallen blossoms and willow floss—
such is the scholar who achieves nothing of substance,
adrift and rootless, floating and insubstantial.

Wandering between heaven and earth,
never becoming nourishing rain or dew.
The first line describes the form of mist and smoke;
the second, their futility—
unlike rain and dew, which benefit the world,
mist and smoke remain ever useless,
never transforming into life-giving moisture.
"Drifting" depicts their rootless wandering;
"floating" their emptiness and unreality.

Fame and life—which is closer?
No one fails to cherish reputation,
and none neglects the body.
Yet when weighed, which is truly dear?
Naturally, the body is nearer.
Alas, how half a lifetime slips by in idle procrastination—
"procrastination" meaning adherence to old ways.

We cling to the dietary and marital habits passed down by humanity’s primal ancestors, never willing to change. We submit to the laws of nature governing birth, aging, illness, and death, never daring to defy them. All this is called *yin xun* (adherence to old ways).

Of late—meaning in recent years, roughly from middle age onward—
one’s body gradually decays, and all efforts at cultivation depend on *shen* and *qi*.
If *shen* and *qi* cannot settle within, all practice is but empty toil.

Pity the fine foundation, the golden hall and jade chamber, left without its master—
the body likened to a house, the *yuan shen* (original spirit) to its owner.
If the owner constantly wanders outside, never returning to the chamber,
the house, though fine, will fall into ruin from neglect, lack of upkeep, and disrepair.

We must persuade the master to dwell long within,
to abide in stillness and emptiness, free from distraction,
so that old habits may slowly fade.

In the state of profound stillness, the original spirit cannot be called "existent."
When stirred to response, it cannot be called "nonexistent"—
it is best described as "subtle existence within nonbeing."
All truths beyond words or thought are called *miao* (subtle).
*Miao you* (subtle existence) is such a truth—
neither leaning toward being nor nonbeing, hence difficult to grasp,
meaning it eludes firm hold, slipping beyond reach.

How, then, to begin practice?
Consider how a child is nurtured wholly by its mother’s care.
If the original spirit is the child, what is its mother?
Laozi’s *Dao De Jing* states:
*"Nameless, it is the origin of heaven and earth;
Named, it is the mother of all things."* (Chapter 1)
*"Alone I differ from others,
For I honor the mother as my sustenance."* (Chapter 20)
*"There is a thing, formless yet complete,
Born before heaven and earth,
Silent, vast, standing alone, unchanging,
Circling endlessly, never exhausted—
It may be called the mother of all under heaven.
I know not its name; I style it the Dao."* (Chapter 25)
Thus, we may conclude: the mother is the Dao.
To nurture the original spirit, one must rely on the power of the Dao.

What is the Dao?
The Dao is yin and yang; yin and yang are *xing* (nature) and *ming* (life);
*xing* and *ming* are *shen* and *qi*.
The first step in practice is to guide *qi* with *shen* and nourish *shen* with *qi*.
When *shen* and *qi* unite, one cultivates the Dao.

Seal away sharp eloquence, set aside cleverness,
withdraw the spirit, feign dullness—
for the spirit manifests in hearing and sight as *cong ming* (keen perception),
in speech and writing as *jun bian* (brilliant discourse).
*Jian* means to seal; *cang*, to conceal; *chu*, to discard.
*Shou juan* means to retract.
These lines urge one to gather the spirit within,
not dissipate it outward,
to emulate Laozi’s words:
*"Great eloquence seems inarticulate;
Great skill seems clumsy."* (*Dao De Jing*)
This is the initial method of Daoist practice.

Steel the heart, focus the will,
and walk the great path without doubt—
in the end, you will find the Dao does not betray you.
The danger lies in refusing this path,
preferring crooked byways and heretical gates,
gaining nothing in this life
and inviting retribution in the next—
why suffer so in vain?

*Preface to the Anthology of Female Alchemy Poems*

**Compiled by Ji Yizi of Jinxī, Fu Jinquan**

**Three Poems by Immortal Maiden Wu Cailuan, with Preface**

Cailuan was the daughter of Wu Meng. Meng served the Kingdom of Wu as Magistrate of Xi’an. A perfected adept, Ding Yi, transmitted the Daoist arts to him, and Meng in turn passed them to Xu Xun of Nanchang. Xu, then Magistrate of Jingyang, heard of the profound Dao of Immortal Mother Shen of Danyang and journeyed with Meng to seek her instruction. The Mother bestowed her esoteric teachings upon Xu, who then requested that she also transmit them to Meng. She refused, commanding instead that Xu relay them to him.

Cailuan studied under Ding Yi’s daughter, Xiuying, and upon attaining the Dao, ascended with her father.

*Her heart like a jade vase of ice,*
*No dust allowed the slightest trace.*
*Though dust may cloud the jade vase whole,*
*She mounts the jade terrace’s highest place.*

*Fame and disgrace—why strive or fight?*
*Floating clouds cannot dim the moon’s bright light.*
*Call her ox or horse—she answers still,*
*But never lets a speck of dust invade her will.*

*Though dwelling in the city, her nature stays in mountains high,*
*In the puppet’s theater, all is but idle play.*
*A mustard seed contains the boundless void,*
*And all the scriptures of the great chiliocosm may be erased away.*

---

**Six Poems by Immortal Maiden Fan Yunqiao, with Preface**

Fan Yunqiao was the wife of Liu Gang. Both possessed the Daoist arts, able to summon spirits and gods, command transformations, and practice secret cultivation unknown to others. As magistrate, he governed with purity, simplicity, and ease, bringing blessings to the people. The land prospered, and his virtue was revered far and wide.

Often, she and Gang tested their arts against each other. Once, Gang set fire to a guest mill, flames rising from the east. His wife summoned rain from the west to quell them. In their courtyard stood two peach trees: Gang incanted, and one branch fell beyond the fence; she incanted, and the other vanished into a casket. Gang spat into a plate, and it became a fish; she spat, and it was devoured by an otter.

One day, they entered the Siming Mountains, where a tiger blocked their path. Gang subdued it, and the beast crouched, roaring. His wife approached to observe, and the tiger dared not lift its gaze. They captured it and tethered it beside their bed.

On the day of their ascension, beside the county hall stood a great honey locust tree. Gang soared upward from its crown, while his wife sat serenely upon her bed, rising gently like a cloud. Together, they ascended to heaven.

Later, they reappeared upon a boat at Lan Bridge, where they instructed Pei Hang in the Dao, giving him their sister Yunying in marriage. Together, they attained the supreme fruit.

*Qian’s strength is firm, Kun’s virtue soft—*
*Seek first within stillness the work’s true source.*
*Stir clear a ladle of Jade Pool’s water,*
*Why need the seven treasures to polish the moon’s course?*

*Dragon and tiger, wild horse—why cage in vain?*
*Let them soar, and karmic obstructions will obscure the way.*
*In perfect quiescence lies the wondrous Dharma:*
*Capture them, and they become a single day’s breeze.*

*To nurture nature, first calm the heart.*
*Why labor to thread needles in vain art?*
*When the iron ox turns with you in tow,*
*Then shall boundless wisdom’s depth be shown.*

*Why pray for long life? Seek instead*
*The primordial spirit’s utter clarity.*
*Shatter empty space—then all is free.*
*Mani and śarīra are but empty names, you see.*

*Within a golden house dwell two fair maids,*
*Their forms may charm, yet their intent betrays.*
*If from the moon a sun should rise,*
*The night-shining pearl the dragon’s mouth displays.*

*Waves of the River of Love rise high,*
*Dense, they sink; light, they fly.*
*If with oars you strike with bold resolve,*
*How can the weak waters of Penglai be crossed with difficulty?*

---

**Six Poems by Immortal Maiden Yuehua Cui Shaoxuan, with Preface**

**Preface and Six Poems by Immortal Maiden Yuehua Cui Shaoxuan**

Cui Shaoxuan was the youngest daughter of Cui Gong, Prefect of Fenzhou during the late Tang. Born with refined beauty, she was devoted to the Dao from childhood. At fifteen, she married to the frontier, where she cultivated for ten years of arduous practice and attained the Way at twenty-four. While her husband served as an official in Minqiao, passing through Jianxi and the Wuyi Mountains, he saw amidst the clouds the Purple Empyrean Sovereign and the Lady of Fusang, who asked him, *"Has the Lord of Moonlight arrived?"* Perplexed, he questioned them, and they replied, *"I was once the Left Attendant of the Jade Emperor, recorded as the Lord of Moonlight. By karmic destiny, I was demoted to be your wife."* Later, after resigning from office, she left a letter for her husband in Luoyang, which read:

*"Attainment of the One Origin comes not from heaven’s bestowal.*
*The truth of the Great Elder, the supreme immortals—*
*Light veiled, shadow concealed, form arises from spontaneity.*
*The real abides not in seeking; long does man tarry.*
*Pure and fair is its truth, its nature both firm and yielding.*
*Amidst cinnabar skies and azure heavens, it joins the ranks of the highest sages.*
*A hundred years hence, naught remains but an empty mound."*

With the letter complete, she transformed.

On the third day, her reflection appeared like the moon’s crescent,
Facing the sunlight, radiant and bright.
To achieve the coalescence of yin and yang on a jade night,
First seek the message and grasp the golden waves.

Where innate nature is clear, the foundation of life stands firm;
Nine revolutions of the River Chariot, nine cauldrons complete.
Gold Tiger and Jade Dragon unite in harmony,
The Three Flowers present a minor immortal.

The heart, still as water, drifts serenely,
Ever tranquil, ever wakeful—thus progress in cultivation.
Nurture the crow’s plumpness, foster the hare’s leanness,
And the magic fungus blooms atop the emerald peak.

Know that beneath the earth, heaven also exists;
Devote the heart to self-cultivation, and you seek immortality.
Once the principles of yin and yang are fully realized,
All lies in the primal qi of ceaseless generation.

How fleeting the time of green tresses and rosy cheeks!
In an instant, crane-white hair tangles like silk.
Parting the curtain, she glimpses the plum blossoms bloom—
A moment of spring’s splendor must not be delayed.

She sought no external protection, nor did she study Chan;
The vicissitudes of the world beneath her gaze she let pass.
Know that the cinnabar path ascends straight upward—
The Mysterious Pearl lies only within my breast.

**Four Poems by Perfected Guangzhen of the Tang, with Preface**

Perfected Guangzhen of the Tang, a native of Yanzhou, served her mother with utmost filial piety. After marriage, she suffered from a blood ailment, but dreamed of a Daoist adept who gave her medicine, and she was cured. From then on, she devoted herself to the Dao. She revered Immortal He Xiangu and one day received the profound mysteries directly from her. During the Chunxi era of the Song, three immortals led her to the seashore, where she rode a giant toad across the sea and wandered through famous mountains. The immortals asked her, *"Do you wish to transcend the mundane and enter sagehood? To preserve your form and dwell in the world? Or to abandon your bones and ascend as an immortal?"* She replied, *"My mother still lives; I wish to serve and care for her until the end."* They bestowed upon her a single elixir pill, which she swallowed, and thereafter she no longer consumed grains. Later, she was summoned to the Deshou Palace and enfeoffed as Perfected One of Serene Stillness and Congealed Spirit.

*Face-to-face with the Mysterious Mechanism, one searches in vain—*
*Only by fixing the gaze does one fathom its profound truth.*
*The nature of being defies description in words;*
*When karmic conditions are settled, the true heart is revealed.*

*The heart and nature were ever the simplest to understand,*
*Yet they shift like evening clouds over the mountain peak—*
*East or west, south or north, all places are fit to dwell,*
*Then one may wander Penglai’s peaks or the azure sea.*

*Not knowing one’s nature, not knowing one’s fate,*
*One splits heaven and earth, dividing the path in two.*
*But when the elixir is formed through their union,*
*One becomes drunk within the gourd, needing no support.*

*Without anger, without joy, the qi harmonizes and warms;*
*Responding to affairs as they arise, like wind chasing clouds.*
*The tiger subdued, the dragon tamed, the heart grows still—*
*Beneath the bright moon of the azure sky, white [snow/petals] scatter.*

**Five Poems by Perfected One of Mystic Stillness, Lady Zhou, with Preface**

Perfected One of Mystic Stillness, née Zhou, was the mother of Wang Chuyi of Dongmu in Ninghai. Chuyi was born in the second year of the Huangtong era of the Jin, during the Song dynasty. While pregnant, Lady Zhou dreamed one night of red clouds swirling around her body, and she awoke startled from the vision. From childhood, Chuyi was exceptionally intelligent. One day while wandering in the mountains, he encountered an old man seated upon a great stone, who said to him, *"In the future, you will rise to fame at the imperial court and become the patriarch of the Daoist teachings."* He then stroked Chuyi’s head and departed. Chuyi once composed a verse and sang it to himself: *"Why contend for fame?"*

Why contend for profit?
Better to hear early and cultivate the heart-ground.
One’s own cultivation and realization shape one’s own future;
if one does not act, one becomes like the common herd.”

In the eighth year of the Dading era, she met the Patriarch Chongyang at Quanzhen Hermitage and requested to become his disciple. She served her mother while cultivating the Dao together, each receiving the Great Way. Though their family was poor and their means meager, they endured hardship with steadfast resolve.

Later, when Chuyi answered the summons to court, his memorial included these words: *“A mirror, though bright, can still reflect things—how much more the mirror of Heaven and Earth, which illuminates all without fail? What can escape it? This mirror of Heaven and Earth is none other than the wondrous clarity of one’s own spirit.”* The imperial response praised him highly, and the ancestral master sighed, saying, *“With purity and brightness in one’s person and a will as keen as the spirits—this describes the Master indeed.”*

The following year, when his mother turned ninety, he submitted a petition to attend to her for a day. Lady Zhou said to Chuyi, *“My time of return has come,”* and left behind words on *“neither clinging to life nor fearing death”* before passing. After her burial, he told his disciples, *“The Perfected Ones have made an appointment—I am leaving.”* He then bathed, burned incense, and ascended.

The secret of the feminine Dao must be sought in stillness;
within stillness, the dynamic mechanism remains.
If you merely sit in emptiness, clinging to withered thoughts,
how can the tiger be subdued or the dragon tamed for the elixir’s casting?

A speck of numinous terrace rests firm as bedrock,
heedless of the thousand shifts of glory and decline.
The yang radiance is itself the mani-jewel—
within this, gather and refine the Great Elixir.

The heart is like an autumn stretch of Caoxi’s stream;
best to devote effort to the midnight and noon practice.
Though fish and dragons thrash, the waves return to calm—
only the moon’s reflection lingers in the vast sky.

Light mist and thin haze veil the empty void,
driving the numinous clarity to find no dwelling.
Love and hate, glory and ruin—all are sharp blades;
if you wound the child, how can you seek the child?

First, one must inquire at the Moon Grotto of life and destiny;
seize the dragon and bind the tiger—do not delay.
When the time of yang’s arising leaks true yang,
how can a grain of millet be preserved whole?

*Later Chapters of the Anthology of Feminine Elixir Poems*
Recorded by Jishan Hall of Accumulated Virtue in Qianzhong

The White Lotus Perfected One

Though the terrain of Golden Elixir differs for men and women,
yin and yang share one principle in truth.
Purity of heart and few desires form the root—
to lay the foundation, first sever the Crimson Dragon.

Though the terrain of Golden Elixir differs for men and women,
(what men observe in their terrain differs from what women observe at the outset),
yin and yang share one principle in truth,
(for both must refine themselves into pure yang—essentially, the principle is one and the same).
Purity of heart and few desires form the root—
to lay the foundation, first sever the Crimson Dragon.
(The Crimson Dragon is the monthly flow.
When the flow ceases through refinement, and the breasts retract like a man’s withdrawn turtle,
only then is the foundation of the Elixir complete.)

Born in stillness, seek within stillness—
with single-minded steaming, mend the broken boat.
Blood transforms into qi, the tide of menses ceases,
and the primordial unity returns to ascend Penglai Isle.

Born in stillness, seek within stillness,
(for women are born with an inherently still nature,
and thus succeed in refining the Elixir more swiftly than men within stillness).
With single-minded steaming, mend the broken boat—
blood transforms into qi, the tide of menses ceases,
(Women abound in blood; through method, it is entirely transformed into qi,
and the monthly flow may be halted).
The primordial unity returns to ascend Penglai Isle.
(To restore one’s original nature is to become immortal or Buddha.)

When the aperture of emotion opens like a split melon,
rely wholly on the virtue of earth to grow the Yellow Sprout.
Morning and night, diligently nurture it—
naturally, you will attain the flower of ageless life.

When the aperture of emotion opens like a split melon,
(when the water of Heavenly Dew arrives, like a melon splitting open),
rely wholly on the virtue of earth to grow the Yellow Sprout.
(The sprout grows within the five soils, hence it is called the "Yellow Sprout.")
Morning and night, diligently nurture it—
naturally, you will attain the flower of ageless life.
(By nurturing the Yellow Sprout without harm, it gradually blossoms,
becoming the foundation for transcending kalpas and attaining longevity.)

The Fragrant, Leisurely, Numinous, True Buddha

Within the body lies the White Sparrow Temple—
learn Guanyin’s method of refinement.
One day, with merit perfected, pay homage to the Queen Mother,
and all shall feast together at the Peach Banquet.

Within the body lies the White Sparrow Temple—
(referring to the breasts, transformed from red to white,
hence likened to the White Sparrow Temple.)
Learn Guanyin’s method of refinement—
(once, the holy mother Guanyin refined her practice in White Sparrow Temple
and ultimately attained the great Dao.)
One day, with merit perfected, pay homage to the Queen Mother,
and all shall feast together at the Peach Banquet.
(may all ascend the other shore, a universal compassion.)

Quanzhen One-Stroke Buddha

Women, busy yet let worldly dust grow faint,
recite Amitābha Buddha in the heart.
Never apart from the Yellow Maiden, sit and lie together—
the mortal husk will naturally shed, exchanging for immortal bones.

Women, busy yet let worldly dust grow faint,
recite Amitābha Buddha in the heart—
(constantly reciting these four characters is the method to tether the mind.)
Never apart from the Yellow Maiden, sit and lie together—
(guard the true intention at all times, and merit may swiftly ripen.)
The mortal husk will naturally shed, exchanging for immortal bones.
(with such wondrous benefits, how can one not cultivate?)

Wujī True Person

Though undead, learn death to never die,
meet birth with slaying—never to be born.
Neither dead nor born, the True Person’s wonder,
one attestation of Nirvāṇa, the great achievement complete.

Though undead, learn death to never die,
(though the person is not yet dead, act as one already dead—
thus in the end attaining deathlessness.)
Meet birth with slaying—never to be born,
(when the mind gives rise to birth, slay it with the sword of wisdom—
thus in the end attaining non-birth.)
Neither dead nor born, the True Person’s wonder,
one attestation of Nirvāṇa, the great achievement complete.
(When form does not die and mind does not arise,
one may attest to the supreme fruit of Nirvāṇa—how great the achievement!)

Above "Right" True One, reverently annotated by Ancient Mother
Compassionate Ancient Mother

Turn the light inward, reflect between the breasts,
cultivate spirit, focus before the golden lock.
After two and a half days, yellow transforms to white,
shift the cauldron, change the furnace—refine and become immortal.

Turn the light inward, reflect between the breasts,
cultivate spirit, focus before the golden lock,
(gaze between the two breasts, direct spirit below the two kidneys—
naturally, the river chariot ascends to the central field to mend the broken body.)
After two and a half days, yellow transforms to white,
(after two and a half days of practice, blood turns to yellow—
at this time, purify the mind and apply effort.
Soon it turns white, the red vessels severed, returning to a maiden’s body.)
Shift the cauldron, change the furnace—refine and become immortal.
(When the red dragon is slain, the place of practice differs—
with the proper method, refinement leads to attainment.)

Língzhēn Tàihuà Buddha

Compassion as the root, the heart is the womb—
at home or leaving home, nurture great harmony.
Every person possesses the bodhi tree;
long seated in bodhi, ascend to the Great Luo.

Compassion as the root, the heart is the womb—
at home or leaving home, nurture great harmony.
*(Whether a laywoman, nun, or Daoist adept, all may cultivate.
Above all, let compassion be the foundation.)*
Every person possesses the bodhi tree;
long seated in bodhi, ascend to the Great Luo.
*(Know where the bodhi tree resides—
cultivate upon it, and in time, bodhi will surely be attained.)*

Qīngyún True Person

The medicine refines within the fire,
the fire manifests within the medicine.
With fire but no medicine, the cauldron boils empty;
with medicine but no fire, it dissipates in the end.
When fire and medicine harmonize,
the golden elixir coalesces, entering the paradise of Làngyuàn.

The medicine refines within the fire—
*(The medicine is qi; the spirit is fire.
This is the spirit guiding qi.)*
The fire manifests within the medicine—
*(The spirit grows ever more numinous through qi.)*
With fire but no medicine, the cauldron boils empty—
*(Do not play with fire!)*
With medicine but no fire, it dissipates in the end—
*(Do not lack fire.)*
When fire and medicine harmonize,
the golden elixir coalesces, entering the paradise of Làngyuàn.
*(When spirit and qi interdepend, how could success not follow?)*

The Radiant and Greatly Compassionate Perfected One

In seclusion, chastity, and tranquility, she nurtures her nature and emotions;
In womanly virtue, she fulfills her power—no light matter.
Ever mindful of the breast and sea of blood,
She gathers her form to merge with the Great Yin.

In seclusion, chastity, and tranquility, she nurtures her nature and emotions;
In womanly virtue, she fulfills her power—no light matter.
*(The former line nurtures nature; the latter cultivates virtue—this is the work of inner and outer cultivation.)*
Ever mindful of the breast and sea of blood,
*(The initial practice.)*
She gathers her form to merge with the Great Yin.
*(The Great Yin’s method of gathering form, her breasts shrinking like a man’s.)*

The Immortal of Colored Clouds and Lapis Lazuli

Best of all is the Six-Character Scripture;
From front to back, it abides in the heart.
Within it lies concealed the River Chariot method—
Unknowingly, unconsciously, the true refinement is achieved.

Best of all is the Six-Character Scripture,
*(Namely, "Namo Amitābha.")*
From front to back, it abides in the heart,
*(The method of reciting the Buddha’s name: "Nan" at the navel, "Wu" at the Yang Pass, "A" at the Yin Kidney, "Mi" at the Mud Pill Palace, "Tuo" at the throat, and finally resting the character "Buddha" in the heart. This both collects the mind and harmonizes qi.)*
Within it lies concealed the River Chariot method,
*(By reciting thus at all times, the postnatal yang qi is charioted into the Central Field, naturally replenishing the prenatal.)*
Unknowingly, unconsciously, the true refinement is achieved.
*(This method is simple—women may recite it, and men may do so as well. The longer it is practiced, the greater its efficacy.)*

The Spirit-Perfect Ancient Mother
Holds two *ruyi* diagrams in her embrace,
Born with them, all people possess them alike.
If one truly attains *ruyi*,
Even the heavens above become attainable.

Holds two *ruyi* diagrams in her embrace,
*(These diagrams protect the heart and also connect it.)*
Born with them, all people possess them alike,
*(Regardless of gender, all have them.)*
If one truly attains *ruyi*,
*(The *ruyi* cloth—if one truly attains the Tathāgata’s *ruyi*, boundless佳境 will unfold within the heart.)*
Even the heavens above become attainable.
*(Not betraying the name of the *ruyi* diagram, one gains its true essence, and ascension may be expected any day.)*

The Great Compassion Pure-and-True Buddha
In movement and stillness, never depart from this,
Depart from this, and the path is lost.
The Yin of Kun transforms into the Yang of Qian,
Standing between heaven and earth, be not weak or timid.

In movement and stillness, never depart from this,
Depart from this, and the path is lost,
*(This refers to true intention. With sincerity at all times, the prenatal naturally returns. If intention strays even slightly, it is no longer the correct path.)*
The Yin of Kun transforms into the Yang of Qian,
*(Women are inherently Yin; only by transforming into Qian Yang can the body become light.)*
Standing between heaven and earth, be not weak or timid.
*(The Yin nature is often soft; one must have the aspiration to stand between heaven and earth, only then can there be hope for the Dao.)*

The Jade Perfected One of Wuji

Entering the gate of emptiness, all affairs cease—
Why still cling to the red dust in the heart’s core?
The ancient model of Gold Sand Buddha remains;
In silent sitting, why not emulate Chan meditation?

Entering the gate of emptiness, all affairs cease—
*(Nuns and Daoist sisters have already entered the gate of emptiness, yet their hearts still linger on the red dust—why?)*
Why still cling to the red dust in the heart’s core?
The ancient model of Gold Sand Buddha remains;
*(Gold Sand Buddha is the Great Being Guanyin. Why not follow this path to perfect enlightenment? This urges those who have left the household life to cultivate with urgency.)*
In silent sitting, why not emulate Chan meditation?

Awakened World Buddha

Most pitiable are those not yet deceased—
Vowing steadfastness like frost and ice, truly bitter and toilsome.
Unwavering integrity is the seed of immortals and Buddhas;
Hasten to cultivate, hasten to refine, and rise above the dusty world.

Most pitiable are those not yet deceased—
*(The living are called "not yet deceased" when their spouses have passed.)*
Vowing steadfastness like frost and ice, truly bitter and toilsome—
*(Widows who keep chastity, resolute as frost and ice, endure untold hardships!)*
Unwavering integrity is the seed of immortals and Buddhas;
Hasten to cultivate, hasten to refine, and rise above the dusty world.
*(Immortal maidens and female Buddhas all take steadfast integrity as their foundation. As a chaste widow, you are already the seed of immortals and Buddhas—what use is remaining in the dusty world? This urges chaste widows to cultivate with urgency.)*

Queen Mother selects and esteems the true maidens,
Form is emptiness—this insight is clear.
The primordial qi, undiminished and whole,
With the proper method, one refinement and merit is achieved.

Queen Mother selects and esteems the true maidens,
Form is emptiness—this insight is clear.
*(Those with profound karmic roots perceive that form is emptiness, emptiness is form, and thus cultivate into true maidens, so the Queen Mother particularly favors them.)*
The primordial qi, undiminished and whole,
*(No need to mend leaks,)*
With the proper method, one refinement and merit is achieved.
*(Attaining the precelestial method, one refinement suffices—this speaks of ease. This urges maiden girls to cultivate with urgency.)*

Merciful and Benevolent Mirror of Goodness Buddha

Do not say household affairs are too tangled to sever,
The Earth’s upright qi, once cultivated, is attained.
From nonbeing into being, nurture the spirit-embryo,
The lower field bears no fruit—only the middle field does.

Do not say household affairs are too tangled to sever,
The Earth’s upright qi, once cultivated, is attained.
*(All women possess the Earth’s upright qi; do not let worldly affairs delay your entire life.)*
From nonbeing into being, nurture the spirit-embryo,
The lower field bears no fruit—only the middle field does.
*(Fruit in the lower field is the mortal embryo; fruit in the middle field is the spirit-embryo. The spirit-embryo coalesces from one’s own spirit and qi, not from external sources—thus called "from nonbeing into being." This urges household women to cultivate with urgency.)*

Eternal Mother of Myriad Kalpas’ Spiritual Wholeness

The Four Great Sacred Mountains abound with female immortals,
Half through filial piety, half through chastity, half through Chan meditation.
All root in the mind-nature as their foundation;
When merit is complete and conduct perfected, they ride the jade lotus.

The Four Great Sacred Mountains abound with female immortals,
Half through filial piety, half through chastity, half through Chan meditation—
*(Some attain through filial devotion, some through chastity, some through Chan practice; though their paths differ, their destination is the same.)*
All root in the mind-nature as their foundation;
When merit is complete and conduct perfected, they ride the jade lotus.
*(The two characters "mind-nature" are the essence of elixir cultivation, indispensable to both men and women. This verse concludes the Female Elixir Canon.)*

Above inscribed by the Jade Maiden under the White Lotus Perfected One
*Western Pool Collection*
Composed by the Golden Mother of the Western Pool, the Youngest Perfected Lady Wang
Annotated by Lord Fu You, the Rejuvenating Master
Collated by Fu Jinquan of Jinxī

*Preface to the Western Pool Collection*
It is said that Qian’s strength governs heaven, while Kun’s compliance finds its master. The Way of nurturing life embraces the two qi in harmonious union; the harmony of Qian and Kun unites the Three Powers in shaping creation. Virtue, speech, work, and appearance—Kun’s Way is fulfilled in the virtues of gentleness, prudence, warmth, and softness, thus establishing the model for inner chambers.

Yet those born with innate spiritual essence and wisdom may still weave brocade in reverse script, only to incur resentment in waves of slander; or be buried beneath the green mound, their souls returning in vain to the moonlit night. Others, with mist-like temples and cloud-like tresses, remain mired in the bitter sea, weeping fragrant tears and lamenting powdered sorrows, cast into the fiery pit. The five-leaking body is but the karmic obstacle of past lives; failing to awaken the three causes only compounds present-life transgressions. Their fates—long or short, prosperous or destitute—cannot be enumerated; their forms—fair or plain, foolish or wise—are beyond description!

All this stems from the lack of a Kun transmission in the world, making it difficult for people to transcend the kalpas. Thus, a petition was made to the Supreme Venerable, who commanded the host of perfected beings to expound mind-nature through poetic verses, conveying awakening through paradoxical words. Knowing stillness, they feared falling into empty dullness, so they further transmitted true teachings to guide practitioners in beginning their practice.

Every word is jade nectar, none but the work of cultivating life and establishing destiny; every character is a golden needle, all the secrets of binding tigers and leading dragons. If one can refine with sincere heart, the Dark Continent appears before the eyes; if one cultivates according to the method, crimson snow congeals within the cauldron. Rather than entangle oneself in the world’s net, clinging to this fleeting breath of splendor, how much better to sever the passions, seizing the eternal realm of the Dao!

The Western Pool offers a path—here lies the oar for crossing. I humbly append these words to serve as the wooden clapper, respectfully inscribed by Chongyangzi.

*Eighteen Poems on Nature Cultivation*
I.

When the moon is full, it shines bright upon the water,
Heaven and earth, vast and gleaming, all aglow.
A lone goose slants past, its shadow cast in the pool;
After a time, the bright moon mirrors the limpid waves.

Huichunzi says:
The subtle mechanism aligns—precious form gathers in perfect roundness.
The moon illumines the cold pool, its radiance shooting forth in all directions.
Only through clarity comes stillness; only through stillness comes illumination.
Silent illumination, perfectly penetrating—awakened spirit manifests of itself.
The wondrous meaning from the West is most great, most perfect,
Vivid and profound, like the pearl returned to the Eastern Sea.

Hmm!
The four seas surge, waters merging with the sky;
In the depths where water and heaven meet, one naturally finds the source.

Haichanzi says:
Delighted to find fellow practitioners annotating poems on nature,
The time when mind is illuminated and nature seen—when the Dao is accomplished.
Liu the Foolish arrives for the Dragon Flower Assembly,
Drunk, he reaches into the limpid pool to grasp the moon—too late!

Lingyangzi says:
On this night, joyfully encountering the opportune moment,
Clarity and stillness must be sought now.
The moon’s pure light imprints the deep pool—
Truly, the stars of heaven are inverted below.
Not clinging to strange forms or appearances,
How could one mistake empty realms for emptiness?
A single spirit pierces through, revealing the pearl before the self—
Fish eyes should know they are not the same.

Changchunzi says:
Mind and nature are not one thing;
Nature is seen within the mind.
Water and moon, both limpid—
The radiance of waves remains unstained.

---

II.
The spirit terrace is deep and vast as a limpid river,
Its distant source flows long—one should know this.
Let poison dragons contend and stir in play,
How could it compare to the filth churned in ditches and drains!

Huichunzi says:
Pure light is like a mirror, requiring no refinement.
With a single wipe, the poison dragon appears.

Hmm!
Nothing more to say.

The subtle essence from the West—
One sandal returns alone.

III.
Unstained by grinding, unsullied by dye,
Why covet favor or shun disgrace?
In stillness appears a perfect moon—
Only then does one trust this man is no fool.

Huichunzi says:
A single blow to the head—
Those who grasp it depart to comprehend,
Those who comprehend nod in assent,
Merging into silent extinction.

IV.
Neither hate evil nor boast of good,
Hold fast to my nature and let others be.
When the earth-shaking thunder manifests truth,
Freely roam the vastness of sea and sky.

Huichunzi says:
What is the Buddha’s Dharma?
A dried turd on a stick!
A single thunderclap—
Fearless even of death.

V.
Thick clouds and dense mist, both bleak and chill,
Veiling the original face of the bodhisattva.
Were it not for the cleansing sweep of pure wind,
How could the full moon’s radiance appear?

Huichunzi says:
A moment on the meditation mat,
In an instant—
A single somersault,
And the bodhisattva emerges.

VI.
Nature is like a limpid pool,
The mind like level earth.
Weeds arise—cut them away,
Wind passes—waves grow clear.

Huichunzi says:
Nature does not leave the mind,
The mind is empty, without thing.
Cutting away sprouting weeds—
