This is the lesser vehicle’s method.

VII.
A single point of luminous clarity, inherently pure and void,
Clouds come and go—the moon remains unchanged.
Responding to affairs is like light’s fleeting dimming,
Yet when the moon’s shadow fades, the bright pearl reappears.

Huichunzi says:
Not knowing how to engage in meditation,
How can one know how to venerate the Buddha?
A single fist shatters it—
The five fingers do not release.

VIII.

The mind is like a wild bird, most difficult to tame—
Scarcely freed from its cage, it seeks to be seized again.
Do not let it drift with the wind, south or north,
For it was born in chaos and falls into deep pits.

Huichunzi says:
Clearly a single moon,
Yet early on, it is mistaken for the sun.
Sun and moon illumine the virtue of heaven,
Mountains and rivers adorn the imperial capital.
Hmph!
Who understands?

IX.
A single point of luminous clarity, a single point of gold,
Drifting with the wind, lost in boundless depths.
Clearly there is a bodhi seed—
Yet when nature scatters and spirit dims, where can it be found?

Huichunzi says:
Dressing and eating,
Unmindful of warmth or fullness.
The mind departs, nature empties—
A lotus appears in the fire.

X.
Sorrow’s sprouts and passion’s seeds—both cast away,
Outwardly spring’s warmth, inwardly iron’s firmness.
Following death and defying life share the same principle,
Yet in movement and stillness, they are not the same.

Huichunzi says:
No harm in stepping into thorns,
Nor in turning within deep pools.
Fear only the clear wind and bright moon,
Sitting face-to-face with green mountains.

XI.
Human life, bustling like duckweed,
On the karmic sea’s waves—when will they cease?
To know one’s original true face,
Diligently knock on the true one beneath the moon.

Huichunzi says:
A single bowl, a single alms-bowl,
Everywhere a home.
Meeting an old Chan hand—
The staff is raised to strike.

XII.
The primordial qi of the undifferentiated was originally without form,
Yet between *geng* and *jia*, one becomes aware of shape.
Do not say being and nonbeing are hard to discern—
One must seek within oneself, not in others.

Huichunzi says:
A single mani pearl,
Unmatched in the sand-realm.
Presented by the dragon girl—
Only now is it recognized.
Hmph!

A single child with two mothers,
Four families as kin—none may be estranged.
Alas, it is lost!

XIII.
Know that the outer turbid holds an inner purity,
Dragon’s head and tiger’s tail arise in due season.
If one blends the mundane and sacred as one,
White snow and yellow sprouts will grow of themselves.

Huichunzi says:
What is the Dao?
Piss and shit must be let go.
Eating meals, donning clothes—
Utterly without distinction.

XIV.
The Great Dao first requires nurturing the numinous spirit,
When the numinous light is fully awakened, return to the root is easy.
Though essence, qi, and spirit may all be full,
How can one refine and temper in darkness?

Huichunzi says:
East, west, south, north, and the central land—
In the dust’s dark obscurity, hair turns white with ease.
Hmph!
Speaker, you’ve got it backwards!
Hard or not hard? A single somersault.
Easy or not easy? Force open both eyes.

XV.
Seal the mouth, still the spirit, and gaze inward,
The Dharma-body ever manifests at a hair’s tip.
In stillness, gather the treasure of numinous clarity,
Place it directly in the central palace—this is the elixir.

Huichunzi says:
Once grasped, seal the mouth.

If you still refuse to take up the task,
How can the bamboo rod bear to strike you away!
Hmph! Hmph! Hmph!
Come again and you’ll not be worth half a penny—
Go rest in the abbot’s quarters behind.

XVI.
The vast sky, clear and turning, remains unstained,
Clouds come and go, ever busy on their own.
The stirring of the xun-wind whirls up and down,
The light of nature and the treasure of life all return to the sack.

Huichunzi says:
A single cloth sack,
Unobstructed in what it holds.
Chaotic and undifferentiated,
It cannot be let out.

XVII.
Do not divide the world’s affairs into light and dark,
In leisure, seek the self that preceded your present form.
Ever wakeful, free from the ways of heat and cold,
Cast off life and death to attain the utmost truth.

Huichunzi says:
Above the nine heavens,
Below the nine springs.
Shaolin’s staff and fist,
Strike above and below.
Once struck open and through,
Roam freely as you will.

XVIII.
Fierce flames leap as the Azure Dragon dances,
Vast clear waves as the White Tiger crouches.
Tiger’s tail and dragon’s head bound by a silken cord,
Seized and returned to the spirit-chamber to merge with the true source.

Huichunzi says:
Long parted from home,
The Way is long and obstructed.
Now that you’ve returned at last,
You find father and mother, wife and children—each safe and well.
Hmph!
The thousand-year-old marble pillar stands unchanged,
While mulberry fields transform into seas at will.

*Postscript to the Western Pool Collection*
The *Yi* says: “Supreme is the power of Kun! All things rely on it for birth.” Is this not what it means to follow and receive from Qian? Yet among the women of the world, few truly understand the Way of Kun and embody its virtue. Some are dull and stubborn in their habits, others indulge in extravagance or violence as if it were their nature—countless delusions, impossible to enumerate. Even among the rare few with resolve who seek to escape life and death, their nature and life remain obscure. Often, one sees village witches and rustic maidens reciting a few Zen phrases, prattling of past causes and future effects, and taking this to be the Great Dao. Blindly cultivating, blindly refining, they deceive themselves and mislead others. Alas! This is all because the true oral instructions for women’s cultivation have been lost. Now, the *Western Pool Collection* emerges, revealing secrets untransmitted for millennia. With great compassion, it opens the gate of expedient means, wishing that women under heaven will reverently believe, cultivate, and delve deeply into its study. Every word bears merit, every line holds a secret—do not lightly pass them by. Where brush and ink cannot convey, it depends entirely on sincerity in pursuing the Dao, that one may encounter a true person’s guidance. The essential first step is to collect the mind and nurture qi. If the mind is not collected, the root of nature is obscured; if qi is not nurtured, the stem of life is lost. Only through dual cultivation of nature and life is the Way of Kun complete. Readers of this collection, take care not to sit idly and miss this opportunity, lest you fail the author’s compassionate vow to deliver the world.

Respectfully inscribed by Lingyangzi.

*Women’s Golden Elixir*
Written by Zhong Zhenyizi
Preface

The Great Dao makes no distinction between men and women—both may attain completion. Thus, when a man completes the Dao, he becomes a Perfected One; when a woman completes the Dao, she becomes a Primordial Sovereign. From ancient times, alchemical texts have expounded in exhaustive detail the practices of cultivation for men, while the Way of cultivation for women is seldom addressed. Even when it is mentioned, only a glimpse is revealed, not out of disdain for women’s practice. The underlying assumption is that since human nature and life are the same, the methods must also be identical—speak of men’s cultivation, and women’s practice need not be separately explained. Yet they do not realize that men are yang without and yin within, while women are yin without and yang within. Their inherent natures differ, their physical forms are distinct, and though both share the same nature and life, their practices diverge significantly. As *Disputations on Cultivating the Real* states: “For men, the essential first step is refining qi; for women, it is refining the form.” Ancestor Xu said: “When a man completes his practice, he no longer leaks essence; when a woman completes hers, she no longer leaks menses.” Their initial barriers are entirely distinct. As for later stages—refining the self, obtaining the medicine, returning the elixir, warming and nurturing, coalescing the embryo, and projecting the spirit—though these align with men’s practices, the subtle details and sequences inevitably bear both great similarities and minor differences.

In the spring of the *renchen* year, a woman of the *kun* trigram came to seek the Dao. I instructed her to read many ancient texts to verify what she had been taught. Yet alchemical works addressing women’s cultivation are rare, making it difficult to examine these differences. Thus, undeterred by the transgression of revealing celestial secrets, I have set down in writing how their practices are the same, how they differ, and the step-by-step progression of each stage, offering a guide for those who seek the ford. May those who study these words find clarity in their meaning, lest they mistake fish eyes for pearls. Through deep understanding and diligent practice, may the golden crucible successfully refine the mercury, and the supreme Dao be attained. Then, at the assembly by the Jasper Pool, they shall not find it difficult to join the immortals in paying homage to Lord Mu and the Golden Mother together.

Written in the *zhonghe* month of the *renchen* year of the Guangxu reign of the Great Qing, by Zhong Zhenyizi at the Reclining Cloud Western Pavilion by Jade Belt Stream.

**Volume One: Admonitions and Resolutions**
**Establishing the Will to Enter the Dao**

Heaven is yang, Earth is yin; Qian is firm, Kun is yielding. Without yang, yin cannot grow; without yin, yang cannot be born. Only when firmness and softness attain their harmonious mean can water and fire achieve mutual completion. Yin and yang must have their counterparts, and from this all beings are conceived. Thus, both Qian and Kun are endowed with the primordial qi of true origin, and both men and women possess the indestructible body. Qian is called "great generation," through which one may attain right enlightenment in the Dao; Kun is called "vast generation," and may likewise realize the fruit of the Primordial Sovereign. If one claims that the yin of Kun cannot enter the immortal Dao, how then does the Queen Mother dwell eternally on Kunlun? Chang’e stole the elixir from the rafters and became the eternal Empress of the Moon Palace; the carefree scholar of Qiyuan read the books of Zhuangzi and styled himself the Exiled Immortal of Yaochi. The goddess of the Luo River and the shamans of antiquity have long been renowned; the spirit of Zigu and the consorts of the Xiang River remain celebrated even today. Their traces are recorded in historical texts and may certainly be verified. How then can one who embodies the virtue of Kun be incapable of attaining the Dao? The obstacle lies only in drowning in the sea of desire without awakening the resolve to enter the Way—how can one leap from the ford of delusion to become a cloud-ascending woman? By nature yin and yielding, women often see with narrow vision, unaware of the Four Virtues and ignorant of the Three Obediences. They fail to correct their faults when they should, and neglect to cultivate the field of blessings when they ought. Indulging in passion without restraint, they lose the true essence of their original nature, abandoning themselves to degradation. Little do they realize that the human world is like floating clouds, and the mundane realm is but a sea of karmic retribution. Rather than entering the wheel of rebirth, why not turn back and ascend to the other shore? Penglai’s path is indeed real, and lies before one’s eyes; the ladder to Jade Capital is not absent, but must be realized through the heart’s awakening. The Buddha is the mind, and the mind is the Buddha; immortals may emulate me, and I too may become an immortal. Mercury and lead, gold and cinnabar, are the precious rafts that ferry one across existence; the alchemical classics and Buddhist scriptures are the celestial ladders to the Dao. Yet the celestial mechanism is not lightly disclosed—teachings often conceal their beginnings and ends. Even if one recites the jade-like verses to the end, one must still seek teachers and companions, for each secret revealed leads to another, and each stage of progress opens another beyond. If one can truly sever worldly attachments, bow before a wise teacher to seek the mind’s method, what difficulty remains in stepping into the Jasper Palace, paying homage to the Golden Mother, and joining the ranks of immortals? One must establish resolve—let each exhort themselves.

**Twelve Precepts for Entering the Dao**
1. Guard against the ceaseless arising of deluded thoughts
2. Guard against indulgence in lust and desire
3. Guard against harshness, cruelty, and ruthlessness
4. Guard against vexation and anger
5. Guard against anxiety, fear, and dread
6. Guard against excessive and reckless gazing
7. Guard against excessive and reckless listening
8. Guard against excessive and reckless speech
9. Guard against miserliness and attachment to wealth
10. Guard against killing and harming living beings
11. Guard against immoderate consumption of meat and fish
12. Guard against disrespect for the Dao and one’s teacher

Though women ought to guard against more than twelve things, by observing these twelve precepts, they are not far from immortality.

**1. Guard against the ceaseless arising of deluded thoughts**
The mind’s issuances are called thoughts. When thoughts are upright, all actions are upright; when thoughts are awry, all deeds are awry. The mind is the lord of heaven, and thoughts are its servants—when the lord of heaven is at peace, the hundred limbs obey its commands. Deluded thoughts are all reckless and improper notions. The ancients said: “The thought of no-thought is the true thought—this is the genuine intent.” This genuine intent cannot be dispensed with, for only with it can one refine lead to seek mercury and concentrate the spirit to nurture the embryo. The alchemical classics say: “In walking, standing, sitting, and lying, never depart from this.” *This* is the genuine intent. How can it compare to these improper deluded thoughts, which arise in countless tangled forms beyond description? At first, they arise from nothing; then, they take shape in response to circumstances. One thought has not yet faded before another follows, linking this to that, and stirring the mind upon encountering objects. When thoughts turn to gratification, one cannot help but congratulate and content oneself; when they turn to disappointment, how can one bear the self-reproach and sorrow? Throughout the day, there is no respite, and when night falls, even in sleep, the spirit is not preserved. Since the thoughts that arise are deluded, all actions that follow are likewise deluded—how can such a deluded person speak of the immortal Dao? Even if these thoughts have not yet manifested in action, the divine eye, like lightning, sees all with unerring severity. Moreover, the cycle of birth and death for all beings is created by a single thought—the realm of infatuation is the very ground of rebirth. Alas! A single thought sows the seeds of endless rebirth—how can one not be cautious in what thoughts arise? As for the perfected, knowing that these rootless, baseless deluded thoughts destroy one’s nature and hasten one toward the six paths, they diligently examine and keenly observe: Where do these thoughts arise? Where do they vanish? And before they arise—what then?

Once arisen—what then? The moment this thought arises, raise the sword of wisdom to sever it, striving to pluck out its root so it does not stealthily sprout and grow in the hidden depths. Ensure this nature remains empty, still, and silent, all thoughts extinguished, only the solitary spirit abiding—free and untrammeled, vivid and unbound. Thus, with selfish desires wholly purified and the heavenly principle flowing freely, the immortal Dao is not far off. Therefore, those who enter the gate must first guard this critical juncture with vigilance, for only then are they resolute and steadfast, true vessels of the Dao.

**Second Precept: Against Lust and Sensual Indulgence**
Of all barriers humans find hardest to break, none surpasses the pass of lust. Why are humans born? Because this root of desire remains unsevered, and upon encountering sensual matters, the nature enters the bodily root of male and female, taking rebirth through conception. Why does one have a body? Because it begins with the lustful union of parents. Born from lust, its habitual defilement runs deep, and its root is not easily plucked. Moreover, what sustains human life is entirely dependent on this vital essence. Indulgence in lust injures the essence; when essence is harmed, the Gate of Yang weakens, and a hundred illnesses arise. The ancients said: *"Taking a hundred pills is not as good as sleeping alone for one night."* This is to cherish one’s vital essence. Preserving even a fraction of this essence adds years to one’s lifespan. When the foundation is firm and the Gate of Yang sealed, longevity is attained. Furthermore, women’s natures are easily swayed; once lustful affairs are indulged, the fire of desire burns the body, and passion becomes uncontrollable. Without a husband to fulfill her desires, she may engage in conduct that shames her integrity. Even if she does not lose her virtue, the mere stirring of lustful thoughts ignites a fire that consumes the entire body, and vital essence is no longer preserved within. In the past, when the eleventh disciple Wei studied the Dao, her master transformed into a handsome man who teased and tempted her in every way, even attempting to force himself upon her. Yet her resolve did not waver, and only then was she taught the immortal arts. Thus, those who fail to sever the root of lust often lose their connection to the Dao. Even if they chance upon the teachings, this path is practiced within the realm of form—how can they succeed? Even if they do, the embryo they cultivate is but a lustful one; when the spirit departs, it will inevitably see lustful unions and be reborn, all prior efforts wasted. Many celestial maidens banished to the mortal realm suffer this fate due to unresolved emotional ties—how can one not be vigilant? The method of guarding against this lies in *not thinking* and *not looking*. Not thinking prevents passion from arising in the first place; not looking severs the念 at the moment of encounter. Wenchang dijun said: *"Do not think of what you have not seen; do not be disordered by what you see; do not dwell on what you have seen."* This is truly a fine admonition against lust. Yet how can one stop at merely not thinking, not being disordered, or not dwelling? One must utterly eradicate the root of passion, seeing form as emptiness and emptiness as form, forgetting emotion in the face of circumstances, and being desireless amid desire. Only then can one speak of the immortal Dao. Those who cling to lustful desires while seeking the path of the perfected—if any have succeeded, I have never heard of it.

**Appendix: The Story of Wei the Eleventh Disciple**
Wei the Eleventh Disciple was a poor girl from Chang’an during the Song dynasty. She married a man from the same village, Deng, who loved gallantry and adventure. Despite her repeated admonitions, he turned against her. During the Zhenghe era, he joined the military at the frontier and was gone for a long time without any word. Her husband’s elder brother was of ill character and often teased her with lewd words, but Wei sternly rejected him. Remembering the Daoist nun Zhao, whom she had loved since childhood and who possessed arts that could be transmitted, she sought her out. The nun gladly took her into the convent and taught her the methods. One evening, the nun descended the mountain, leaving Wei alone in the convent, and warned her: *"Do not drink wine or indulge in lust."* Wei thought deeply: *How could such things exist in this mountain?* After the first watch of the night, a man climbed over the wall and entered. Wei quickly rose and questioned him, but he did not answer. He approached and embraced her; she refused. His advances grew more insistent, and Wei drew her sword to strike him. He countered with a sword of his own, sharp and swift. Realizing she was no match, Wei cast aside her sword and pleaded: *"My fate is thin, and I have long renounced worldly desires. How could I bear to corrupt myself? Moreover, my master has given strict warnings—I dare not transgress."* He pressed his sword to her neck, demanding compliance. Wei bared her neck to receive the blow. The man withdrew his sword, smiled, and said: *"I know your heart has not changed."* Looking closely, she saw it was the Daoist nun. From then on, the nun taught her all her arts.

**Third Precept: Against Cruelty, Violence, and Harshness**
Firmness is a virtue. Without it, one becomes weak and listless, achieving nothing. This quality of firmness is indispensable. Yet firmness without propriety degenerates into violence—self-willed, refusing to yield, heedless of right and wrong, unmoved by others’ words, disrespectful to parents-in-law, discordant with sisters-in-law, lacking reverence for her husband, tyrannical toward children and nephews, and harsh toward servants. Such is the way of the shrew. Thus, firmness followed by violence often leads to cruelty, mercilessness, and stinginess. A person must possess a heart of benevolence and compassion to cultivate a magnanimous capacity. The Holy Mother, Yuanjun, regards the world as one family and the Middle Kingdom as one person. All beings between heaven and earth—every kind of creature—she cherishes as tenderly as her own child. How could she ever harbor even a trace of malice or descend into cruelty? Guanyin Bodhisattva is called the *"Vessel of Compassion"* because her nature is formed of benevolence, universally aiding all beings, serving as the compassionate mother through eons. Thus, her divine spirit endures through the ages. If one is violent and harsh, they lose the yielding and gentle virtue of Kun’s Yin; if cruel and harsh, they lack Kun’s capacity for broad nurturance. What use does Heaven have for such a person? What benefit do they bring to the earth below? It would be better for them to die swiftly—how can they hope for longevity?

Only those whose nature is violent must be nurtured with gentleness and slowness, as mild as the spring breeze that delights all, never clashing with anything. Those who are cruel and harsh must be transformed with kindness and generosity, like the timely rain that nourishes all things, so that none fail to receive its grace. Outwardly manifesting thus, the inner nature becomes luminously free, vibrant and fluid, containing boundless vitality within. How then could Guanyin, the Compassionate Ferry, sit idly by without awaiting your arrival?

**Four: Guard Against the Afflictions of Resentment and Anger**
Resentment arises when one is vexed by labor and harbors ill will. In this fleeting life, who is without tasks? One must proceed slowly, neither calculating merit nor shunning toil, nor fearing weariness—only then can affairs be accomplished. If vexation breeds resentment, what in this world can ever be achieved? In cultivating one’s nature, patience is even more essential. For the heart, long scattered, once reined in like a living dragon or tiger, refuses to submit at once. When it resists submission, one must nurture it with ease, neither restraining nor neglecting, neither forgetting nor forcing, allowing it to follow its natural course. Cultivate one degree today and gain one degree; cultivate another tomorrow and gain another. When cultivation reaches its fullness, clarity will naturally arise. If one grows resentful at the difficulty of perfection, how can the nature ever attain luminous wholeness?

Anger arises when things fail to conform to one’s wishes, breeding hatred and rage. Yet how can all things in the world always align with one’s desires? Anger does not make them so—why not reflect more deeply? One must simply accept what comes. Moreover, those consumed by anger harbor a poisonous heart; many die and are reborn in the bellies of venomous snakes. In summer, Xi La transformed into a serpent through anger, and Empress Chi became a python—these are clear proofs. Anger may arise from resentment, and resentment from anger; both are burdens on the heart. One must swiftly guard against them.

**Five: Guard Against Anxiety, Overthinking, Fright, and Fear**
Overthinking injures the spleen, causing qi to stagnate and fail to rise. Fright injures the kidneys, scattering the spirit so it cannot be stored. Before matters arise, one frets endlessly; as they approach, one trembles without cease. These are the roots of obscured nature and the tools of depleted essence and scattered spirit. If unchecked, they often lead to internal injuries. The way to guard against them is to remain steadfast and empty, the heart unburdened by things. When anxiety comes, reflect on reason; if reason cannot dispel it, let it follow its course—what benefit is there in worrying?

The reason people harbor such fears is the dread of calamity befalling them. Yet if one lives uprightly, with nothing to shame heaven above or humanity below, having done no wrong to others, others will not harm them—what is there to fear? Even if misfortune strikes suddenly, life and death are fated; one must accept it with equanimity. The sage remains unshaken before a collapsing mountain, unafraid even with a blade at the neck—such is the power of a settled spirit. Long ago, when Wanqiu tested the Green Bird Master with the six senses—sight, sound, scent, taste, touch—and the seven emotions—joy, anger, sorrow, anxiety, grief, fear, and shock—two traces remained unresolved, and he barely attained the rank of earthly immortal. If scholars do not exert effort here, they remain mere mortals—how can they hope for accomplishment?

**Six: Guard Against Excessive Wandering Gaze**
The eyes are the chief of the six thieves. When they see form, the heart is ensnared, and the hun-soul leaks through the eyes, injuring the liver. The hun-soul rests in the liver at night and dwells in the eyes by day; a wandering gaze causes the hun-soul to leak, leading to excessive dreaming. The sage has no dreams, for they can withdraw their gaze inward, preventing the hun-soul from straying outward and the heart from being led astray by form. Thus, to gather the heart, one must first restrain the eyes. The method is to constantly return the gaze to the Mysterious Pass, keeping this luminous nature undimmed, nurturing the unshakable spirit. Naturally, the eyes will not wander, the heart will not stray, the hun-soul will not roam, and the spirit will not flee, allowing the primordial qi to return to the body.

**Seven: Guard Against Excessive Wandering Hearing**
The kidneys open at the ears; reckless listening stirs the heart, and essence leaks through the ears, injuring the kidneys. The kidneys are the true water of the primordial One, irrigating the body’s defensive and nourishing qi. Injury to the kidneys leads to emaciated skin, essence depletion, and deafness. If one can often concentrate the ears to listen inwardly, not only will the heart remain undisturbed by sound, but the heart will be nourished and essence will congeal in the kidneys—ascension to immortality is not far off.

**Eight: Guard Against Loquaciousness and Recklessness**
Words easily invite blame. Nan Rong was praised by the sage for reciting the *White Jade* thrice, for he knew the value of cautious speech. Loquaciousness fails to nurture the heart’s benevolence. When Sima Niu asked about benevolence, the sage advised him to be sparing in speech—restraining the external to cultivate the internal. Moreover, words are the voice of the heart; the heart stores the spirit. Excessive speech injures the spirit. If one can guard the mouth like a sealed vessel, the spirit will naturally abide in the heart. The nature will not be disturbed by words, and in social exchanges, one will not speak amiss.

**Nine: Guard Against Stinginess and Hoarding Wealth**
Wealth exists to serve the world; no one is without need of it. The key is not to be enslaved by it. If one is utterly stingy, refusing to part with even a penny, then even if gold is piled like mountains, can it buy back the five constants? Rather than hoarding wealth to enrich oneself and become a miser, is it not better to disperse it to accumulate merit and forge bonds with immortals on the Dao? All immortals and perfected beings have attained their state by broadly sowing fields of blessing. As the ancients said: *"If one is stingy with wealth and hoards treasures, ten thousand immortals will refuse to come."* The perfected regard wealth and honor as floating clouds, gold and jade as dung and dirt, unstained by even a speck of dust, unattached to even a single thing. Since this body is not truly one’s own, how much less so the wealth beyond it?

Thus, those who enter the Dao must break through this barrier all the more, lest they be deluded by it.

**Tenth Precept: Refrain from Killing Living Beings and Harming Life**
Heaven and Earth cherish life as their essence; the sages embrace all things with compassion. Heaven and Earth give life, yet I recklessly take it—this greatly disrupts the harmony of Heaven and Earth. The sages love all beings, yet I willfully harm them—this utterly betrays the benevolence of the sages. Moreover, all creatures share this same nature and life: they love life and fear death, just as we do. If I dread injury to my own life, how can I not fear the death of other living things? Extending my heart to others, there must be those who cannot bear to kill or harm. As the ancients said: *"If you wish to prolong life, you must release life."* Since I seek the Dao to attain longevity, I ought to extend my own heart to cherish the lives of all beings. Though it is said that the lives of creatures lie in human hands and that killing brings cruel retribution, saving an ant may lead to imperial honors, and freeing a sparrow may yield a luminous pearl. From ancient times to the present, many have gained blessings through releasing life. How can one fail to cultivate compassion toward all beings to nurture the virtue of cherishing life?

**Eleventh Precept: Refrain from Excess in Meat and Fish**
Though meat and fish nourish the human body, those who observe fasting and abstain from such flavors have never been harmed—thus, sustaining life does not depend entirely on flesh. In high antiquity, people wore clothing of grass and ate the fruits of trees, and their people often lived long. Yet since the rise of cooked flesh and blood, people have tended to die young—why is this? For rich and fatty foods stir the fires of desire; consuming them excessively often leads to indulgence and depletion of essence. Grain, however, embodies the harmonious qi of Heaven and Earth, offering the purest and most wholesome flavor, making it the finest sustenance for nurturing life. Moreover, to eat the flesh of one’s kind to nourish one’s own flesh is something the heart cannot bear. The Buddhists abstain from meat and fish, for they do not indulge the palate at the cost of increasing the karma of killing. The Lady of South Marchmount warned Yang Xi: *"Those who cultivate the Dao should not wish to see blood and flesh; if they see it, they should avoid it—better still not to see it at all. Once dead, a creature becomes corpse qi, and contact with it most harms the Dao."* Thus, those who enter the Dao must value restraint in meat and fish.

**Twelfth Precept: Refrain from Disrespecting the Dao and Belittling One’s Teacher**

The most precious and revered in Heaven and Earth is none other than the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir. It can lift one from the mortal realm, resolve the cycle of life and death, and transcend nine generations of ancestors. For the ancients, it was an encounter once in ten thousand kalpas—how could this be a trivial matter? Even one as noble as Emperor Wen of Han humbled himself before He Shanggong; even one as wealthy as Ma Danyang emptied his heart before Wang Chongyang; even one as martial as Han Zhongli lowered his head before Wang Xuanfu; even one as learned as Baopuzi knelt before Deng Siyuan—how much more so for those beneath them? Thus, those who seek the Dao must not cling to their own wealth and status, flaunt their own power, or rely on their own learning and intelligence. They must approach with an empty heart and sincere seeking. Like Wu Chongxu, who swept floors and performed menial tasks, asking earnestly for twenty years before attaining it, the one who imparts it does not do so lightly.

Baopuzi said: *"Those who receive the true and singular secret must swear an oath, partaking in the blood of a white sacrificial animal. They receive it on an auspicious day, sealing the pact with white silver and white silk, splitting a golden contract. To speak of it lightly or transmit it recklessly is to forfeit the protection of the gods."*

Chongxuzi said: *"Since ancient times, the immortals and perfected ones have transmitted the true Dao only after purifying themselves through fasting and ritual offerings, presenting formal gifts of sincerity, swearing blood oaths to Heaven, and submitting memorials to the Supreme Emperor. They petition the Three Northern Dipper Stars, the Southern Asterism, the Three Officials, the Four Sages, the Five Emperors, and the Lords of Destiny, seeking their approval before transmission. Whenever one person is taught, all the gods and sages between Heaven and Earth are notified. If there are wicked sorts who act for profit or deceit, engaging in private transmission, both master and disciple will face divine punishment—how can one not be cautious? Since the celestial envoys do not bestow it lightly, how can the recipient treat it with irreverence?"*

Baopuzi said: *"The grace of a true teacher surpasses that of Heaven and Earth, outweighs that of one’s parents—how can one not revere and honor them?"* From this, it is clear that those who seek the Dao, upon encountering a true teacher, must show reverence and respect, inquire diligently and earnestly, and persevere without slackening. Only then can they ultimately hear the heart-method and find the path to ascension.

**Oral Instructions from the Lower Scroll of the Female Golden Elixir**

**Gathering the Mind**
The principles of the Golden Elixir are most profound and subtle;
Each step of the practice must be pursued with intent.
If you ask where to begin,
Sweep away deluded thoughts to gather the mind.

The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir is most sacred, most divine, most mysterious, most wondrous. It encompasses both inner and outer, beginning and end. One must understand its subtle details and practice with clarity. If one does not apply oneself with intent, knowing the inner but not the outer, one will ultimately fall into emptiness; knowing the beginning but not the end, one will achieve only minor fruition. The first step in cultivation is to gather the scattered mind.

Mencius said: *"The way of learning and inquiry is nothing other than seeking the lost mind."* If this mind is daily scattered outward, like a wild horse or a mountain ape, never at rest, and if it daily clings to emotions and selfish desires, the true nature will be increasingly obscured by material cravings. Once the root of equilibrium is disrupted, the nocturnal qi will not endure—how then can one hope to conceive the Embryo Immortal?

Thus, at the outset, one must lay down all entanglements, free from the slightest attachment or defilement, releasing the mind into the realm of nothingness. In utter clarity and stillness, emptiness and silence, with long and steady practice, the mind will naturally die, and with the mind’s death, the spirit will live. Even if one does not ascend as an immortal, immortality is not far off.

**Nurturing Nature**
A pearl of *maṇi* like crystal,
Who would expect dust and grime to obscure its brilliance?
But with quiet sitting and turning the light inward,
The moon of the celestial heart shines bright once more.

The true nature is inherently luminous and clear, yet constrained and obscured by the endowment of qi and the desires of the material world, it may at times grow dim. However, its fundamental brightness never ceases. If one diligently polishes and nurtures it, it will once again shine like the moon of the celestial heart, illuminating all lands.

The method of nurturing: each day, sit in stillness, first laying down all entanglements. Turn the light inward and reflect, like the moon reaching the heart of the sky or the wind rippling the water’s surface—serene and buoyant, lively yet formless, neither forgetting nor forcing. When matters arise or things approach, remain unmoving yet fully aware. To lack awareness would be to fall into the dullness of wood or stone!

In reflection, one must be at ease and comfortable; if not at ease, the method is not yet mastered, and one should not continue reflecting, lest it lead to qi stagnation. As the ancients said: *"At first, one must both release and gather; later, with mastery, there is neither release nor gathering."* When gathering and releasing are balanced, with long and steady nurturing, one may suddenly perceive a flash like lightning—this is the first sign of the true nature’s appearance. From there, with continued practice, there will come a time of perfect clarity. However, the work of reflection must not be prolonged.

The ancients said: *"Before obtaining the Great Medicine, one must not reflect for too long, lest the yin spirit emerge and enter demonic paths."* Thus, within the twelve hours of the day, one reflects for one hour, while the rest of the time remains carefree and at ease. The practice must never be interrupted. With mastery, the natural light of wisdom will spontaneously manifest, illuminating the nine provinces and touching the future with insight. Yet one must know and then forget what is known, forgetting until there is nothing left to forget, naturally attaining the state of transformation.

**Nurturing Qi**
Though one may hold the thought of the first dhyāna,
Only when the breath reaches harmony is Heaven revealed.
There is no other art to nurturing qi—
Walk together, sit together, sleep side by side.

The Buddha said: *"In the first dhyāna, thought is stilled, and the mind is free from arising and ceasing. In the second dhyāna, the breath is stilled, and there is no inhalation or exhalation."* When the breath is stilled, qi is properly nurtured.

This breath is not the breath of mouth and nose. At the beginning of life, one relies on the mother’s breath to form the embryo. Upon breaking the womb, this breath is stored in the ancestral aperture, though it remains connected to the breath of mouth and nose.

The breath of ordinary people enters and exits through the mouth and nose, unable to enter the ancestral aperture and return to the root. The true person, however, breathes such that each breath returns to the root. Thus Zhuangzi said: *"The breath of the multitude reaches only the throat, while the breath of the true person reaches the heels."* To seek the breath of the true person, one must regulate the postnatal breath to find the true breath, returning it to the root in the ancestral aperture. There, qi is stored, and when the breath is harmonized, qi is balanced; when the breath is stilled, qi does not scatter. This is what the ancient immortals meant by *"qi returning to the primordial sea, life knows no end."*

Yet the breath must be regulated to the point of harmony before one may gather the medicine or nurture the embryo. Hence it is said: *"Only when harmony is attained does Heaven appear."* Liu Changsheng said: *"Harmony unites Kan and Li."* He also said: *"Harmony nurtures spirit and qi."* What is harmony? Master Wu said: *"Neither partial nor biased, neither hasty nor slow, neither existent nor nonexistent."* How does one cultivate harmony? Master Wu further said: *"Husband and wife walk side by side, yin and yang unite as one. By day, they move together without one leading or lagging; by night, they dwell together without clinging or parting."* Such realization is the samādhi of harmony.

In short, Master Wu’s words are but a metaphor for the interdependence of mind and breath, never parting day or night. Zhongli Quan said: *"When mind and breath depend on each other for long, the body is transformed and jade nectar flows."* Thus, when mind and breath are harmonized through interdependence, the work of nurturing qi is complete.

**Concentrating Spirit**
Spirit is the husband, breath the wife—
Let not cloud and mud keep them apart.
When the two unite and return to the root,
Together they ascend the moonlit ladder.

Spirit is fire; breath is wind. In refining the elixir, wind fans the fire, and fire refines essence. Wind and fire work as one, spirit and breath depend on each other, never parting—like husband and wife. Thus, one must concentrate spirit within the qi cavity, spirit embracing qi, intention binding breath, neither clinging nor separating, neither forgetting nor forcing. Only then is there true union, true communion.

Zhu Yuanyu said: *"To seek the primordial true seed, one must establish the root in chaos."* Chaos is the place of returning to the root and reviving life. Ancestor Hai Chan said: *"The sages of old revealed the secrets of the elixir platform; many times the spirit bird nested in the osmanthus branch."* Ancestor Jingyang said: *"I reveal my family’s tradition to you: the sun moves into the bright moon."* All these are metaphors for concentrating spirit within the qi cavity.

Ancestor Sanfeng said: *"In seated meditation, one must embrace qi with spirit and bind breath with intention, circulating gently within the cinnabar field, gathering without scattering. There, the inner qi and the incoming qi merge in the cinnabar field, growing day by day, filling the four limbs and flowing through the hundred channels. Breaking through the double pass of the spine, they ascend to the mud pill, enter the vermilion palace, descend the multi-storied tower. Spirit and qi guard each other, breath and breath depend on each other—then the river chariot’s path is open."* At this stage, half the effect of laying the foundation is achieved. From this, we know that the work of concentrating spirit is indispensable for both the firm and the gentle.

**Three Destinies of Women**
Women’s destinies originally number three—
Purple, white, and yellow light, unlike men’s.
In youth, above; in decline, middle; in completion, below—
One must deeply understand this path at the pass.

Women have three destinies: purple, white, and yellow. The yellow light is where the elixir is born in the cinnabar field. The white is where the embryo is nurtured in the womb. The purple is the sea of blood where blood is generated. The upper is the yang cavity, the middle is the yellow chamber, and the lower is the cinnabar field.

In youth, when the celestial water fills the measure of one catty, the true qi of the cinnabar field is abundant, ascending to generate blood in the blood origin. Yang at its extreme transforms into yin, and the turbid essence flows outward as menses—thus in youth, it comes from above. In decline, when the celestial water is exhausted, qi cannot ascend to generate blood, and with the waist and spine drained of blood, the menses cease—thus in decline, it comes from the middle.

To cultivate the qian body, one must move from the lower field to the upper yang cavity, steaming with the fire of spirit until the menses turn yellow, the yellow turns white, and the white dissolves into nothingness—then the form naturally conceals itself. Thus it is said: in completion, it comes from below. Unlike men, if one does not recognize this pass, the elixir will not form.

**Qi Cavity**
The qi cavity is none other than the breasts—
Do not mistakenly measure below the navel.
If one does not know where yin arises,
How can the poison dragon be made to hide?

The qi cavity is the blood origin, the breasts—one inch and three fen within, not the two breasts. In men, the destiny lies in the cinnabar field, so the lower field is the qi cavity. In women, it lies in the breasts, so the breasts are the qi cavity. Yang at its extreme transforms into yin, flowing outward from the qi cavity as yin blood. Thus, to sever the red dragon, one must work at the place where yin arises. With long practice, the form will naturally conceal itself. To point to the qi cavity one inch and three fen below a man’s navel is a mistake. Know the timing—

When flowers bloom beneath the moon’s gaze,
The ram butts against the fence in vain.
I urge you—when the time is ripe, delay no more,
Lest the red dragon emerge from the water’s edge.

*The Subtleties of the Primordial* states: "Before a girl is born, at the moment her parents unite in essence, the father’s essence arrives first, the mother’s blood follows after, and blood enfolds essence to form the female body. At the hour of her conception, she first receives one *liang* of the mother’s lead-qi, which generates the right kidney, then draws a thread upward to form the two eyes, and another downward to produce the golden elixir. From then on, for every twelve days, one *zhu* of *gui*-water is generated; for every one hundred eighty days, one *liang* of *gui*-water is generated. Thereafter, for every fifteen days, one *zhu* of *gui*-water is generated; for every year, one *liang* of *gui*-water is generated. By fourteen years of age, fourteen *liang* of *gui*-water have been produced in the sea of blood, which, together with the one *liang* carried from the womb, completes the full measure of one *jin*—three hundred eighty-four *zhu*—corresponding to the three hundred eighty-four degrees of the celestial sphere, the three hundred eighty-four days of the year, and the three hundred eighty-four lines of the *Book of Changes*, the numbers of Heaven and Earth. When yin reaches its extreme, yang arises; when *gui* is exhausted, lead appears. At twice seven, the celestial *gui* descends. At fourteen, the celestial *gui* descends, and thereafter, every twenty-six months and seven and a half days, one *liang* of *gui*-water is consumed, until at forty-nine it is entirely spent. From the age of twice seven, a woman’s menses flow once each month, circulating without cease, in perfect accord with the moon’s waxing and waning, never deviating from its rhythm. Should it stray even slightly, illness will arise. Thus, month after month the flowers bloom, and the menses flow in their season. The reason for this flow is that yang transforms into yin. Once yang has become yin, it cannot be circulated. If one recklessly attempts to circulate it, lives will be lost. One must begin practice before the ram butts against the fence, when the time is ripe and the work can commence."

*The Spiritual Mirror of Superior Medicines and Three Lives* states: "The monthly signal is not the arrival of the menses. The word *signal* is like a messenger arriving before the traveler returns home. On the day the signal arrives, she will know it—perhaps by pain in the waist or legs, discomfort in the head or eyes, or loss of appetite. This is the signal’s arrival, the formation of blood, which is qi. One must devote oneself to practice two and a half days before this time. Once the menses begin, the red dragon’s yin-essence cannot be grasped. Reckless circulation will cost lives. One must wait two and a half days after the menses have ceased, then test with white silk. When its color turns golden, the menses have ended. Proceed with the previous work to sever it."

**Severing the Dragon**
As yang transforms to yin and flows from the jade ravine,
The fire-wheel must be swiftly driven—do not delay.
Let the *xun*-wind blow upward to the crimson origin-palace,
And sever the red channel so it never flows again.

When yang is about to transform into yin—when the signal has arrived but the menses have not yet begun—hastily stir the *xun*-wind, drive the fire-wheel, and circulate from the cinnabar field upward to the crimson origin-palace to sever it.

*The Method of Refining Form in Great Yin* states: "At the outset, close the eyes and gather the spirit, resting fully to calm the heart and regulate the breath. Then concentrate the spirit and enter the qi cavity. Cross the hands to cradle the breasts, gently kneading them three hundred sixty times. Draw the qi upward from the lower cinnabar field with twenty-four subtle breaths. Then, still cradling the breasts, return to contemplation and regulate the breath. With long practice, the true breath will naturally come and go, opening and closing, nurturing the *yin* and *e* until spirit and qi are abundant, true yang flourishes, the menstrual water ceases, and the breasts shrink like a man’s. This is called severing the red dragon." After long practice, there is no need to cradle the breasts or draw the qi—simply concentrate the spirit in the qi cavity and turn the light inward. This is called establishing the gate of the *pin*.

When the true breath flows gently, emptiness reaches its depth and stillness its utmost. Yang-qi steams upward, the river chariot turns backward, ten thousand clusters of purple clouds gather at the jade palace, and a thousand channels and a hundred vessels plant seeds in the mud-pill. Such are the effects of severing the red dragon. Thus, for women, cultivation hinges on severing the red dragon.

**Concealing Form**
No demon is deadlier than this enchantress—
When the seventy-sevens are complete, life is spent.
But once the form is refined in Great Yin and concealed,
The purple palace is prepared to celebrate long life.

Master Xu said: "When a man cultivates to the point of no seminal leakage, when a woman cultivates to the point of no menstrual flow—this is the way. For a woman, the menses are the beginning of life. When the signal returns and the menses transform into qi, the breasts shrink like a man’s, and the flow ceases of itself. For a man, refining essence into qi causes the yin-root to shrink like a boy’s, and leakage ceases of itself. Only then can life be prolonged. If this demon is not severed, by seventy-sevens—forty-nine years—blood will be exhausted and the menses will end, leaving no vitality. When vitality is gone, life is spent."

*The Spiritual Mirror of Superior Medicines and Three Lives* states: "For women, blood is the kidney, an empty cavity. After forty-nine years, the waist dries, blood desiccates, and vitality is lost. Yet with long nurturance, the blood-origin is born anew, as in a maiden. This is the marvel of creating something from nothing."

When one perceives and possesses this, severing it transforms it at once, and life is born." How then to nurture it so that the blood-origin is reborn? It is nothing more than gathering the heart to cultivate nature, nurturing qi to congeal spirit. The Non-Dual Primordial Sovereign said:

"Originally it begins with non-doing,
Who could foresee its fall into the postnatal?
The moment a word leaves the mouth,
Three inches already seize control.
Moreover, troubled by worldly toil,
How can one endure the coils of illness?
When the child is plump, it nourishes the mother—
Do not say there is no turning back."

There are other supplementary practices, but I dare not lightly disclose them. This is what Sun Bu’er meant by: *"Bind the tiger and return it to the true cave; lead the dragon to sever the elixir of benefit."*

When a woman’s cultivation reaches the point where her menses no longer leak, her subsequent work in nature and life differs only slightly from that of men. The trouble lies in no one revealing the secret to unlock its mysteries!

**Seeking the Elixir**
Refining form into qi, laying the foundation—
The work of highest celestial immortals differs.
If you ask where the Golden Elixir truly lies,
It is the sun coming to illuminate the moon across the primordial vastness.
Only when the sun comes to illuminate the moon
can it radiate light for ten thousand ages.
Understand this principle, and the Dao of the elixir is not far.

**Refining the Self**
The living dragon and tiger battle the lotus chamber,
Most fear the wild monkey of the heart and galloping horse of the mind.
If refining the self is not yet mastered,
How can one sail across the sea and hoist the sail?

The Lüzu’s *Lyrics of Ascending the Toad Palace* says: *"To refine metal and wood, one must generate dragon and tiger."* Know that dragon and tiger are not external things—they are only "living" when they arise from within. When practicing the Dao, drive the living tiger to meet the living dragon, but beware the wild monkey of the heart running rampant and the galloping horse of the mind flaunting its power. Without taming them, how can one cross the sea and retrieve the scriptures? Thus, one must refine the self to perfection, so that spirit is whole, qi abundant, the seven emotions unmoved, the five thieves unchaotic, the six roots firmly stabilized, and both form and appearance forgotten. Only then can one enter the lotus chamber to seek the elixir.

*The True Principle of Celestial Immortals* states: *"Refining the self means: enduring hardship in what ought to be done is refining; mastering what ought to be done is refining; abstaining from what ought not to be done is refining; advancing with unyielding resolve to ensure success is refining; severing desire without lingering attachment is refining; prohibiting old habits without contamination is refining. The self refers to the true nature in stillness and the true intent in movement—another name for the primordial spirit."* As the ancients said: *"Before refining the returning elixir, first refine the nature; before cultivating the great medicine, first cultivate the heart."* This is the Dao of refining the self.

**Sequence and Reversal**
Following the sequence gives birth to humans; reversing it makes one immortal.
When Kun and Qian form *Tai*, this is the true principle.
Do not be alarmed if the elixir is hard to congeal at the furnace—
When you pull the goat cart, you will naturally see heaven.

The Non-Dual Primordial Sovereign said: *"Watch closely for the moment of intermingling; pay heed to the path of sequence and reversal."* The *Rootless Tree* says: *"Sequence makes one mortal; reversal makes one immortal—it all lies in the inversion between."* The Dao of the elixir employs reversal, thus the hexagram *Tai* has Kun above and Qian below, enabling heaven and earth to unite and the myriad things to communicate. Without pulling the goat cart in reverse, the host of yin will obstruct—how then can one dispel turbid yin to perceive the heart of heaven? Therefore, in immortal cultivation, whether man or woman, one need only understand sequence and reversal.

**The Elixir’s Birth**
In vagueness and obscurity, emotions seem dull,
Yet in harmony, the medicine is born.
When the cinnabar field gives its signal, the opportunity arrives—
Hasten to adjust the fire-work and harvest the jade mushroom.

This elixir is endowed by parents and stored in the qi cavity. In youth and prime, it possesses the mechanism to push outward and transform. Seizing this transforming mechanism and reversing it into the yellow center is called "the elixir." Yet when the elixir is born, there are signs to observe. The *Daodejing* says: *"Vague and indistinct, yet within it is substance; dim and obscure, yet within it is essence."* Niwan said: *"When spirit and essence merge in obscurity and qi returns, flesh and bone fuse without awareness."* Master Yin said: *"Suddenly, an itch arises in the pores, the limbs become soft as cotton, the heart feels vague and indistinct—this is the scene of the elixir’s birth."* Moreover, there are signals to hear. Shaozi said: *"Suddenly, in the dead of night, a thunderclap—thousands of gates and ten thousand doors open in succession."* Master Hunran said: *"When the time arrives and qi transforms, the mechanism stirs and the flute sings—this is the moment the signal arrives."* The moment the signal arrives is the living midnight. Upon hearing this signal, prepare swiftly and do not err in the fire-work. If you miss it in the moment, how can you harvest the jade mushroom in an instant?

**Harvesting the Medicine**
Suddenly beholding the primordial single grain of elixir,
Its light blazes like a golden pellet.
If the *Xun* wind does not blow the bellows,
Even with desire, crossing the pass would be difficult.

A single grain of Golden Elixir, round and glowing, shines brightly like a golden pellet. Without the *Xun* wind blowing the bellows, how can it pass the *Weilü*, ascend the double passes of the spine, reach Niwan, and enter the cinnabar field?

*The Mirror of Entering the Medicine* says: *"Rise with the *Xun* wind, move the Kun fire."* Xiao Zixu said: *"The bellows of Qian and Kun have their number."* The *Xun* wind refers to the qi of breathing. The bellows is the mechanism. Only with the qi of breathing can one activate this mechanism, causing the medicine to rise and descend, circulating to complete one celestial cycle. Liu Huayang said: *"The Dao of the Golden Elixir begins when yang is born: concentrate spirit into the qi cavity, stir the bellows’ *Xun* wind, and blow gently into the furnace with each breath, like a blacksmith pumping the bellows."* When wind arises, fire flames; when fire flames, essence transforms; when essence transforms, qi is born. Harvest this born qi, let it rise and descend in circulation—this is called a "celestial cycle."

**Rising to the Origin**
The sun rises in Fusang, east of the great sea,
The wheel of fire flies across—do not slacken your effort.
The deer cart carries it to the summit of Kunlun,
Where cinnabar mercury naturally returns to the chamber of spirit.

The red sun of Fusang emerges from the west to the east—this is the true medicine, like the radiant sun moving from east to west.

At this moment, swiftly mount the wheel of fire to cross the vast and surging sea, ascending from the *Weilü* through the twin passes of *Jiājí*. Master Pure Yang said: *"Sit in stillness from midnight to noon, and the twin passes of Jiājí will lead you past Kunlun."* He also said: *"Rely on the hours before midnight and after noon to see—one pulse of celestial fluid rests at the spine’s end."* Xiao Zixu remarked: *"How many times I’ve laughed and pointed to Kunlun’s path—clearly, a road runs through the twin passes."* Yet for the reverse course, only the deer cart can push it forward. To pull the deer cart is true intent. With this single point of true intent, one can surmount perilous obstacles, pass the twin passes, ascend Kunlun, descend the Magpie Bridge, and guide the living mercury into the golden crucible of the spirit chamber, there to become an eternal treasure of the body.

**Uniting the Elixir**
The *Gèn* boy first returns to the womb of primordial chaos,
From this moment, husband and wife weave their silken bonds.
Seal the six gates and diligently tend the fire,
Within the curtains, spring’s warmth must be harmonious.

The *Gèn* boy symbolizes the medicine arriving from the *Gèn* palace. The womb of primordial chaos is the cavity of ancestral *qi*. The medicine of the *Gèn* boy enters the cavity of ancestral *qi* from the northeast, uniting with true lead like a couple binding their silken threads. Yet at first, they do not congeal—one must tightly seal the six gates, regulate the breath in unbroken continuity, and steam with the fire of spirit until they merge as one. The fire for uniting the elixir must be a gentle warmth, unlike the fierce blaze of the harvesting phase. Only with perfect harmony and balance can one achieve the touch that brings spring to life.

**Fire Timing**
Though six hundred scrolls record the fire’s lore,
None truly write the timing on phoenix paper.
Clearest of all are Chongxu’s words:
*"Breath by breath, the immortal’s path is complete."*

Master Zixian said: *"Sages transmit the medicine but not the fire—few have ever known its timing."* Ten thousand elixir classics never commit it to writing. Yet Chongxu alone declared: *"Who says the fire timing cannot be passed down? Follow the moment’s flow, silently guiding it into the profound. Gazing back at the sages of old—breath by breath, the immortal’s path is complete."* Thus, the essence of fire timing must be sought in the true breath. For breath arises from the heart; when the heart is still, the breath is regulated, and each breath returns to its root—this is the mother of the Golden Elixir. Yet fire timing is not uniform; the ancients distinguished inner and outer. Outer fire timing involves action and effort, the work of laying the foundation. Inner fire timing comes after the elixir is obtained, a matter of natural non-action. Both hinge on the two words *breath* and *rest*: without regulating the outer fire’s breath, one cannot circulate the elixir; without regulating the inner fire’s breath, one cannot nurture it. Thus, Liu Huayang said: *"The fire of ordinary breath transforms the essence of food to aid primordial essence; the fire of spirit transforms primordial essence to aid primordial qi; the fire of primordial qi transforms breath to aid primordial spirit; and the fire of primordial spirit transforms form to return to emptiness and aid the Dao. From beginning to end, all depends on the power of fire to ascend to the Great Luo as an immortal. Yet one must know that fire and timing are never separate—fire must respond to timing, and when timing arrives, so does fire."* Thus, the two words *fire* and *timing* involve step-by-step procedures, which, without a master’s transmission, remain difficult to fully grasp. One must cultivate virtue and pledge to heaven to seek a teacher’s guidance.

**Nurturing**
Now behold the white snow sowing cinnabar sand,
Balance cold and warmth—take care not to err.
When the thirty-six palaces brim with spring’s fullness,
Naturally, a path opens for the immortal’s raft.

Once true lead is obtained as white snow and cinnabar sand, the living mercury must be tempered with balanced warmth. *Nurturing* means avoiding excess heat. If too cold, the water chills and the elixir fails to form; if too hot, the fire scorches and the elixir melts. Thus, *warm nurturing* is a patient, unhurried process of natural transformation—like heaven nurturing things with timely rain and sun, like a mother carrying a child with regulated rest and activity, like a dragon guarding its pearl in still hibernation, like a hen brooding her eggs with unbroken warmth. Before midnight, advance the fire; after noon, withdraw the tally. At other times, regulate the breath in unbroken continuity, as if present yet absent, banishing deluded thoughts to dwell in simplicity and silence. As the ancients said: *"Harvesting the medicine takes but a moment; uniting the mercury requires ten months."* And: *"After ten months, the embryo is complete, and the foundation of sagehood is laid."* Without skillful nurturing, how could the embryo reach perfection? The thirty-six palaces represent the three hundred and sixty degrees of the celestial cycle. After each full cycle, rest a while. The celestial cycle has its measure, and nurturing has its count—only when fully nurtured can the sacred embryo be formed. The details of its stages and numbers dare not be written on bamboo or silk, lest the celestial mechanism be revealed. Alas!

The immortal raft is not distant, the path to ascension lies open—only lament that people fail to cultivate virtue, ally with Heaven, or seek with empty heart and sincere devotion.

At this stage of *embryonic breathing*, take care not to grow careless. Let the spirit-breath flow ceaselessly, merging with the Great Void. One may ask: to what may the nurturing of the embryo be likened? It is as if returning to the state before father and mother were born.

After the elixir coalesces, spirit congeals with *qi*, and *qi* abides with spirit—like a woman with child, hence it is called the embryo. Yet there is no true embryo; it is the dwelling of spirit-breath. The embryo is the palace that shelters spirit; breath is the source that transforms the embryo. The embryo arises from breath, and breath abides through the embryo. Without breath, the embryo cannot form; without spirit, breath has no master. When spirit and breath depend upon each other, this is true *embryonic breathing*.

At this stage, take care not to grow careless. One must forget form and appearance, body merging with the Great Void, so that true breath comes and goes in unbroken continuity—seeming to exist yet not, as if forgotten yet present. Like fish in a spring pond, like insects in hibernation, exhalation reaches the root, inhalation reaches the stem, all day long in a state of undifferentiated oneness, as if still in the womb before birth. The Unparalleled Lord said: *"With every breath, return to the primordial Qian."* This is that.

When breath abides and the pulse stills, spirit settles. Only when spirit settles can one emerge from concentration, and the sacred embryo be perfected.

**Degrees of Practice**
Refining the elixir follows an order—*Kan* and *Li*, *Gen* and *Xun* reversed and inverted.
When the nine-nines of the celestial cycle are complete,
Who would fear failing to become a transcendent immortal?

The gentle practitioner’s path differs from the resolute one’s, yet success may come more easily. The resolute may subdue *qi* in three years; the gentle may achieve it in one, for the elixir already resides within. Yet though *qi* is more readily subdued, the red pulse remains the greatest threat to the Way. If a woman of true fortitude can sever the red pulse, her menses will cease, and the foundation will be laid.

After laying the foundation, refine essence into *qi*, dissolving the two *yang* of *Li* and the two *yin* of *Kan*, transforming them into the body of *Qian*. Though the *Kan-Li* work is complete and the *Qian* body formed, *qi* remains unrefined, spirit unenlightened. One must then refine *qi* into spirit, transmuting the single *yang* of *Gen* and the single *yin* of *Xun*, smelting away all external *yin* *qi* to forge pure *yang* spirit. When the celestial cycles reach their term and the nine-nines restore pure *yang*, one must then refine spirit to return to emptiness—only then does one become a transcendent immortal.

**Emergence from the Womb**
Seventy-six celestial cycles complete,
The *yang* spirit suddenly ascends to the upper field.
A thunderous roar shakes the Gate of Heaven,
Above the crown, white mist rises in contest.

When the work of the celestial cycle reaches seventy-six degrees, the sacred embryo is perfected. The *yang* spirit moves from the middle field to the upper field, breaking through the crown and emerging—this is called *emergence from the womb*. Zhongli Quan said: *"Thunder shakes the Gate of Heaven, startling gods and demons; the cosmos overturns, white clouds take flight."* The *True Transmission of the Golden Elixir* states: *"Suddenly, thunder resounds at the crown; in one’s arms, the infant is held."*

The reason spirit can emerge from the womb is because spirit has settled. Chongxu said: *"Initially, spirit cannot settle and relies on the two *qi* for stability. When *qi* settles, spirit follows and settles with it. When *qi* settles, it ceases to be *qi*; spirit, no longer dependent, stands alone—only then can it leave concentration and depart the body. Without attaining great stillness, spirit, still clinging to *qi*, cannot leave the body. Thus, when heart and breath depend upon each other, spirit settles and can emerge from concentration."*

The manifestations of emergence vary: some emerge amid a rain of heavenly flowers, others through a seven-tiered pagoda, or a body beyond the body. Some emerge with wind, clouds, and thunder—such was the case for Master Ma Danyang. Others emerge with fragrant winds and auspicious mists—such was the case for the Unparalleled Lord. In sum, spirit’s emergence arises from a single thought in deep stillness; its transformations and manifestations likewise arise from a single thought. Even a thousand, ten thousand, or myriad bodies arise from a single thought. One should not doubt the path because of differing manifestations. The "white mist rising" is but one example offered as evidence.

**Nurturing the Infant**
The newborn infant’s *qi* is not yet pure;
Still, it must swallow *wood mercury* to restore primordial truth.
After three years of nurturing,
It transforms, becomes numinous—truly spirit.

As the ancients said: *"The newborn infant is not yet fully formed; it must rely on the kindness of Mother Kun for nurture."* Only after three years of nursing can it transform and become numinous. Thus, it must still swallow *wood mercury* to restore primordial truth—not that three years are truly required, but rather three celestial cycles.

**Wall-Facing**
Though the elixir work is complete, there remains labor to be done.
With profound stillness, sit in meditation within the cave-heaven.

**Wall-Facing (continued)**
Nine years of forgetting form in samādhi complete,
Only when emptiness is shattered is true emptiness attained.
Wall-facing is the work of non-action.
The Non-Dual Primordial Sovereign said:
*"All affairs now done, I sit serene within my niche,
Light of body, I ride the purple qi,
Still of nature, I bathe in limpid pools.
Qi merges yin and yang as one,
Spirit unites heaven and earth as three.
Work complete, I ascend to the Jade Towers,
Letting forth a long whistle amid mist and clouds."*

Thus, when the nurturing work is fulfilled, one must choose a renowned mountain grotto or abode, sit in profound stillness, and face the wall to harmonize the spirit. Emerging, one takes the Great Void as the realm of transcendence; entering, one makes the Upper Cinnabar Field the dwelling of repose. Forgetting form, one enters samādhi to refine true emptiness—refining until emptiness itself is emptied, shattering the void so that spirit may return to the formless. This was Bodhidharma’s practice, requiring nine years of wall-facing.

**Ascension**
Refining to true emptiness, the Dao grows ever loftier;
A celestial writ descends, summoning one to tread the cloudy heavens.
Henceforth dwelling forever in the Jasper Pool Gardens,
Accompanying the spirit consorts to the early court.

The Non-Dual Primordial Sovereign said:
*"The auspicious hour arrives as I emerge from the valley,
A mere span away, I ascend to the divine clouds."*
And again:
*"One day, immortal and mortal part,
Cool and detached, I cross the sea’s tide."*

Once the return to the formless and ascent to the distant realms is at hand, a heavenly decree descends, cloud-cranes come to welcome, and one is summoned to the Jasper Pool for a celestial banquet, to attend the Golden Mother and receive the sacred charts. Jade towers and pearl palaces become one’s home; the Xiang River consorts and Jade Maidens, one’s companions. What joy could surpass this? The only regret is that the cosmos lacks resolute women—those who cannot stand beside Ma Gu. Yet if a woman is truly a hero among her sex, cultivating virtue to pledge herself to heaven, emptying her heart in sincere pursuit, she may walk this path—indeed, she may succeed even more readily than men. Let her but set her resolve and strive.

**Women’s Daoist Studies: A Small Collection**
*Preface to the Reprint of* Women’s Daoist Studies: A Small Collection

To propagate Daoist culture, excavate the treasures of Daoist scriptures, and provide theoretical guidance and practical verification for immortal Dao cultivation, we now reprint *Women’s Daoist Studies: A Small Collection*. Compiled by the advocate of immortal Dao, Chen Yingning, this work draws upon the physiological and psychological characteristics of women, adhering to the Daoist principle of seeking truth through practical realization. It gathers women’s cultivation methods from across the ages, meticulously selecting and editing them into five texts: *The Wondrous Scripture of Kun’s Peace*, *The Orthodox Methods of Women’s Practice*, *Ten Principles of Women’s Elixir*, *A Discourse on the Differences Between Men’s and Women’s Elixir Work*, and *A Collection of Women’s Elixir Poems*. This is one of the rarest specialized works on women’s cultivation.

Today, we reissue this book, consolidating it into two volumes without altering the original text, for the convenience of readers in perusal and preservation. Given current limitations in printing (and the lack of certain characters), we ask readers for their understanding and corrections.

**Fu Yuantian**
*August 25, 1994, at Feixian Temple, Mount Qingcheng*

**Editorial Principles of the *Women’s Daoist Studies Series***

1. Carefully selecting exemplary conduct and edifying words from women of past and present—concise works that cultivate lofty character and nurture exceptional wisdom in women—pertains to the psychological dimension.
2. Carefully selecting methods of cultivation, scriptures, treatises, and poems from women across the ages—works that foster extraordinary talent in women and strengthen the physical constitution of the fairer sex—pertains to the physiological dimension.
3. Daoist studies are the quintessence of the Chinese nation, unique to our land and absent in other countries. Previously, due to the lack of widespread education for women, this transcendent discipline remained inaccessible. Now, in accordance with the principle of equality between men and women, we gradually make this knowledge public.
4. Ancient writings vary in depth; some are profoundly abstruse and difficult to comprehend, while others are overly vulgar and uninspiring. To ensure accessibility, we annotate the abstruse passages to aid understanding and refine the vulgar ones to avoid offending refined sensibilities.
5. The editorial aim of this series is to promote China’s indigenous Daoist tradition while resolutely eliminating superstition. Any text tainted by superstition is excluded.

---

**Preface to the Collated *Wondrous Scripture of Kun’s Peace***

The origins of Daoist studies are lost to time. The earliest recorded instance appears in the teachings of Guangchengzi to the Yellow Emperor, dating back over 4,600 years. Though transmitted unbroken through the ages, it has occasionally faced suppression by narrow-minded Confucians and marginalization by Buddhist partisans. On the surface, it seemed to fade into obscurity, yet hidden in mountain forests and among wandering adepts, its secret oral transmission endured for millennia, unchanged. These masters vowed never to commit their teachings to writing, leaving outsiders unaware of their profound depths. Thus, while Confucian and Buddhist canons fill libraries, authentic Daoist texts remain as rare as morning stars—women’s Daoist scriptures even more so.

Over twenty years ago, I resolved to circulate esoteric alchemical texts but was hindered by unfavorable circumstances. Years passed with little progress. Now, fortunately, I have met Zhang Zhuming, master of Yihua Hall, a kindred spirit. After mutual consultation, we have compiled the *Women’s Daoist Studies Series*, with the *Wondrous Scripture of Kun’s Peace* as its first publication. We gathered six versions—printed editions, hand-copied manuscripts, and family-held texts—each differing in phrasing and riddled with errors. Comparing them, we selected the finest readings, revising and polishing the text from beginning to end until it took on a new form. Though not yet perfect, what remains are but minor flaws in an otherwise refined work. Eager to meet the needs of the world, we publish it now. Should we later obtain rare manuscripts, we will undertake another round of collation to achieve perfection—this we leave to future efforts.

*Written in January of the 24th year of the Republic of China (4,631st year of the Yellow Emperor’s reign) by Chen Yingning of Wanjiang, at Hongdao Studio in Shanghai*

---

**Guidelines for Reading the Scripture**

1. Women who seek the Dao often struggle without texts to study or scriptures to expound. Even those earnest in their pursuit, unable to find the proper path, frequently stray into error. Once the cause is misplaced, the result is entirely lost—a lamentable outcome. With this scripture’s publication, if those well-versed in profound principles thoroughly study its text, delve deeply into its meaning, adapt teachings to the times, and expound them skillfully, allowing all to hear and harmonize understanding with practice, it may well herald a dawn for women’s Daoist cultivation.
2. This scripture first expounds the principles of creation and human life, then the cultivation of women’s virtue, followed by the roots of body, mind, nature, and destiny. It reveals the secrets of the Golden Elixir and Jade Crucible, guiding practitioners to awaken the heart, realize truth, and ascend together to the ranks of immortals. It encompasses all fundamental methods of Kun’s cultivation, addressing the psychological and physiological needs of women. If expounded skillfully, its effects will be profound.
3. When Buddhist masters lecture on scriptures, laywomen often mingle among monks, nuns, and lay devotees. They join in palms, bows, chants, and even drowsy nods—mirroring the crowd in every gesture. Occasionally startled awake, they feign attentiveness, their discomfort plain. Yet when the lecture ends and they are asked about the scripture’s meaning, they respond with blank stares, offering only, *"I don’t understand."* To claim such listening yields benefit is self-deception. The reasons for their incomprehension are twofold: first, the scripture’s teachings fail to resonate with women’s sensibilities, feeling alien and unrelatable; second, the lecturer’s monotonous and dull delivery fails to engage the audience, lulling the entire hall into a hypnotic state. Thus, Buddhist scripture lectures become mere ritual, impressive in appearance but hollow in substance.

**IV.** To lecture on the *Kun Ning Jing*, one must devise ways to avoid the second pitfall of Buddhist scripture lectures. Within the lecture hall, the temperature should be comfortable, and the air should circulate freely. All rituals and arrangements may be adjusted flexibly according to time and place, without rigid adherence. If financial constraints exist, burning a small amount of fine incense suffices; other decorations should be kept simple. Most crucially, the audience must be kept calm and focused, never allowed to drift into drowsiness.

**V.** The lecturer’s qualifications must include: (1) mastery of the philosophical principles of the Supreme Ultimate, yin-yang, the Five Phases, and the Eight Trigrams; (2) knowledge of the histories of eminent women in ancient China; (3) understanding of the roots of body, mind, nature, and destiny; and (4) clarity on the methods of *nüdan* (women’s internal alchemy) cultivation. Lacking any one of these four fields of learning, one cannot properly explicate this scripture.

**VI.** Each lecture should last no more than one hour, lest the audience forget what they have heard. Fortunately, the text is concise, comprising eighteen chapters in total. One chapter may be covered per session, completing the scripture in eighteen lectures; or two sessions per chapter, finishing in thirty-six. Whether held daily, every two or three days, or weekly, the schedule may be decided as circumstances allow.
