The *Tuan* says: “Supreme is the Kun’s primal power—all things draw life from it.” Kun belongs to old yin; when yin reaches its extreme, yang is born. In following Heaven, it gives life to humans and creatures; in following the self, it completes the Dao and attains perfection.

Xiang Yi, the Ancient Mother, says:
In all cases, men’s cultivation begins with the initial work of refining and laying the foundation. For women, however, cultivation differs from that of men. In men, yang descends and is discharged; in women, yang ascends. Men’s bodies are firm; women’s bodies are soft. Men use the yang essence of the elixir field, constantly preserving it to prevent external leakage. When accumulated over time and refined with fire, essence transforms into qi, qi into spirit, and spirit into emptiness. Progressing gradually, the work is completed, the Dao attained, and ascension achieved. Women, however, are different. Their bodies are inherently turbid yin, vessels of blood and fluid. They employ the numinous fat of the breasts to transform their temperament. Through prolonged refinement, the red naturally reverts to white, and blood transforms into qi. Once blood has become qi, fire talismans are used to further refine it, enabling qi to revert to pure yang, completing the Dao and returning to the true. In their initial work, women first refine the physical form, then the original essence. This is unlike men’s work, which first refines the original essence before refining the form. Their bodies differ, and thus their practices naturally diverge. Without distinguishing these paths and establishing separate teachings, how can the transformation of yin and yang be achieved, and men and women together attain completion?

Yet if the body is already one’s own, why refine it? Women’s bodies are originally turbid yin, unlike men’s, which inherently possess firm yang. Without refinement, blood cannot transform into qi, nor can the prenatal be conceived or true qi produced. Without true qi, one remains a mass of pure yin—how then can the returned elixir be obtained to accomplish the great Dao? Thus, a woman’s form must first be refined before further progress is possible.

The path of female immortals fundamentally differs from the work of men. Men’s path values the refinement of the medicine; thus, the earlier stages of practice are explained step by step. If diligently practiced day and night with sincere devotion, the blood of the five viscera within the body will all revert to qi, naturally effecting transformation. When true qi subtly arises, the turbid yin body is transmuted into pure yang. Only at this stage can fire talismans be employed, aligning the work with that of men. Without distinguishing these methods, how could the practice bear fruit? Therefore, men first refine the medicine, then the form; women first refine the form, then the medicine. Their bodies differ, so the sequence of their work also varies. In establishing teachings for others, I must clarify these distinctions so that learners may proceed without deficiency.

The principles of female elixir cultivation are not inherently complex, and their practice is not difficult. The reason most elixir classics do not transmit women’s cultivation is that they fail to address both men and women. In offering these teachings, I truly wish for the dual salvation of men and women. Thus, following the elixir texts, I append *Ten Principles of the Female Dao* to guide those of karmic affinity.

Why is the path of the female elixir so simple and uncluttered? Women’s nature is pure and whole; their bodies are tranquil and serene. With but a single practice, they can achieve thorough accomplishment. This is unlike men, whose thoughts are often wayward and partial, leading to differences in their aspirations and physical constitutions. Thus, women’s work is somewhat swifter than men’s. From nurturing the true to fetal breathing, two-thirds of the work is already achieved—unlike men, whose practice requires many additional steps to reach the stage of spirit regulation. Therefore, in female elixir texts, from nurturing the embryo to fetal breathing, once the work is complete, external practices are recorded next. Only when external practices are abundant may the stage of spirit regulation be refined.

Why must female immortals undergo a waiting period after completion? Their bodies, weak in blood, rely on internal refinement to become yang. Though the body may become yang, the congealed yin nature has not yet been fully refined. Thus, women’s practice lacks the final stage of emptying the void, preventing them from fully embodying the wondrous transformations of Heaven and Earth. This is why they cannot transcend the worldly realm—entirely due to the fragility of their physical form. Men, however, refine their bodies into indestructible vajra, and with the work of emptying the void complete, their spirit-light fills Heaven and Earth. Thus, without needing to wait, they may complete the Dao, attain perfection, and ascend to pay homage to the Supreme Emperor or wander at leisure in Penglai. Women are not so fortunate. After completing the female elixir, they must broadly perform meritorious deeds. Only when their merit is full, and the sages above take pity on them, will they petition the Supreme Emperor. Then, an imperial decree may be issued, a golden writ of selection bestowed, and the supreme Dao-fruit of humanity and Heaven attained. Otherwise, they will merely become solitary immortals.

Master Lü says:
“The refinement of the Great Yin’s form differs only slightly from men’s cultivation. The initial work begins with severing the Red Dragon. Thereafter, ten months of practice bring forth the yang spirit, shattering the void. The path of cultivation from then on is identical to men’s, with no distinction between them.”

The Daoist of Sui Mountain says:
“Once the Red Dragon is severed, the breasts naturally retract, becoming like a man’s. The true yin qi then transforms into true yang, and subsequent practice is no different from that of men. However, women belong to the tranquil body, and though the latter four stages share the same operations as men’s elixir work, their achievements are even swifter.”

Master Lü says:
“Men’s cultivation subdues the White Tiger; women’s cultivation severs the Red Dragon.”

The *Three Destinies Treatise* states:
“A man’s destiny lies in the elixir field—the elixir field is the true earth that gives birth to the elixir.”

A woman’s destiny lies in the breasts—the breasts are the essence of the mother’s qi, the wood spirit of nurturance.

It is also said: “Women take blood as their kidneys, which are hollow cavities. After forty-nine years, the waist dries and blood withers, leaving no vitality. Yet if nurtured long enough, the primordial blood revives, returning to a maiden’s state—this is the marvel of creating something from nothing. Once this something appears, a single severing transforms it, and life is reborn. From this point onward, the work of refining nature and life is identical to that of men.”

Master Lazy Daoist says: “Why are there three destinies for women? They are the upper, middle, and lower. The upper is the yang cavity, the middle is the Yellow Chamber, and the lower is the elixir field. In youth, one begins from the upper; in decline, from the middle; and only when mature does one proceed from the lower.

Moreover, women are inwardly yang and outwardly yin. First, one must sever the Red Dragon to preserve the integrity of the body, whereupon *kan* transforms into *qian*. Only then may one apply the methods of men’s practice, achieving success in a single year, for the golden elixir already resides within.”

The *Disputations on Cultivating the Real* poses a question: “How do the initial steps differ between men and women?”

The answer: “For men, the essential first step is refining qi; for women, it is refining form. Refining qi means subduing the qi, and subduing the qi requires its return. When qi returns, emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness becomes profound, root and life are restored, and the White Tiger is subdued. Refining form means concealing the form, and concealing the form requires its dissolution. When form dissolves, the Four Great Elements enter emptiness, the limbs decay and rot, and the Red Dragon is severed.

When a man subdues the White Tiger, he reverts to a youthful body, and the post-heaven essence no longer leaks, allowing the elixir to coalesce and years to be prolonged. When a woman severs the Red Dragon, she likewise reverts to a youthful body, and the impure yin blood no longer descends, allowing her to escape death and enter life. Thus, men’s practice is called ‘Greater Yang Refining Qi,’ while women’s practice is called ‘Greater Yin Refining Form.’”

Another question: “In refining form, do women not subdue qi?”

The answer: “Women are inherently yin, so their qi is easily subdued. Yet the Red Dragon is most harmful to the Dao, and this is where the emphasis lies. Thus, one begins by applying effort where it is most needed. Once the Red Dragon is severed, the qi naturally becomes docile—not like men, who are inherently yang and whose qi is difficult to subdue. For example, while a man may require three years to subdue qi, a woman can achieve it in one.

If a woman is truly a hero among her kind, receives her teacher’s oral instructions, and practices the Greater Yin Refining Form method, she may attain the Dao in three to five years, with less effort than a man. Yet such women are exceedingly rare. Only those whose fortitude surpasses a man’s by a hundredfold can succeed. Those equal to men in strength will never achieve it.”

Another question arises: "The Great Dao makes no distinction between male and female—why then are there differences in practice?"

The answer: "The Dao is the same, but its application differs. This is because men and women are endowed with different natures and distinct physical forms. Thus, though the Dao of cultivating life and destiny remains one, the methods of practice diverge considerably."

The Dark Heavenly Emperor declares: *The Book of Changes* states, "Qian is the father, Kun is the mother"—the principle of yin and yang is luminously evident. Only with heaven and earth do male and female come into being; thus, the Dao of yin and yang is self-evident, and it is clear that heaven and earth cannot be without them. Men receive the transformations of Qian and Kun to form their image, while women inherit the harmonious union of Qian and Kun to embody their form. All who possess these forms are imbued with the qi of Qian and Kun, standing together within the cosmos.

Now, in this era of compassionate salvation and shared effort to uphold the precious raft, men are taught through many methods—how could we neglect the Kun axis? Texts expounding the profound mysteries of male practice fill libraries, while those guiding women’s cultivation are few and far between. With deep sorrow, I speak of this alone.

A man’s body is pervaded by qi within essence, while a woman’s is suffused by qi within blood. When the *xiezi* is full, the lotus aperture is full; when the lotus aperture is full, the drawing and replenishing begin; and only then does the sweet dew descend. A man’s essence is abundant in qi, whereas a woman’s blood contains but a trace—hence the distinction: man is yang, woman is yin.

For those who cultivate this Dao, severing the seven emotions is fundamental, and cutting off the six desires comes first. Thus, the faintest qi in women often surpasses that of men. Why? A man’s heart is easily stirred, while a woman’s mind is somewhat still. Movement scatters qi, while stillness nurtures it. A man’s seven lotuses are easy to gather but hard to release; a woman’s seven lotuses are easy to release but hard to gather. If she remains unwavering in sincerity, within three to five years, the sweet dew will flow ceaselessly, and the seven lotuses will bloom perpetually. If blooming is effortless, how could gathering be difficult? The distinction between male and female is thus made clear.

Yet when texts speak of emptiness, void, mystery, subtlety, spirit, or transformation, these are the great commonalities shared by men and women. Men refer to their practice as "embryo," while women speak only of "breath," lest later generations misconstrue the term "embryo" and suffer false accusations. Moreover, when a man’s spirit departs, it must attain pure yang before the opportunity for shedding the husk arises, for yang contains yin within. A woman’s spirit departs differently: upon reaching the stage of the Three Yangs, she may transcend a hundred leagues; when she attains the union of the Pure and Aged Two Yangs, her departure is permanent, free from the risk of premature demise. For men, yang contains yin within, while women, yin contains yang within. In men, yin resides within, and yang without; in women, yin is without, and yang within. When yang prevails, all yin readily retreats.

I now spare no sacred truth, revealing this fully, solely in the earnest hope that many may swiftly realize their true aspiration."

The Golden Palace Emperor declares: "From a grand perspective, all meridians emerge from the formless Wuji. In finer detail, they belong to the formless and imageless, yet originally encompass all transformations. Both men and women share this supreme treasure, differing only in the two paths of blood and essence. A man’s essence ascends in reverse to become immortal, while a woman’s blood rises straight to the heart aperture—thus, each has their own method and illumination.

The male elixir transforms essence into qi, qi into spirit, and spirit into emptiness. When emptiness reaches its utmost stillness, the elixir naturally forms. The female elixir transforms blood into qi, qi into spirit, and spirit into emptiness. When emptiness becomes spontaneous, the elixir naturally coalesces.

Some claim that without severing the Red Dragon, the elixir cannot form and the Dao cannot be attained. Yet if blood is exhausted, qi is also exhausted—just as when a man’s essence decays, the elixir is difficult to complete. The principle is the same. For essence in men and blood in women must not be entirely cut off. Qi departs from blood, and without blood, qi has no source; blood transforms into qi, and only then does essence flow freely. If one claims to refine the elixir only after blood is spent, how then do young women with withered blood fall ill instead? This is final proof that blood cannot be abandoned."

The Queen Mother of the Jasper Pool says: "Women’s practice shares much with men’s, differing only in stages—the ground itself is not mysterious. All wondrous transformations are no different from those of men. You must awaken to this fully, for the texts hold the complete teaching, without error. Cultivate in seclusion, each according to your path."

The Venerable Perfected One of Perfect Illumination says: "Now I shall reveal the truth to spare you from suffering wrongful karmic entanglements. The paths of yin and yang are apportioned, yet for men and women, the essential node is one. Men possess this ancestral qi, distributed within essence and blood. Women’s ancestral qi converges, its yin aspect spoken of in relation to the Sea of Blood. Men have this yang barrier, its flow and reversal requiring no confusion. Women possess the Northern Sea’s terrain, its waves surging as if pressed by water.

The White Lotus Perfected One says: "The terrain of the Golden Elixir differs for men and women, yet the principle of yin and yang is truly the same. Purity of heart and restraint of desire are the root. To lay the foundation, first sever the Red Dragon."

The Master of No-Mind says: "Men’s essence-fluid is yang within yin; women’s essence-fluid is yin within yang. Swiftly seek a luminous teacher to have this revealed, and returning to youth in your native land shall be yours. If the Heavenly Heart of seven days may be restored, this is the supreme wondrous technique. Thereafter, the work aligns with that of men, yet each oral instruction must be imparted by the teacher."

Master Lü says: "In women’s cultivation, as with men, what is difficult to attain is immaculacy. Know that women’s desires exceed those of men. When the menstrual flow has ceased, the heart is like a lotus newly opened, riding the Heavenly rain and dew to bear its fruit. If a woman lacks a man, she is solitary yin. "

The Master of Integrity says: "The Great Dao makes no distinction between men and women—all may achieve completion. Thus, when a man completes the Dao, he becomes a Perfected One; when a woman completes it, she becomes a Primordial Sovereign. From ancient times, elixir scriptures have described men’s cultivation in exhaustive detail, while women’s practice is seldom addressed. When it is mentioned, only a glimpse is offered—not out of disdain for women’s cultivation. The underlying assumption is that since human nature and life are the same, the work is identical: speak of men’s practice, and women’s work need not be elaborated. Yet they do not realize that men are yang externally and yin internally, while women are yin externally and yang internally. Their inherent natures differ, their forms are distinct, and though their nature and life are the same, their practice diverges greatly."

*The Discernment of Cultivating the Real* states: "For men, the essential starting point is refining qi; for women, it is refining form."

Master Xu says: "When a man completes his practice, he no longer leaks essence; when a woman completes hers, she no longer leaks menses."

Their initial barriers are entirely distinct. Yet in refining the self, obtaining the medicine, returning the elixir, nurturing the embryo, and projecting spirit, though the work aligns with men’s, the subtle sequences may still differ in significant ways. In the spring of the *renchen* year, a woman of the *kun* trigram came to inquire about the Dao. Drawing from my extensive reading of ancient texts, I verified what had been transmitted to her. Yet elixir scriptures state: "Works on women’s practice are rare, making it difficult to examine differences." Thus, undeterred by the transgression of revealing celestial secrets, I set forth in writing the points of convergence and divergence, step by step, to serve as a guide for those seeking the path. May those who study these words and grasp their meaning avoid mistaking fish eyes for pearls. If they deeply understand and diligently practice, they may attain the supreme and ultimate Dao, and at the assemblies of the Jasper Pool, they shall not find it difficult to join the male immortals in paying homage to Lord Wood and together worship the Golden Mother.

Heaven is yang, earth is yin; *qian* is firm, *kun* is yielding. Yin cannot grow without yang, nor yang generate without yin. When firmness and softness achieve harmony, water and fire attain mutual completion. Yin and yang must have their counterparts, and from this all beings are conceived. Thus, heaven and earth both bear the true primordial qi, and men and women each possess an indestructible body. *Qian* is called Great Generation, through which one may realize the Dao and awaken to truth; *kun* is called Vast Generation, through which one may likewise attain the rank of Primordial Sovereign. If one claims that the yin of *kun* cannot enter the immortal path, how then does the Queen Mother dwell eternally on Kunlun? Chang’e stole the elixir from the rafters and became the eternal Empress of the Moon Palace. The carefree reader of the *Zhuangzi* styled herself the Exiled Immortal of the Jasper Pool. The Luo River Goddess and the Wu Mountain Maidens have been renowned since antiquity; Purple Maiden and the Xiang River Consorts remain illustrious to this day. Their traces are recorded in historical texts and may be verified. How could one who embodies the virtue of *kun* be incapable?

Women’s destinies originally have three phases: purple, white, and yellow—unlike men’s. Youth belongs to the upper, decline to the middle, and completion to the lower. A single misstep at the critical juncture must be deeply understood.

The qi cavity is the origin of blood, which is the breasts. It lies one *cun* and three *fen* at the center—not the two breasts. Men’s destiny resides in the Cinnabar Field, thus the Lower Field is their qi cavity. Women’s destiny lies in the breasts, thus the breasts are their qi cavity. When yin reaches its extreme and transforms into yang, it converts from the qi cavity into yin blood and flows outward in form. Therefore, severing the Red Dragon must begin where yin arises, and through prolonged practice, the form will naturally recede. If one mistakenly identifies the qi cavity as the point one *cun* and three *fen* below a man’s navel, it is an error.

Master Xu says: "When a man completes his practice, he no longer leaks essence; when a woman completes hers, she no longer leaks menses." Women’s menses are the first sign of life’s inception. When menses return to qi, the breasts shrink like a man’s, and the menses naturally cease. For men, refining essence into qi causes the yin root to shrink like a child’s, and essence no longer leaks. Only when there is no leakage can life be extended.

It is also said: "When a woman’s practice reaches the point where menses no longer leak, her subsequent work on nature and life aligns closely with men’s, differing only in minor ways. The obstacle lies in the lack of someone to reveal the secrets!"

The path of the yielding differs from that of the firm, yet its success may be easier. The firm require three years to subdue qi, while the yielding may achieve this in one year, for the elixir resides within their bodies.

The Primordial Sovereign Sun Bu’er says:
"Men and women arise from the same qi,
Yet differ in purity, turbidity, movement, and stillness.
If a woman wishes to cultivate the real,
She must gather her true primordial essence.
Within yin lies primordial yang—
Preserve its clarity and do not discard it.
Understand that form and desire
Are originally without encumbrance."

"Banish greed, wrath, and delusion; sever the bonds of care and anxiety.
Refine the turbid to cultivate a pure nature, and you shall not fall into evil destinies.
Abide in stillness and uphold non-action—
I, though a woman, possess the qualities of a man.
Without form in the realm of non-being,
Yet with intent in the realm of being.
Inwardly perceive form, sound, and emptiness,
Not a trace of attachment remains.
Rely on earth as the foundation of Kun,
For the one yang originally arises from the earth.
Lead and mercury may differ,
Yet qi and spirit share the same principle.
The subtle, empty, and numinous heart—
The heart-spirit can govern all.
When the one qi returns to spring harmony,
It soars beyond the clouds.
Together we wander in the Great Clarity,
This is called the gatha of true suchness.

The Way of Qian is movement; the Way of Kun is stillness.
To cultivate nature and life, one must begin with stillness.
You are originally still—why then seek to cultivate?
The Way of Kun is turbid; the Way of Qian is pure.
To cultivate nature and life, one must seek purity.
Only by refining the turbid into purity
Can one enter the Dao and attain fruition.

The *Scripture of the Divine Embryo’s Longevity and Function* states:
'After success, men gather essence in the Gate of Origin,
While women’s wombs no longer conceive embryos.
Though desire stirs, it cannot contend with spirit.'
This is the true essence returning to spirit in the midst of embryo formation—
This is the Upper Clarity.

*Ten Principles of the Female Elixir*
Composed by the Ancient Buddha Qinglie of Huazang Mountain."

**Editorial Principles of *Women’s Daoist Studies: A Small Collection*
Compiled and Revised by Chen Yingning of Wanjiang**

1. To select exemplary conduct and edifying words from women of past and present, along with concise writings, that may cultivate lofty character and impart exceptional wisdom in women—this pertains to the psychological aspect.
2. To select methods of cultivation, scriptures, treatises, and poems from women of past dynasties that may foster extraordinary talent in women and develop robust, graceful physiques—this pertains to the physiological aspect.
3. Daoist studies are the quintessence of the Chinese nation, unique to our country and absent among all others. Formerly, as women’s education was not yet widespread, this transcendent discipline remained unstudied. Now, in accordance with the principle of equality between men and women, such knowledge shall gradually be made public.
4. Ancient writings vary in depth; some are profoundly abstruse and difficult to comprehend, while others are overly vulgar and uninspiring. For the sake of accessibility, abstruse passages must be annotated to ensure clarity, and vulgar ones refined to avoid offending refined sensibilities.
5. The purpose of this compilation is to promote China’s indigenous Daoist tradition while utterly rejecting superstition. Any text even slightly tainted by superstition shall not be included.

**Notes to the Reader**
1. This book bears no author’s name, and the traditional attribution to "Ancient Mother Xiangyi of Jinhua Mountain" is crude and shallow. Though the author may not wish to use their real name, why fabricate a sacred title? Thus, it has been omitted.
2. Though the book is titled *Ten Principles*, the fifth and sixth principles largely overlap in content, and the tenth is excessively vague, rendering it unnecessary as a separate entry. These three have therefore been substantially abridged to spare the reader tedium. Other principles have also been edited or revised. Comparing the original with this edition will reveal the necessity of such changes—this is no idle revision.
3. Beyond the main text, readers should pay close attention to the appended annotations. These commentaries are the fruit of decades of experience and are not mere casual remarks, as in the writings of others, which lack substance or evidence.
4. The appended *Kun Formula*, though not definitively attributable to Sun Buer, is written in a concise and admirable style. The annotations by Fu Jinquan, however, are regrettably filled with metaphorical language, which may prove difficult for scholars to grasp. The origins of the terms "Yellow Sprout" and "White Snow" at the end are explained in detail—readers should study them carefully.
5. Among all extant *Women’s Elixir Scriptures* containing true methods, only twenty-odd have been found throughout the country from ancient times to the present. Scholars must read them all to grasp the essentials, then seek teachers and companions for practical cultivation—only then may success be hoped for. Beware of half-understanding and self-satisfaction.

*Seventh month of the twenty-fourth year of the Republic of China,
The four thousand six hundred and thirty-second year since the Yellow Emperor.*

Chen Yingning of Wanjiang wrote this preface in lieu.

**First Principle: Nurturing the True and Transforming Qi**

*(Yingning states: In Daoist practice, the value lies in oral instructions; the quality of the prose matters little. Thus, works with flawed theories, awkward phrasing, vulgar insights, or superstitious admixtures often invite disdain, causing even their authentic oral instructions to be buried and forgotten—most lamentable indeed. In compiling this book, I aim to guide women of above-average learning. Had I merely transcribed the original text, it might have drawn ridicule from the refined. Instead, I have distilled its essence, excised its flaws, and though it may not be entirely pure, it is now far more presentable than the original.)*

Women’s cultivation differs from men’s. In men, yang descends and dissipates; in women, yang ascends. Men’s bodies are firm; women’s are yielding. Men preserve the yang essence of the dantian, preventing its outward leakage. Over time, they refine it with the true fire within, transmuting essence into qi, qi into spirit, and spirit into emptiness—thus attaining the Dao. Women, being of yin constitution, must employ the spiritual marrow of the breasts to transform their temperament. Through prolonged refinement, the red naturally reverts to white, and blood transmutes into qi. Once blood has become qi, they too use the fire talisman to advance and retreat, enabling qi to revert to pure yang and return to the true.

Thus, women begin with refining the physical form before refining the original essence, unlike men, who first refine the original essence before refining the form.

What is "nurturing the true"? The human heart is easily swayed; if constrained within, it grows restless. Therefore, the initial step teaches the method of nurturing the true, naturally easing restlessness, harmonizing the four limbs, and enabling further progress.

In daily seated practice, one must mobilize the qi mechanism from the dantian and sea of blood, directing it toward the spirit chamber within the heart. A wisp of pure qi emerges from the sea of blood. With prolonged focus, the qi stirs and, following its dynamic impulse, soars upward to the niwan. It then descends again. At this moment, gently guide it with intention as it flows from the niwan down the *chonglou*. Here, one must not exert intent, lest the body be harmed. Simply follow the qi’s movement as it descends from the *chonglou* to the space between the breasts, where an empty cavity resides. After prolonged concentration, if movement arises, proceed as before. Within forty or fifty days, the qi will penetrate, blood will transmute into qi, and the red will revert to white. At this stage, the elixir origin is revealed, and the Dao-heart is sincere.

If one persists in tranquil abiding, unwavering day and night, and diligent practice, how can the great elixir fail to form, or the female immortal fail to manifest? This is the first step in women’s cultivation. If one attains its most profound and wondrous state, all subsequent practices will advance from this foundation.

Students, take heed.

*(Yingning notes: When the qi descends from the niwan, it is first said, "gently guide it with intention," and then, "do not exert intent." So, is intention to be used or not?)*

Is intention to be used or not? At this stage of movement, it lies between intention and non-intention. To follow its natural course, one says, "Do not exert intent." Yet it cannot be left unattended, hence one says, "Gently guide it with intention."

**Second: Nine Cycles of Refining the Form**
Refining the form means regulation and nurturance. Blood and fluids, belonging to yin and yang, congeal and reside below, stored in the Sea of Blood and the womb, transformed at the summit of the Five Aggregates Mountain. They irrigate the entire body, nourishing the hundred vessels, circulating without cease, overflowing through the meridians, and becoming dreg-like matter, discarded and unused. Only after two hundred and forty *ke* (twenty-four hours) have passed does the refined essence re-emerge, the primordial transform the form, and it is retained for the sustenance of life. This is what is meant by: the pure qi ascends to the breasts, while the turbid qi descends to become stagnation. The mechanism of human life and immortality truly diverges here. Thus, in women’s cultivation, one must first discern the pure from the turbid to refine the true form.

How is the form refined? While seated, employ the spirit’s mechanism to move. When the mouth is full of fluid, gently rinse several times, waiting until it clarifies. Then, inhale the pure qi through the nose, swallowing it along with the jade-like fluid, descending past the Towering Mansion into the Crimson Palace, then further down to the Yellow Chamber, halting at the Sea of Blood in the Pass Origin. Briefly stabilize it, then circulate it from the Sea of Blood to the Tailbone, ascending the Spine’s Ridge to penetrate the Crown Gate and enter the Niwan Palace. From there, descend again to the space between the breasts and pause. After a long gathering, the fluid transforms into qi—this is one cycle. Repeat this three times three cycles. Once complete, use both hands to rotate the breasts thirty-six times. Then, cup them and gently guide them to the center, halting at the Sea of Blood. Repeat the refining process as before. One round consists of three cycles; three rounds complete nine cycles.

If a woman is profound, composed, deeply rooted, and of substantial capacity, practicing thus for no more than a hundred days, her form will be refined, and the path to longevity will open. From here, further progress may be sought, and the great elixir will be within reach.

**Third: Applying the Fire and Talisman**
Men first refine the medicine, then the form. Women first refine the form, then the medicine. Due to the differences in their physical constitutions, the sequence of practice differs. Women cultivating the Dao should follow the preceding oral instructions with diligence. When, in the Sea of Blood within the Elixir Field, the qi mechanism warms, a wisp of pure qi will naturally surge upward to the Heart Chamber, reaching the breasts. At this moment, one must not stir a single thought. Continue the practice and circulation, allowing it to descend naturally back to the Sea of Blood. By this time, the qi mechanism has stirred, and true qi has arisen. The yin of red blood transforms into the yang of white qi. If the fire and talisman are not applied, the qi will revert to red blood, the white will turn red again, and all effort will be wasted.

At this stage, one must use true fire to refine it and true talisman to respond. When the talisman arrives and the fire is sufficient, the qi will congeal. At this moment of congealment, a distinct phenomenon arises. If not clearly explained, the application of fire and talisman may err, risking harm to the elixir origin. Cultivators must memorize this carefully and not forget. If this stage is mishandled, not only will prior efforts be wasted, but future practice will be difficult, and bodily harm—even the danger of blood collapse—may ensue. Students, take heed.

When the qi returns to the Sea of Blood, though it emerges from the blood, it is not blood itself. It is like a person who leaves home, changes appearance, and returns—even their family and wife may not recognize them, let alone restore the former intimacy. Thus, as it descends, the Sea of Blood must feel like a fish gulping water. At this time, the four limbs grow languid as if intoxicated, and the bliss is akin to the union of husband and wife, an ecstasy beyond restraint.

*(Yingning notes: Some say the bliss within the body at this moment surpasses that of sexual union manyfold. For certain women, naturally cold in temperament, or when paired with a physically weak man, may feel no pleasure at all. Yet women practicing the Dao differ: though alone, with a pure heart and few desires, seated in quietude, if they harmonize the yin and yang within themselves according to the method and enter the most profound and wondrous state, extraordinary phenomena will manifest. The bliss defies description. While men practicing quiet cultivation may sometimes experience blissful phenomena, compared to the degree of a woman’s bliss, they attain only twenty or thirty percent. This pertains to the physiological differences between men and women, not something that can be forced by human effort.)*

At this stage, one must firmly maintain control and not indulge. Focus the mind and stabilize it in the Central Palace. After a long gathering, it will naturally surge upward through the passes, ascend into the Niwan Palace, and transform into jade-like fluid. Guide it with intention down the Towering Mansion, halting between the breasts. Use the method of congealing qi to blend it, ensuring it gathers and does not scatter. With prolonged practice, one will naturally return to the origin and achieve fetal breathing. Once fetal breathing is attained, the Dao of immortality can be realized in due time. Women cultivators of the truth, take this to heart.

**Fourth: Silent Circulation of Fetal Breathing**
If women cultivators of the truth follow the preceding oral instructions with wholehearted dedication, true qi will arise daily, blood will transform into fluid, and from the space between the breasts, it will flow through the hundred vessels, moistening and nourishing the entire body.

This fluid, transformed from blood, must be constantly nurtured with the internal circulation of primordial harmonizing qi to warm and nourish it. Only then can it stabilize the Central Field, becoming the foundation for transcendence.

What is the internal circulation of primordial harmonizing qi? It arises from and settles within the center—breath born of the middle, returning to stillness in the middle.

For women cultivators of the truth who have attained the jade-like fluid, this qi must be employed to congeal it. Only thus will the fluid not dissipate, and only thus may it coalesce into the Reverted Elixir. During this internal circulation, the movement neither adheres to nor departs from the mouth and nose. Though it bears the name of breathing, it possesses no form of breathing. Why? It borrows the breath to fulfill the meaning of breathing.

For the breathing of mouth and nose is postnatal breathing, while the internal circulation is prenatal breathing. At this stage, emphasis is placed on the prenatal, not the postnatal. Prenatal breathing has a name but no form, and as it follows the postnatal breathing of mouth and nose—one exhalation, one inhalation—it naturally ascends and descends. With prolonged practice, each breath returns to the root, and the qi of breathing no longer depends on the mouth and nose. Once fetal breathing is achieved, the Dao of immortality is not far off.

The subtler intricacies and finer turns of this practice must be transmitted orally and demonstrated in person by one’s master. Moreover, the disciple must inwardly grasp and spiritually comprehend them, verifying them through direct experience within their own body. With deep and prolonged cultivation, the work will naturally come to fruition. Should one suddenly achieve a thorough and luminous penetration, they will spontaneously align with the profound mysteries of the Dao, no longer needing to cling rigidly to the written word.

**Fifth Principle: Broadening Meritorious Conduct**

If a woman can quietly cultivate the supreme Dao, having already refined the Jade Fluid and Returned Elixir, recognized her prenatal visage, and stabilized her fetal breathing practice, she must then rely on external conduct to nurture the root of the Dao. Only thus will she avoid deficiency in the outer while gaining support within. Therefore, in serving her parents-in-law, she should fulfill her filial devotion; in interacting with others, she ought to maintain sincerity and kindness. She should take pity on orphans and widows, relieve suffering and aid the poor, respect her teachers and elders, and foster harmony with her neighbors. Her movements should not be frivolous, her speech not arrogant. All her actions should conform to principle and propriety, naturally refining her qi and disposition into a state of harmonious equilibrium. Without seeking meritorious conduct, such conduct will establish itself.

The Sixth Principle: Resolute Commitment in Practice

Should a female cultivator of the Dao secure an enlightened master, grasp the oral instructions, cultivate her character, and pledge unwavering dedication to her practice with enduring perseverance, then the attainment of immortality may be realized in due course. Yet the nature of women is often fickle and mutable; when faced with adversity, one can scarcely guarantee that her original resolve will not falter. Some may be led astray by divergent paths, harboring wayward and biased views, thereby squandering all prior efforts and sinking into the abyss of karmic suffering—how lamentable this would be!

Though many in the world transmit the Dao, those who receive its true transmission are rare, and many err, leading others astray. Here, the oral instructions of the Female Elixir are revealed with clarity, to guide those of karmic affinity. May they leap from the sea of delusion and reach the other shore, ensuring that no jade-like souls are shattered in the Yellow Springs, and that the fair and rosy-cheeked may preserve their youthful countenance. By steadfastly upholding these teachings without loss, they shall enjoy boundless joy—how could this not be a cause for celebration?

The precepts are as follows:
1. Filial devotion to parents-in-law.
2. Uprightness and integrity.
3. Prudence in speech.
4. Carefulness in conduct.
5. Reverence for the master and the Dao.
6. Unwavering resolve.

**Seventh Rule: Cultivating the Original Spirit**
The practice of women is swifter than that of men. In the Female Elixir method, from nurturing the true to achieving fetal breathing, the greater part of the work is already complete—unlike the male path, which requires many additional steps to reach the stage of spirit regulation. Thus, once the work of nurturing the true and fetal breathing is finished, one proceeds to external cultivation. When external practice is abundant, the work of refining and regulating the spirit may begin. For by this time, the blood has already been transmuted into *qi*, and this *qi* can then be transformed into spirit. If, at this stage, one does not refine the temperament and supplement it with external practice, the spirit may fail to congeal, risking the loss of all prior effort. One must adhere strictly to the precepts, observe the methods with rigor, and polish the mind until it becomes like a block of crystal—refined and refined again, polished and polished again, until inner and outer are immaculate, transparent within and without, the form empty and free of the slightest dust. At this point, a luminous, transformative mechanism naturally arises, illuminating the cavity within. In deep meditation, one neither eats nor hungers, neither moves nor speaks. At this time, a guardian must be present to prevent startling disturbances, lest the spirit be harmed or demonic influences intrude, which would cause grave harm. Women who have reached this stage must remain vigilant, avoiding any misstep. Day and night, someone must watch over them. If their breath ceases entirely and their complexion remains unchanged—whether for a day or two, five or seven days, or even ten—do not disturb them. Wait until their breath returns faintly and their spiritual light begins to emerge before calling to them in a low voice. After emerging from meditation, let them eat, drink, and dress as they please. Companions of like aspiration must remain constantly attentive, guarding them until the spirit has fully departed the body, at which point the danger is past.

**Eighth Rule: Moving the Spirit Beyond the Shell**
The path of women is to return from yin to yang, and when yang is at its peak, the spirit is complete. Yet even when the body becomes like an ice-clear vessel and the spirit like autumn waters, the spirit must not be allowed to linger too long within. Thus, when the melon is ripe, it falls of itself; when the spirit is perfected, it naturally departs. At this time, one should employ the method of spirit projection, moving the spirit beyond the body. However, it must not be expelled too swiftly, nor should the initial excursions be overly prolonged, lest the spirit become lost and unable to return. The spirit should be drawn back into the body, moving in and out, beginning nearby and gradually extending farther. Take care not to act recklessly; only when the spirit is fully matured may it come and go freely. Once mastery is attained, there will be no fear. Yet the work of spirit projection requires further elaboration.

Before the yang spirit emerges, its nature is utterly still, and the practice remains governed by the rules of nurturing the true. This continues until the spirit is perfected. Once the yang spirit has emerged, its nature becomes dynamic, and the previous methods no longer suffice. One must then employ the method of freeing the spirit, allowing it to move with lively agility, unobstructed. Whether wandering mountains and rivers or manifesting in response to conditions, when opportunities arise, one should perform meritorious deeds. When merit is complete and virtue perfected, the day of ascension will naturally arrive.

**Ninth Rule: Awaiting Ascension by Grace**
When the Female Elixir is cultivated to completion and a body of pure yang is attained, one is unbound and moves freely. Yet one must widely perform virtuous deeds and plant many roots of goodness. One must not, in the freedom of spirit projection, neglect the fruition of the Dao, nor boast of the marvels of creation, lest one stray from the uprightness of heaven’s principle. Any reckless conduct will surely invite retribution. Instead, one should subtly employ spiritual power to protect the nation and save the people. When merit and practice are complete, high sages and perfected beings will naturally come to deliver one to ascension—ascending to the Golden Towers, receiving imperial decrees, and dwelling forever in the celestial palaces, enjoying boundless joy.

But why must female immortals await deliverance? It is because, though their weak constitution has been refined through internal practice into a yang body, the residual yin essence has not yet been fully dissolved. They lack the final stage of returning to emptiness and cannot fully embody the wondrous transformations of heaven and earth. Thus, they cannot transcend the world on their own, for their form is not yet indestructible. This differs from the male body, which, when refined into an indestructible vajra form and the work of returning to emptiness is complete, radiates spiritual light that fills heaven and earth. Thus, men need not await deliverance but may realize the Dao, ascend to the presence of the Supreme Emperor, and wander the immortal isles of Penglai.

*(Sakura’s note: I cannot endorse this theory. I record it here merely as it stands. If a woman truly possesses great wisdom, great power, and attains great liberation, then at the pinnacle of her practice, she may take a further step—where form and emptiness are nondual, self and other forgotten, and the luminosity of her original nature transcends the beginningless. Only then will she know that the mundane world and celestial palaces are equal in suffering and joy, that yin and yang, though named differently, emerge from the same source. The ten directions and three realms do not lie beyond the Gate of the Mysterious Female, and immortals, buddhas, and sentient beings alike all seek sustenance from the Mother. At this stage, what need is there to speak of awaiting ascension? "Emerge from the same source," "the Gate of the Mysterious Female," and "seek sustenance from the Mother" are phrases from Laozi’s *Dao De Jing*, each containing profound meaning.)*

The Tenth Principle: Attaining the Dao and Realizing Truth

What is most valued in cultivation is the transformation of the mortal frame into a sacred vessel. Without this, one remains like a frog at the bottom of a well—still a creature of the karmic sea, how could one escape the cycle of rebirth and transcend the turning of kalpas? If a woman in the world can maintain a heart unstained by even a speck of dust and a qi unbroken by a hundred setbacks, following her teacher’s oral instructions and practicing in seclusion day and night, she may attain the fruit of the supreme vehicle in merely three to five years. Why, then, would anyone shrink from such a path?

*(This passage has been largely revised, as it consisted of empty words.)*

**Appendix: The Kun Formula**
By Sun Bu’er, the Pure and Primordial Sovereign
Collated by Fu Jinquan, the Integrator of Unity

True transmission has its secrets—true transmission has its secrets.

Women embody the yielding virtue of *kun*, and within their true yin resides true yang, making their cultivation comparatively easier. The key lies in exerting effort within *you* ("being"). *You* is the origin of *wu* ("non-being"); from *you* to *wu* is the station of true yang. Though these two lines emphasize the work of *ming* (destiny), they unite both *xing* (nature) and *ming*, forming the foremost crucial point in the *kun* path.

The first line requires returning to *wu* within *you*; the second, generating *you* within *wu*. One must understand *geng* and *jia*—scholars of Confucianism expound upon this in detail.

*Geng* and *jia* clarify the entry point of *ming* cultivation.
*Geng* is metal, the tiger.
*Jia* is wood, the dragon.
*Geng*-metal is the foundation of cultivation, while *jia*-wood constantly fears its restraint. Yet within this restraint lies the germ of life. Alchemists delight in seeking vitality within death. Thus, when the *geng*-tiger descends, the *jia*-dragon rises—a descent and a rise, the mechanism of life and death is already latent, and the principle of inversion becomes ever more real.

When one knows this moment of life, death, and inversion, one applies the method to sever the dragon’s head and lead the tiger by its tail, ensuring the dragon does not summon clouds and the tiger does not stir winds. When wind and clouds subside, the sky clears and the moon shines bright; when dragon and tiger are subdued, nature and emotion harmonize. Returning to the furnace to refine, the millet-pearl is immediately formed—no method of preserving life surpasses this.

The word "know" implies quiet observation and vigilant guarding. When the wind is about to rise, one must seize the tiger; when rain is about to fall, one may sever the dragon—neither too soon nor too late, cutting at the opportune moment to refine. Without understanding this principle, one has no means to learn the Dao. Only by integrating it with the study of the classics can one avoid falling into emptiness.

To grasp the turning phrase beneath the *Doctrine of the Mean* is to perceive the Dao. The principles of governance must be studied, the nature-school thoroughly penetrated; one cultivates both nature and life, joining yin and yang. The alchemical Dao is contained within the *Book of Changes*, where the *Tuan* states: "Supreme is the origin of Kun! All things draw life from it." Kun belongs to old yin; when yin reaches its extreme, yang is born. Following heaven’s sequence, it gives birth to humans and creatures; following one’s own sequence, it completes the Dao and perfects the true. By closely examining where Kun’s true yang arises, one knows where the true unity of the self is produced. Seek this unity, secure this unity, and the treasure of life is whole.

The work of nature is the beginning and end of entering the Dao. Without penetrating nature, this treasure cannot abide; it must be like the autumn moon in serene meditation—spotless, without a speck of dust. Once the beginning of no-beginning is clearly understood, the non-empty emptiness all returns to spontaneity, and thus nature and life are dually cultivated, yin and yang joined.

Teach others to discern being and non-being with care, lest they waste a heart full of ardor. Yin and yang are precisely being and non-being; one must attend to the scriptures with diligence. In three days, the coming of true yang and the going of true yin will be as orderly as a well. The dragon’s head may be severed, the tiger’s tail retained; when the two qi intermingle in harmonious warmth, the dharma-body is formed. Otherwise, one merely exhausts heart and blood—how can refinement proceed?

The mechanism lies before you; qi is drawn forth from it. Above is the Celestial Valley, below the Spring Cavern. Fix your attention on these two places without the slightest deviation. At the critical moment, begin with the eyes to direct the intent, with intent to guide qi, with qi to congeal spirit, with spirit to refine the true. Thus one connects heaven and earth, and nothing is not efficacious.

If the Celestial Valley does not grow warm, qi does not ascend; if the Bubbling Spring does not grow warm, qi does not descend. One must focus the eyes and intent, directing force upward and downward to draw it. Fix attention on the two cavities without the slightest error. Practice at midnight and noon until fully mastered, then proceed to refinement. With timely insight and diligent cultivation, yellow sprouts and white snow will naturally appear.

*(Yingning’s note: "Yellow sprouts and white snow" are originally technical terms in external alchemy. Here they serve as metaphors for internal alchemy, prompting reflections I cannot suppress. Consulting the *Golden Elixir Secret Formula* of the Ancestor Fu Li, Chapter Seven: "Purple powder like frost, yellow sprouts fill the chamber." Xu the Perfected’s *Stone Casket Record*, in the "Purple Mother Treatise," states: "A cauldron of cinnabar may be ingested; long consumption reverses yang and changes the bones, reversing yang and changing bones to become an immortal. This requires divine talismans and white snow. Great indeed is the divine talisman, true white snow—reviving the soul and resurrecting Xu Jia." The *Stone Casket Record*’s "Luminous Chamber of the Spirit" further says: "Each grain a true red pearl, red blossoms and purple veins give birth to the Metal Duke. When Metal Duke, water, and earth unite, refined yellow sprouts become white snow. Purple sand and red powder drift about—drifting about, the Green Dragon’s ointment. When red powder is scarce and the White Tiger aged, refined dragon’s ointment and tiger’s brain become the wish-fulfilling jewel in the Hall of Longevity. To transmute ten thousand taels of gold is nothing—it can transmute the feeble old man to eternal youth."

Observe the above: red blossoms, purple veins, yellow sprouts, white snow, red powder, purple sand—these terms all refer to tangible substances produced in the external alchemical furnace. They possess real forms, visible to the eye, graspable by hand, and ingestible. Thus they are called the golden elixir. Later practitioners, failing to receive the true transmission—or even if they did, guarding it in secrecy—transformed the term, calling the undispersed union of essence, qi, and spirit in the human body the "golden elixir." Though this deviates from the ancient immortals’ methods, traces remain discernible. A second transformation later applied Buddhist terms like *tathata*, *perfect enlightenment*, *nirvana*, and *wondrous mind*, or Confucian terms like *wuji*, *taiji*, *heavenly principle*, and *innate knowledge*, indiscriminately attaching them to the "golden elixir." Thus later Dao-seekers fell into a fog, utterly bewildered—truly a descent into further obscurity.

The art of transmuting mercury into gold, once withheld by the Chinese, was lost and later discovered by foreigners. The elixir of immortality, which the Chinese dare not claim for themselves, may yet be seized by outsiders. For a nation with five thousand years of civilization and four hundred million enlightened people to forget its heritage and seek the Dao abroad—how grievous!)*

**Supplementary Q&A**

**Question from Miss Lü Bicheng, Answer by Mr. Chen Yingning:**

*The Ten Principles of Female Elixir* states: "The yang of women ascends from above." May I ask what is meant by "the yang of women," and how does it ascend?

**Answer:** What is called the yang of women refers to a kind of generative qi within the female body. "Ascends" means it rises to the two breasts. In maidenhood, the breasts remain unformed because this yang qi is inwardly gathered. After the age of ten or so, the breasts gradually develop. The cause of this transformation is the function of yang qi.

**Question:** How are the terms *fire* and *symbol* to be explained? What is their function?

**Answer:** Daoists have the terms "advancing yang fire" and "retreating yin symbol." The two words *fire* and *symbol* are abbreviations of these. For example, when a blacksmith forges iron, he first uses fierce fire to heat it until it glows red inside and out—this is "yang fire." Then he quenches the red-hot iron in cold water to harden it—this is "yin symbol." The human body operates on the same principle. As for their specific functions, they cannot be explained in a few words.

**Question:** What is meant by *form and substance*? What is meant by *original essence*? What is meant by *prior and posterior*?

**Answer:** *Form* refers to the two breasts, and *substance* refers to menstruation. *Original essence* refers to the "prenatal qi." In men’s practice, one first begins by gathering the prenatal qi, then seals the essence orifice to prevent any leakage. This is called "first refining the original essence, then refining form and substance." In women’s practice, one must first "sever the Red Dragon," waiting until the monthly flow ceases entirely and the breasts retract like those of a maiden. Only then does one gather the "prenatal qi" to congeal the inner elixir. This is called "first refining form and substance, then refining the original essence."

**Question:** How is the practice of "nurturing the true" performed?

**Answer:** The method for nurturing the true has already been explained in this text, namely the passage below that states: *"When sitting in daily practice, one must mobilize the qi mechanism from within the elixir field and sea of blood."*

**Question:** Where in the body are the *elixir field* and *sea of blood* located?

**Answer:** The *Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon* states: *"The brain is the sea of marrow, the womb is the sea of blood, and the chest center is the sea of qi."* To know where the sea of blood is located, one must first understand what the womb is. The womb lies before the rectum and behind the bladder; in women, it is called the *uterus*, the place where conception and gestation occur.

**Question:** What is meant by *mobilizing the qi mechanism*? Is it like performing gentle calisthenics?

**Answer:** The *qi mechanism* does not refer to human strength but to the activating mechanism of the vital qi within the body. The term *mobilizing* means that the true intention and original spirit take charge—it is not a matter of moving the hands or feet. At this time, one remains in stillness while seated.

**Question:** What is the *spirit chamber within the heart*?

**Answer:** This refers to the *chest center*, the region between the membranes in the chest, which is the location of the pericardium.

**Question:** What is meant by *prolonged stabilization*?

**Answer:** When the heart is calm and the breath harmonized, spirit and qi coalesce—this is called *abiding*. Maintaining this state continuously, extending it for as long as possible without scattering the spirit or falling into torpor, is called *prolonged stabilization*.

**Question:** What is the *mud-pill palace*? What is the *heavy tower*?

**Answer:** The *mud-pill palace* (泥丸) is located at the crown of the head—it is the brain. The *heavy tower* (重楼) is the straight central pathway descending from the front of the chest, roughly corresponding to the medical conception of the *Chong* and *Ren* meridians.

**Question:** Where is the empty cavity between the two breasts, and what is its name?

**Answer:** The empty cavity between the two breasts is called *Shanzhong* (膻中) in medical texts. The *Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon* states: *"Shanzhong is the sea of qi."* It also says: *"Shanzhong is the ministerial envoy, from which joy and happiness emerge."* And further: *"Shanzhong is the palace city of the heart’s ruler."* This region contains the diaphragm, which connects anteriorly to the *xiphoid process*, posteriorly to the spine, and laterally to the ribs. Above the diaphragm lie the heart and lungs. The heart stores spirit; the lungs store qi. If the heartbeat ceases, death follows immediately; if the breath of the lungs is cut off, death likewise ensues at once. Thus, the *Shanzhong* region is of paramount importance in the human body.

**Question:** What is the *Five Aggregates Mountain Peak*?

**Answer:** The term *Five Aggregates* (五蕴) originates in Buddhist scriptures, not Daoist discourse. The Five Aggregates are also called the Five *Skandhas*—namely, *form, sensation, perception, mental formations,* and *consciousness*. Here, however, the character *yun* (蕴) should be understood as *harmony*. It refers to the harmonious coalescence of the qi of the Five Phases. The *mountain peak* denotes the *Shanzhong* region, which lies above the *sea of blood* and is thus called the *peak*.

**Question:** The texts say, *"Blood and fluids transform into dreg-like matter, which departs and is not used."* How can it depart and not be used?

**Answer:** *"Departs and is not used"* refers to monthly menstruation. This is a natural process, not something contrived by human effort.

**Question:** Two hundred forty *ke* (刻漏) and thirty *shichen* (时辰) together equal how many hours?

**Answer:** Two hundred forty *ke* are equivalent to thirty *shichen*, for one *shichen* comprises eight *ke*. Thus, thirty *shichen* equal sixty hours.

**Question:** The texts speak of *"refining the essence to reveal it anew."* What is *refining the essence*?

**Answer:** The term *refining the essence* (熔华) does not appear in ancient Daoist texts. Its meaning refers to the period after menstruation concludes, when thirty *shichen* have passed and the vital qi in the womb is abundant. If one follows the human path, conception may occur; if one follows the immortal path, the foundation for elixir-cultivation may be established. *Rong* (熔) means *to dissolve*; *hua* (华) means *essence*.

**Question:** What is meant by *prior to Heaven*?

**Answer:** The concept of *prior to Heaven* (先天) requires study of the *Yi Jing*’s trigrams and images for proper understanding. Confucius said: *"That which is prior to Heaven—Heaven does not oppose it."* Laozi said: *"There is a thing, formlessly formed, born before Heaven and Earth."* And further: *"Vague! Elusive! Within it there is image. Elusive! Vague! Within it there is something. Dark! Obscure! Within it there is essence. Its essence is most real; within it there is sincerity."* These lines vividly depict the landscape of *prior to Heaven*. Master Zhang Ziyang’s *Awakening to Reality* states:
*"Within the vague and elusive, seek the image;
Within the dark and obscure, find the true essence.
From here, being and non-being interpenetrate—
How can mere imagination ever grasp it?"*
This poem describes the scenery of *prior to Heaven*, which must be personally verified through practice to be understood. Without direct experience, idle speculation remains confusion.

**Question:** What is *pure*? What is *turbid*? How are they distinguished?

**Answer:** Qi is pure; blood is turbid. The pure ascends; the turbid descends. The pure is usable; the turbid is not. However, students must not misunderstand the statement that the turbid is unusable and thus allow it to depart without retention, failing to cherish or refine it. Know that turbid blood is transformed from pure qi—if too much turbid blood is lost each month, the pure qi will also become deficient. In superior practice, the pure qi ceases to transform into turbid blood, and menstruation naturally ceases. In middling practice, one refines pure qi from turbid blood, gradually reducing menstruation until it stops entirely. Not only must the red discharge vanish completely, but even the white must be absent—only then is there hope for success. Otherwise, one may only cultivate for a future life; dreams of attainment in this one are futile.

**Question:** The texts speak of "employing the divine mechanism to move, causing the mouth to fill with fluid." If one merely raises the tongue for a moment, the mouth will fill with saliva. Is this what is meant by employing the divine mechanism to move? It also says, "Draw in the pure qi with the nose." Is this "pure qi" simply the air of the external world?

**Answer:** In the teachings of internal alchemy, there are the concepts of "golden fluid" and "jade fluid." This practice resembles what the ancients called the "jade fluid river chariot." First, sit upright with the body aligned. Next, calm the mind and settle the breath. Then, regulate the breathing and concentrate the spirit. At this time, gaze at the tip of the nose, listen to the breath, and press the tongue against the upper palate (a technique known as "bridging the heavens") to await the generation of saliva in the mouth. When it fills slightly, swallow it. Then proceed according to the method of circulating the river chariot as described in the texts. If this can be done smoothly, it is best. If there are difficulties and one cannot follow the instructions precisely, one must devote careful study to the matter.

**Question:** Where are the "heart chamber," "yellow court," "Pass of Origin," and what is "jade fluid"?

**Answer:** The "heart chamber" refers to the region of the heart. The "yellow court" lies below the heart and above the navel, between the two. The "Pass of Origin" is located a little over two inches below the navel. "Jade fluid" is the sweet, cool, and clear saliva in the mouth.

**Question:** Where are the "coccyx," "spine’s midpoint," and "crown gate"?

**Answer:** The "coccyx" is the small final segment of the spinal column, where four bones fuse into one, directly above the anus. The "spine’s midpoint" is below the eleventh vertebra of the spine; acupuncturists call this point the "Spinal Center." The "crown gate" is the center of the top of the head, known to acupuncturists as the "Hundred Meetings" point.

**Question:** How does one ascend and descend—should it be allowed to occur naturally, or should one force it with effort?

**Answer:** The jade fluid river chariot is akin to the ancient art of *daoyin*. It is neither left entirely to nature nor forced by effort. Rather, one guides it with intention and moves it with spirit. The human spirit and intention can reach anywhere, thus allowing such subtle circulation.

**Question:** How can saliva transform into qi, and how does one know when saliva has become qi?

**Answer:** During proper practice, one will feel the body entirely unobstructed, the head and eyes clear and refreshed, a warm qi like fire rising within the abdomen, and the mouth producing fluid as clear as water, welling up continuously. These are the signs that saliva has transformed into qi. Beginners may not yet reach this stage, but there is no need for haste—such effects will gradually manifest with practice.

**Question:** The texts say: "Use both hands to revolve the two breasts thirty-six times, then cup them together at the center." The breasts are fixed in place—how can they be moved? Even if they could be moved, how would one revolve them? How can they be cupped to the center?

**Answer:** "Cupping to the center" means using both hands to hold the breasts, compressing them into a firm sphere rather than letting them sag like bags. The right breast is pressed toward the left, and the left toward the right, preventing them from inclining to either side. At this time, one’s spirit and intention should silently reside in the "Central Altar" between the breasts. "Revolving thirty-six times" means gently massaging the nipples and areolae thirty-six times with the hands—it does not mean shifting the base. The base is fixed and cannot change its position. This method is not used by those who have preserved their virginity.

**Question:** What are "refining the medicine," "refining the form," "true fire," and "true tally"?

**Answer:** First refine the form, then refine the medicine—this is what was previously meant by first refining the physical substance, then refining the original essence. "True fire" and "true tally" refer to the subtle workings of advancing yang fire and retreating yin tally. The circulation of yin and yang is profoundly subtle, and its applications are manifold. Not only is it difficult to describe in writing, but even verbal explanation may not fully convey it. One must read many Daoist texts, diligently practice, engage in real cultivation, and contemplate at all times to gain correct understanding. Once sudden comprehension dawns, it can only bring personal satisfaction, for it cannot be easily communicated to others. The principles of yin and yang are indeed mysterious and ineffable.

**Question:** What does it mean to "damage the cinnabar origin"? What is the "central palace"?

**Answer:** The cinnabar origin is the foundation of internal alchemy. To damage it means that the qi scatters and the blood rushes, destabilizing the foundation of the elixir. The "central palace" lies below the chest and above the navel—since this is not acupuncture, there is no need to locate the exact point.

**Question:** What is "rushing the passes"?

**Answer:** "Rushing the passes" refers to the moment when one’s true qi is full and spontaneously activates. Because the lower orifice is tightly sealed and cannot leak outward, the qi rushes into the "coccyx pass," penetrates the "spine’s midpoint pass," and ascends directly to the "jade pillow pass." This is a natural surge of abundant qi—there is a genuine, powerful warmth within the body, not an imagined or forced circulation. An ancient poem says:

*"The river chariot through the spine ascends the crown gate,
The swiftest path to immortality—none more revered.
From jade pool’s fluid, swallow again and again,
The Purple Palace’s sovereign soars straight upward.
Let qi surge through the joints unceasingly,
Naturally essence fills and the Valley Spirit abides.
Once the path to longevity is recognized,
Gratitude must be felt for those who first guided the way."*

This process knows no difference between ancient and modern times, nor any distinction between male and female—it is the universal formula for attaining immortality and returning to one’s original nature. Beyond this, there is no other path.

**Question:** What is meant by "condensing qi and merging"?
**Answer:** It refers to concentrating the spirit into the qi cavity, the principle of heart and breath interdependence.

**Question:** What is "embryonic breathing"? What is the "Central Field"?
**Answer:** "Embryonic breathing" means the nose ceases to exhale, like an infant in the mother’s womb, where nasal respiration is absent. The Central Field is the Middle Elixir Field, also called the Crimson Palace, which is the *shanzhong* (center of the chest).

**Question:** What does "jade fluid returning to the root" mean, and why must qi be condensed to prevent its loss?
**Answer:** "Jade fluid returning to the root" refers to the work of qi transformed from the Sea of Blood returning to the breasts. "Using qi to condense it" echoes the earlier principle of condensing qi and merging—essentially, the interdependence of heart and breath.

**Question:** What is "returning the elixir"?
**Answer:** "Returning" means restoring one’s original state—replenishing a depleted body and restoring lost primordial qi.

**Question:** What is "postnatal"?
**Answer:** All things with form are called "postnatal," as they arise after the existence of heaven and earth. This is the broad sense. In alchemical texts, "prenatal" and "postnatal" often carry a narrower meaning: the fetus in the womb is "prenatal," while after birth, it is "postnatal."

**Question:** What is the "breathing within the Central Palace"?
**Answer:** As Immortal Cao Wenyi’s *Song of the Great Dao of the Numinous Source* states: *"When primordial harmony circulates internally, truth is realized; seeking breath externally never ends."* Zhuangzi says: *"The multitude breathe through the throat, but the perfected person breathes through the heels."* There is profound subtlety in this. Without having cultivated to this stage, no amount of explanation will bring clarity—only through direct practice and realization can one truly understand.

**Question:** What is meant by "each breath returning to the root"? Where is this "root" located?
**Answer:** One inhalation and one exhalation make a single breath. The root of the breath lies within the navel. When an infant is in the womb, its mouth and nose cannot breathe; it relies entirely on the umbilical cord connected to the placenta, which attaches to the mother’s uterus. The circulation of blood and qi flows between mother and child, allowing the infant to grow in the womb. If one wishes to return to the origin and restore the primordial state, one must trace back the path of one’s coming, returning to the state of chaos. Only then can one truly affirm, *"My life is determined by me, not by Heaven."*

**Question:** What does "slaying the Red Dragon" entail? Is it simply the cessation of menstruation?
**Answer:** It refers to refining and severing menstruation, not merely stopping it. Ordinary women may occasionally cease menstruating due to illness, but this is a pathological state. Refining and severing menstruation, however, is a deliberate practice, entirely different from illness. Young girls who have not yet begun menstruating are spared this stage of work. For elderly women whose menstruation has already ceased, they must first regulate their bodies and cultivate the practice to restore menstruation before refining it away—a far more intricate process.

**Question:** What is the form of "internal breathing"?
**Answer:** The function of "internal breathing" distinguishes between "prenatal qi" and "postnatal qi." When postnatal qi descends, prenatal qi simultaneously ascends; when postnatal qi ascends, prenatal qi simultaneously descends. The *Book of Changes* states: *"Closing the door is called Kun; opening the door is called Qian."* Opening and closing constitute transformation; ceaseless coming and going constitute communication. This principle bears a close relation to the method of internal breathing. Yet for those who have not reached this stage of practice, no amount of explanation will bring clarity. Beginners who have not yet mastered the foundational work cannot even begin to discuss internal breathing. Those who transmit the Dao but lack depth in practice, when describing the form of internal breathing, are like scratching an itch from outside one’s boot—leaving the learner with no way to proceed.

**Question:** When entering samadhi, one is speechless and motionless, like a corpse. What should one do in this state?
**Answer:** This is a natural phenomenon, not something forced. As for posture, one may sit cross-legged, sit upright with legs hanging, or recline with the upper body propped up—ordinary supine lying is less suitable. Those refining the yang spirit keep their eyes half-open; those refining the yin spirit keep them fully closed.

**Question:** After emerging from samadhi, one may eat and dress as one pleases. Does this mean one may take and use whatever one desires? And what of the warning to "guard against danger at every step"—does this refer to preventing unexpected disturbances?
**Answer:** "As one pleases" means one may freely dress and eat, with no attachment to preference. If there is attachment, desire lingers, and one cannot enter samadhi. Guarding against danger is not limited to one type; disturbances are among the risks to be prevented. At such times, one must have companions taking turns to keep watch day and night—hence the necessity for fellow cultivators.

*Collected Poems on Women’s Alchemy*
*Compiled by Fu Jinquan of Jinxī, Disciple of Unity*

**Editorial Principles of *Women’s Daoist Studies: A Small Collection*
Revised by Chen Yingning of Wanjian**

1. Carefully selected ancient and modern exemplary conduct and wise sayings from women, along with concise writings, to cultivate elevated character and impart exceptional intelligence in women—this pertains to the psychological aspect.
2. Carefully selected methods of cultivation, scriptures, treatises, and poems from past dynasties to foster extraordinary talents among women and strengthen the physical constitution of ordinary women—this pertains to the physiological aspect.
3. Daoist studies are the quintessence of the Chinese nation, unique to our country and absent in others. Previously, due to the limited spread of women’s education, this transcendent discipline remained unstudied. Now, following the principle of equality between men and women, this knowledge is gradually being made public.
4. Ancient writings vary in depth; some are profoundly abstruse and difficult to comprehend, while others are overly vulgar and unengaging. To ensure accessibility, abstruse passages are annotated for clarity, and vulgar ones are refined to avoid offending refined sensibilities.
5. The purpose of this compilation is to promote China’s indigenous Dao.

**Notes for Readers**

1. Three printed editions of *Women’s Alchemy Poems* exist:
   - A standalone edition,
   - An appended edition within *The True Mechanism of Unity: A Simplified Record*,
   - A collected edition in *The Combined Women’s Alchemy Anthology*.
   Differences in detail and arrangement exist among them. Sun Bu’er’s poems appear only in *The Simplified Record*; other editions omit them. The postscript to *The Western Pond Collection* appears solely in the combined edition. In the standalone edition, *The Western Pond Collection* is placed in the latter section, while the combined edition features *Jishantang Poems* in the latter section, with *The Western Pond Collection* as a separate, preceding section. *The Simplified Record* omits *Jishantang Poems* entirely and titles the work *Women’s Golden Alchemy* rather than *Women’s Alchemy Poems*. Here, the best versions are adopted, with over 180 characters corrected.
2. Determining the true authorship of certain poems is difficult. Daoist texts often bear attributed names without verifiable sources. Moreover, such textual scholarship is unnecessary for cultivation.
3. Though these works are called "poems," their intent lies not in poetry. Do not judge them by literary standards. Where the original text is crude, corrections have been made, but passages that cannot be altered without losing their essence remain unchanged. Few women in China are literate, and even fewer excel in poetry or prose. What may seem vulgar or shallow to us might appear refined and profound to them. The dissemination of the Dao prioritizes universal salvation, not catering to the tastes of a few literati while excluding the majority. Kindly understand the compiler’s intent.
4. *Jishantang Poems* were written by transmitters of the Dao yet falsely attributed to immortals, perfected beings, or ancient mothers—sixteen in total. These attributions have been removed. Each poem now bears a four-character title for the reader’s convenience. The original small-character annotations remain unchanged.

5. The annotations by Huichunzi in *Xichi Collection* consist largely of empty discourses on profound mysteries. They bear no relation to the cultivation of destiny (*minggong*), nor are they truly necessary for the cultivation of inner nature (*xinggong*)—such verbose elaborations are superfluous. Nowhere in *The Complete Works of Patriarch Lü* do we find such idle talk, which does an injustice to the Patriarch. However, as a few passages do offer some clarity, they have not been excised. Readers should discern their value accordingly.

6.
