In all matters and principles under heaven, these three camps inevitably intermingle, and none can eliminate the others. Only one can eliminate oneself. If one endures slander without rebuttal, it is as if one has eliminated oneself. While we have no intention of attacking others, we also refuse to eliminate ourselves. Thus, the debates over the similarities and differences between immortals and buddhas arise. This is the natural course of events, nothing strange at all.

The ancient authors of Daoist texts were, in essence, of the reconciling disposition. Hence, their works often intermingled terminology from Confucianism and Buddhism, yet Confucians paid them no heed, having long tacitly accepted this. The opposing camp, however, sought to create a monolithic following to elevate the status of their own teachings and thus rejected such reconciliatory works, sparing no effort to suppress them. As for us, we strive to preserve the independent standing of the study of immortals, lest it be belittled by the opposing camp, and so we are unwilling to indiscriminately adopt Buddhist terminology.

What perplexes us most is that the Daoist texts popular during the Song, Yuan, Ming, and Qing dynasties were all written in the reconciliatory style. They freely borrowed terminology from all Three Teachings, with few works maintaining a singular purity. Thus, in the Daoist texts I have edited and published, though I have already revised and removed many Buddhist terms, some inevitably remain. To excise them entirely would risk distorting the original texts. Only when I compose my own Daoist works in the future will I be able to avoid Buddhist terminology entirely.

Moreover, ancient Daoist texts such as the *Daodejing* of Laozi, the *Nanhua Jing* of Zhuangzi, the *Zhouyi Cantong Qi*, the inner chapters of the *Baopuzi*, and the *Huangting Neijing* and *Waijing* contain no Buddhist terminology, which is why the older the Daoist text, the better. The only drawback is that the language of ancient works is often too abstruse for easy comprehension.

*The Rekindling of the Lute’s Fire* is a specialized text on external alchemy. Without having received the true transmission and practical experience in the furnace and fire of external alchemy, one will certainly fail to understand it. This book was published by the Alchemical Scripture Engraving Society, and I merely contributed modest efforts in proofreading. After its publication, the society’s staff presented me with several copies as remuneration, which I have long since distributed to those who study external alchemy.

*(Yu Yang’s note: *The Rekindling of the Lute’s Fire* is an exceedingly rare text, scarcely circulated within or beyond our borders. The Alchemical Scripture Engraving Society, fearing its eventual loss, undertook its publication to preserve this national treasure. I am deeply humbled by the honor of receiving this profound work from Master Yuan, and I dare not forget his kindness. Ashamed of my own inadequacy, I have neither had the leisure to study it thoroughly in the past nor, amidst these times of turmoil, the opportunity to attempt its practice. As I review it now, I cannot help but feel a lingering sorrow.)*

As I no longer have a copy on hand, I regret that I cannot present one to you. I hope you will forgive me. In my humble opinion, those who practice internal alchemy need not read books on external alchemy, for they pertain to an entirely different matter, bearing no relation to our bodies and only wasting mental effort.

The Immortal Studies Institute has been in operation since May of the twenty-seventh year of the solar calendar, completing its first year amid constant instability. Thus, no formal regulations have been established, nor have any fellow Daoists taken residence here. Whether it will continue in the future remains uncertain. As inquiries have come from fellow Daoists in other regions, I shall provide a general response here.

**Reply to a Lady from Anyang, Henan:**
I have read your letter with care. That you, at just over twenty years of age and having received a modern education, have wholeheartedly embraced this Way and resolved to pursue it is truly remarkable. Though circumstances may temporarily hinder your aspirations, where there is resolve, success is only a matter of time—do not be anxious. Such matters require the convergence of opportunity, the fullness of both fortune and wisdom, before one may hope to attain the goal. The more one frets, the less effective one becomes.

Consider this: as an ordinary mortal of flesh and blood, if you aspire to accomplish earth-shaking deeds, how must you cultivate deep perseverance and unyielding resolve? How must you embody fluid adaptability and perfect harmony? How must you endure every hardship and trial? How must you maintain equanimity amid adversity and change? Can such things be rushed? If you grow impatient, you risk afflicting your nerves—and all prior efforts may be undone. Would that not be a pity?

Regarding dual cultivation practice, it must only commence after the "Dragon-Slaying" stage to ensure stability. Otherwise, should the partner’s practice momentarily falter, losing their power of steadfast endurance, it would be as Zhang Sanfeng the Perfected One said: *"On the rapids’ edge, one cannot hold back the boat."* Thus, the immortal embryo may fail to form while the mortal embryo takes root, adding yet another layer of demonic obstruction.

After bearing children, one may naturally resume cultivation to restore the original state, yet compared to the time before childbirth, this inevitably introduces many complications and lacks the former ease. As for age differences, these certainly matter. However, if the partner’s internal cultivation runs deep, even a twofold age gap poses no harm. For instance, if through their labor they earn one hundred yuan, and they assist you with fifty, they still retain fifty for themselves. If through your labor you earn two hundred yuan, even if you assist them with one hundred, you still keep one hundred for yourself. In this way, both parties hold one hundred fifty yuan in savings. The next time, and every time thereafter—whether dozens or hundreds of repetitions—the outcome remains the same.

After several years pass, the two of you may become wealthy, but what is to be feared is depleting your principal rather than earning new wealth. After several years of spending, your capital will be exhausted, and poverty will immediately follow—this would truly be untenable! Such is the principle of dual cultivation. The ancients never spoke of it plainly; today, I have revealed but a fraction. This is already an unprecedented discourse—take heed.

**Appendix: Original Letter from a Certain Lady of Anyang**
To the Venerable Master Yuandun,

Since graduating from a women’s normal school in Henan, this disciple has felt the impermanence of worldly affairs and the unpredictability of life, thus harboring no affinity for the red dust and constantly cherishing the intent to cultivate the Dao. I have perused works such as *The Complete Works of Patriarch Lü*, *The True Principle of Heavenly Immortality*, *The Unity of Immortals and Buddhas*, *The Great Compendium of the Daoist Tradition*, *The Laozi’s Classic of the Dao and Virtue*, and various texts on women’s cultivation. Yet without true oral instructions, all remains in vain.

Unexpectedly, upon receiving the recently subscribed *Biweekly for Promoting Goodness*, I found my teacher’s vast learning, profound Dao, and towering virtue so deeply admirable that I prostrate myself in utmost reverence. In recent days, my longing for the Dao has grown even more fervent—I am as if entranced, forgetting sleep and meals, and yearn to present myself before your esteemed presence to receive the authentic transmission. Alas, constrained by circumstances, I cannot fulfill this wish, which is truly regrettable.

This disciple plans, after "severing the Red Dragon," to spare no sacrifice or hardship and resolutely pay homage to your honorable countenance. I implore my teacher to bestow great compassion, take pity on this woman (for women face greater difficulties in cultivation, often burdened by family ties, and with limited knowledge, seldom encounter enlightened masters), and secretly transmit the oral instructions. Should I attain any accomplishment in the future, it will all be by your grace. My gratitude is beyond words.

With deepest respect, I wish you peace in the Dao.

Below, I list further details of my circumstances:
1. I cultivate at home.
2. I have been married for several years, but my partner also practices the Dao and has long severed worldly attachments.
3. I am currently twenty-four years old, and my partner is forty-eight, though in robust health. May I inquire whether dual cultivation is feasible?
4. Only my birth mother is alive; I have one elder sister and one younger brother. I have no children and have decided against having any to avoid hindering my cultivation.
5. My family circumstances are sustainable.
6. I have read some texts from the Confucian, Buddhist, and Daoist traditions.
7. I have generally read all available books on women’s cultivation, though regrettably, such works are scarce in circulation and insufficient to meet demand.
8. My aspiration is to "ascend in broad daylight." What I fear is the unpredictability of worldly affairs—should I suffer unforeseen harm and my body be damaged, my goal would remain unattainable. Were it not for this, my resolve would surpass that of men a hundredfold.

**Yundun’s Addendum:** Since you have graduated from a women’s normal school, your command of classical Chinese must be excellent. The reason I have not accepted female disciples in the past is precisely because their level is generally too shallow to enter the gate. Your resolve to study immortality and the breadth of your reading set you apart from ordinary women of conventional households. Here, I present you with a topic of considerable relevance to the study of immortality. Please compose an essay for the appreciation of fellow cultivators. The topic is as follows: *"On the Similarities and Differences in the Fundamental Tenets of the Four Schools: Confucianism, Buddhism, Daoism, and Immortality Cultivation."*

**Answers to Thirteen Questions from a Lady in Shanghai**
**By Yundunzi**

**Question One:** Are the initial steps of practice the same for men and women?
**Answer:** According to what I consider the most stable and sublime method, the initial steps of practice are identical for men and women. As one progresses, however, differences gradually emerge due to the natural distinctions in male and female physiology, not by human design.

**Question Two:** When practicing the *"Severing the Red Dragon"* technique, how many hours should one practice daily? How long does it take to fully sever the Red Dragon? After severing it, what practice should follow, and is there a clear stage of completion?
**Answer:** For formal practice of this technique, one should engage in four sessions daily, each lasting two hours—eight hours in total, or four *shi* (double-hours). The swift may achieve severance in half a year, while others may require a full year. After severance, one naturally advances to the next stage of practice. Beginners, however, should not yet concern themselves with this. Once the menstrual cycle is refined and ceased, the practice may be considered complete for that stage. If one chooses not to continue, resting for several years is permissible, provided proper preservation is maintained. Otherwise, if the cycle returns after cessation, additional effort will be required.

**Question Three:** Is there a fixed limit to daily practice time, or is more always better? What are the most suitable times for practice, or can one practice at any time?
**Answer:** Certain minor methods require adherence to specific *shi* (double-hours). For superior methods, however, no fixed time is necessary. Practicing four *shi* (eight hours) daily is sufficient; excessive practice may lead to aversion and subsequent obstacles. There is no single optimal time for practice, though it is inadvisable to practice immediately after eating or when fatigued and drowsy.

**Question Four:** In refining the elixir, should one adhere to a vegetarian diet, consume meat, or is either acceptable? Are there particular foods to favor or avoid? Do cultivators require air and sunlight in the same way as ordinary people?
**Answer:** While a vegetarian diet is pure, it should not be overly bland. Meat, though nourishing, should not be consumed in excess, especially strong-smelling varieties. Among vegetarian foods, mushrooms, bamboo shoots, and fresh fungi—though palatable—are prone to inducing illness and should be avoided. Seasonings such as MSG, flavoring powders, and soy sauce concentrates are also inadvisable. If consuming meat, one may occasionally eat chicken, duck, fish, and eggs, but other meats should be minimized. Air must be exceptionally clean, free from dust, coal smoke, foul odors, and impurities. Indoor air should circulate freely; doors and windows should not be tightly sealed. Sunlight is naturally beneficial, but in a meditation chamber, it should not be too bright—slight dimness helps settle the spirit. If full sunlight is needed, one may go outside to absorb it.

**Question Five:**

**Question Five:**
At what maximum age can women no longer cultivate? At what maximum age can men no longer cultivate? Or, if one obtains the proper method, may cultivation proceed regardless of age?

**Answer:**
By ordinary reasoning, men at sixty-four and women around fifty, when *tian gui* (heavenly essence) is nearly exhausted, find it difficult to practice *minggong* (life-cultivation). Yet this is a rigid principle and cannot be taken as absolute. The marvelous arts of immortals lie in rejuvenation and creation from nothing—seizing the power of transformation from Heaven through human effort. If one remains bound by age, awaiting death with folded hands, then the arts of immortality are hardly worthy of esteem.

**Question Six:**
In a bimonthly publication, I read that “men cultivate to achieve non-leakage of essence, while women cultivate to achieve non-leakage of menses.” Does “non-leakage of menses” refer to the practice of “severing the red dragon”? And how is “non-leakage of essence” in men to be understood?

**Answer:**
“Women cultivating to achieve non-leakage of menses” indeed refers to the practice of severing the red dragon. “Men cultivating to achieve non-leakage of essence” means the permanent absence of seminal emission through masturbation, nocturnal emission, urinary incontinence, or intercourse.

**Question Seven:**
Cultivation is divided into *xinggong* (nature-cultivation) and *minggong* (life-cultivation). Which of the three stages—“refining essence into *qi*,” “refining *qi* into spirit,” and “refining spirit to return to emptiness”—pertains to *xinggong*, and which to *minggong*? Or are all three *minggong*, with *xinggong* following a separate method?

**Answer:**
In superior cultivation, *xing* (nature) and *ming* (life) are originally inseparable. The distinction between *xinggong* and *minggong* is merely a provisional teaching. For the sake of guiding beginners, we may temporarily classify “refining essence into *qi*” as *minggong*, “refining *qi* into spirit” as half *minggong* and half *xinggong*, and “refining spirit to return to emptiness” as entirely *xinggong*.

**Question Eight:**
If one cultivates only *xing* and not *ming*, can one attain longevity? If not, how does the outcome differ from that of an ordinary person? Is there a distinction between “longevity without death” and “ascending to Heaven in broad daylight”?

**Answer:**
The effect of longevity arises from the practice of *minggong*. Without *minggong*, longevity is impossible. Those who cultivate only *xinggong* will nonetheless differ from ordinary people in their end: some may die without illness, some may foreknow their time of death, some may pass away in seated meditation, and some may seize another’s body for rebirth—all feats beyond the reach of ordinary people. “Longevity without death” is but an initial effect, while “ascending to Heaven in broad daylight” is the ultimate result, with profound differences in their levels.

**Question Nine:**
Daoist texts speak of the “four essentials”—method, wealth, companions, and place. Regarding the matter of wealth, what is the ideal amount required for perfection, and what is the minimum necessary?

**Answer:**
This question concerns practical matters, not abstract theory. A few words cannot suffice; a detailed analysis is needed.

Based on Shanghai’s cost of living, rent amounts to forty yuan per month, food to thirty yuan, and miscellaneous expenses to thirty yuan—totaling one hundred yuan in monthly expenditures, which suffices. At the very least, fifty yuan per month is required; anything less would likely be impossible. If one leaves Shanghai for a lower-cost city elsewhere, fifty yuan per month would suffice in place of Shanghai’s one hundred. For instance, fifteen yuan for rent, fifteen for food, and twenty for miscellaneous expenses would already constitute a comfortable standard of living outside Shanghai. If one departs from urban areas altogether and resides in a mountain monastery among renunciates, with lodging and meals entirely managed by the monastic community, thirty yuan per month would suffice.

The above calculations apply to individuals. For a group, expenditures may be reduced: the more people involved, the lower the cost per person; the fewer the people, the higher the cost—an inverse proportion. My usual advocacy for collective organization stems precisely from the aim of reducing expenses for fellow practitioners, though the conditions for this have yet to fully ripen. Some individuals, capable of enduring hardship, may further reduce their expenditures, while others, seeking comfort, may require even more.

The above estimates pertain to a moderate standard of living—neither ascetic nor indulgent. They do not include medical expenses, social obligations, or travel costs. Furthermore, personal expenditures such as those for one’s family—parents, spouse, or children—are entirely separate matters.

**Question Ten:** If circumstances permit, one abandons all worldly concerns with unwavering resolve and encounters no unforeseen obstacles, how many years would be required to achieve success in cultivation?
**Answer:** To restore the body to health and freedom from illness takes approximately three years. The work of "severing the Red Dragon" requires another two. Beyond that, progress depends on circumstances and cannot be predetermined. Moreover, this applies specifically to you; for another individual, the timeline would differ.

**Question Eleven:** Many Daoist adepts also practice martial arts. Does practicing martial arts benefit or harm the Dao of internal alchemy?
**Answer:** If practiced naturally and in harmony, it may offer some benefit. But if pursued recklessly or with brute force, it risks causing injury. However, each person’s constitution differs, so no universal rule applies. Given your current physical condition, martial arts may not be advisable; it would be better to focus solely on quiet nurturing.

**Question Twelve:** Internal alchemy distinguishes between solitary and dual cultivation. Which is more advantageous, which more harmful? Which is superior, which inferior? Which yields faster results, which slower?
**Answer:** This is a major question that has remained unresolved since the Han dynasty. Circumstances—environment, family, age, era, customs, propriety, law, morality, religious belief, scholarly pursuits, gender, innate capacity, and lineage of transmission—vary so widely that they naturally diverge into two distinct paths. I cannot favor one over the other.

I have read hundreds of texts advocating exclusively for dual cultivation or solitary practice. Over the past thirty-seven years, I have encountered countless individuals devoted to one or the other. Each path has its own advantages and disadvantages, strengths and weaknesses—how could I presume to judge or critique them?

If I were to praise dual cultivation while disparaging solitary practice, only those with ample resources or householders with spouses could pursue it. What of those facing financial hardship or renunciates? To claim that poverty precludes cultivation unless one first amasses wealth, or that monastic life precludes cultivation unless one first returns to lay life, would discourage the determined and diminish the spirit of the noble—words I cannot bear to utter.

For further discussion, refer to *Yangshan Journal*, Issue 74, page 6, in responses to Mr. Zhang Daochu of Suzhou (Questions 3, 4, and 6), and Issue 76, page 8, in the letter to a certain physician on internal alchemy.

Let us set aside internal alchemy for now and speak of human affairs. Consider this: Is it better for a person to marry or remain single during their decades of life? This, too, is not easily answered. Marriage has its benefits and drawbacks, and the circumstances are complex. One cannot impose a rigid or authoritarian view, for it would leave others unconvinced.

Those who insist on solitary cultivation or dual cultivation, dismissing all else, are no different from despots and autocrats.

**Question Thirteen:** In internal alchemy, should one abstain from desire entirely, moderate it, or does sexual activity have no bearing on the practice?
**Answer:** For those who embrace celibacy, this is not an issue. For those with a spouse, the matter warrants consideration.

"Abstinence" means complete cessation, which requires mutual consent; if one party disagrees, it cannot be achieved. "Moderation" means exercising restraint without excess, which is easier to practice and more aligned with human nature. However, it still poses obstacles to dedicated internal alchemy.

Ordinary sexual relations between men and women are crude, scarcely different from lower animals. Compared to the Dao of immortality, the difference is as vast as heaven and earth. If one does not seek a profound and mysterious method to reverse the course of nature, but instead follows humanity’s inherited base instincts, acting impulsively, how can one escape the cycle of rebirth and shatter the immutable laws of birth, aging, sickness, and death?

Since I have not sought your consent, I will not disclose your name. If you have no objection, future questions and answers may be published under your real name, as was done with Ms. Chen Wuxuan of Baoying, who granted permission.

---

**Letter from a Lady in Anyang, Henan Province, with Responses**

To the Venerable Master Yuandun,

I was overjoyed to receive your reply in *Yangshan Journal* to my previous letter. Your words—"The more urgently one seeks results, the less they manifest; one must await the convergence of conditions" and "Only with both merit and wisdom can the Dao be realized"—are indeed immutable truths, valid for all time.

In the closing section, where you discussed the methods of dual cultivation, though you did not explicitly reveal the oral instructions, I was nevertheless able to grasp the underlying meaning. It was as if I had awakened from a dream, my long-standing confusion suddenly dispelled—I am overwhelmed with gratitude, which lingers without cease.

As for the topic you assigned, *"On the Differences and Similarities in the Core Tenets of the Confucian, Buddhist, Daoist, and Immortalist Traditions,"* asking me to compose an essay for fellow seekers of the Way to appreciate, I feel utterly unworthy of the task. Though I have glanced at a few texts from these four traditions, I have not studied them in depth. For how could one dare to speak recklessly of Confucian *loyalty and forbearance*, Buddhist *compassion*, Daoist *resonance*, or the Immortalists’ *nature and emotions* without profound scholarship and abundant experience? I dare not risk exposing my inadequacies and becoming a laughingstock, thus failing to meet your high expectations! *(The rest is omitted.)*

**Additional Query:**
Previously, while reading the sixth item in the general principles of *Master Sun Bu’er’s Annotated Poems on the Female Elixir*, I came across the statement: *"For women cultivating immortality, aside from the Celestial Origin’s ingestion of essences, all other methods are obstructed."* Your disciple remains unclear about this reference to the *Celestial Origin’s ingestion of essences*—I humbly request your guidance.

**Yuandun’s Response:**
Regarding the *Celestial Origin’s ingestion of essences*, you may first refer to the section on the *Danyang Chen lineage* in *"Correspondence with Physician Zhu Changya on Immortalist Studies"* under the *Theoretical Research* column in this issue. Then, consult the third question in *"Responses to Fifteen Queries from Daoist Zhang Daochu of Suzhou"* on page six of *Yangshan Journal*, Issue 74. These should provide a general understanding.

**Letter from a Lady in Shanghai, with Responses**

Yuandunzi (omitting prior text):

On the morning of the twenty-sixth day of the sixth month, at three o’clock, after sitting in meditation for a short while, I soon felt warmth at the *hai di* (perineum). After some time, the warmth spread to the navel wheel and breasts. Simultaneously, the crown of the head and feet grew hot and began to twitch. When stillness reached its utmost, I entered a state of primordial chaos—though this lasted but a moment. Such occurrences repeated six times. By the seventh, I suddenly felt my entire body contract, as if anesthetized, even my breath becoming unnatural. At that time, my head and chest grew intensely hot, nearly breaking into sweat, while the *wei lü* (coccyx) and spine felt unbearably heavy. I then applied the *Three Unmoving Method* to address it, enduring for twenty minutes before gradually easing. My breath also returned to normal. The above occurred on the third day after menstruation, much like the previous instance I reported in person—also on the third day after menstruation. The difference was that last time I sweated, whereas this time I did not reach that stage, though the duration was longer.

**Five Questions Attached**

**Question One:**
Before and after my menstrual period, I often feel warmth and swelling with twitching in the *xue hai* (Sea of Blood) and yin region, accompanied by soreness and numbness in both legs. Is this the stirring of *zhen yin* (true yin)? Though I attempt to gather and withdraw it with intent, sometimes I fail to contain it. I humbly request the oral instructions for absorption to prevent its loss and irretrievable escape.

**Answer One:**
This phenomenon closely resembles the stirring of *zhen yin*, though its qi is not yet sufficiently vigorous. To gather it back is not difficult—simply apply the *Three Unmoving Method*, which will suffice.

**Question Two:**
When circulating qi with intent through the *Microcosmic Orbit*, from the *wei lü* ascending to the *ding men* (crown), then descending from the crown—where should it stop? Does the speed of ascent and descent matter? Is it permissible to practice this during menstruation?

**Answer Two:**
Descend from the crown to the *zi gong* (uterus) and stop there. Initially, the ascent and descent should be slow, not fast. During menstruation, it is best not to practice circulation, though quiet sitting is permissible.

**Question Three:**
In *Ten Principles of Female Alchemy*, may I practice the *Nine Cycles of Refining Form* method?

**Answer Three:**
If you wish to attempt the movements as described in the text, you may do so, but proceed with utmost caution and avoid forcing it. If it arises naturally and harmoniously, that is best; otherwise, it is better to fall short than to overdo it.

**Question Four:**
When saliva arises in the mouth, where should it be swallowed?

**Answer Four:**
Swallow it as you would tea, directing it to the stomach. If someone claims there is another path, they do not understand human physiology—do not believe such talk.

**Question Five:**
As my weak constitution gradually recovers, may I be granted the formal oral instructions for *"Severing the Red Dragon"* to follow in cultivation and advance my practice?

**Answer Five:**
First study *The Authentic Methods of Female Practice* and the *Severing the Dragon* techniques in *Ten Principles of Female Alchemy*. When convenient, you may attempt them, but proceed gently and avoid forcing it, lest you deviate from the proper path and incur illness. After thirty months of practice, observe the changes. If any transformations occur, write to report them—or we may discuss in person. Should errors arise, correct them promptly.

*Correspondence with Physician Zhu Changya on Immortalist Studies*

Yuantunzi to Physician Changya, with kind regards:

I recently received the letter you sent to my wife Yizhu, along with four poems. From them, I learned of your noble aspiration to transcend the mundane—so unlike the mediocre masses, who cling to shallow habits and neglect long-term reflection. This is truly admirable!

In your fourth poem, you wrote:
*"Among mortals there are extraordinary men and women,
Who set their resolve to ascend to the Ninth Heaven."*
I most heartily endorse these two lines. Though such an endeavor is exceedingly difficult and rarely realized, when we peruse the biographies of immortals across the ages, we find that in every dynasty, a few have succeeded. This alone suggests it is not entirely hopeless. Even if all the records of old are false, there is no reason we cannot pioneer the way ourselves. How much more so when the ancients have left behind guiding principles for us to follow?

During the two hundred and more years of the Qing dynasty, the finest minds among men were shackled by the eight-legged essay, while the brightest women were constrained by outdated rituals. Not only did they dare not test the arts of immortality in practice, but they scarcely dared speak of them. They were forbidden not only from seeking teachers or like-minded friends but even from reading the relevant texts. (Around the age of ten, I took pleasure in reading *The Biographies of Divine Immortals* by Ge Hong in the *Han-Wei Congshu*, but I dared not let my elders know—had they found out, I would surely have been severely reprimanded.)

Thus, the cautious and dutiful flocked to Confucianism, while those seeking liberation turned to Buddhism. Confucianism, though grounded in common sense, often degenerates into rigidity and pedantry. Buddhism, though seemingly profound, falters in its failure to recognize the reality of human life. (Buddhism regards life as illusory, fostering a disdain for the body, yet it cannot escape the very physical necessities of "clothing, food, shelter, and movement"—matters intimately tied to the body. To deem life illusory while still striving to sustain it is a glaring contradiction.)

Daoism comprises two major schools: the Orthodox Unity (Zhengyi) and the Complete Perfection (Quanzhen). The former is the older tradition, while the latter emerged only after the Yuan dynasty. Both have since declined into obscurity, and there is no need to discuss them in depth. Having devoted over thirty years to the study of immortals’ arts, those who know me well understand my position entirely, while others may suspect that, in this age of science, I am merely promoting superstition. In truth, I harbor no superstition whatsoever; I simply recognize that the study of immortality can remedy the deficiencies of human life, and its capacity surpasses all worldly sciences. Whatever problems ordinary science cannot resolve, the arts of immortality can. Moreover, it is grounded in practical, step-by-step practice—unlike Confucianism, which offers no path beyond mere moral conduct; unlike Buddhism, which provides no method beyond chanting the Buddha’s name; unlike the Orthodox Unity school of Daoism, with its talismans and incantations; and unlike the Complete Perfection school, with its repentance rituals and scripture recitations. Thus, the arts of immortality possess an independent character, lying beyond the scope of the Three Teachings, though adherents of any of the Three Teachings may freely pursue them.

Historically, Confucians who studied immortality include the great Han dynasty scholar Liu Xiang and the Song dynasty master Shao Yong. Buddhists who did the same include the Song dynasty Chan master Xue Daoguang and the Qing dynasty Chan master Liu Huayang. Daoists who pursued immortality are too numerous to count. Beyond these, figures such as Prince Wang Ziqiao of the Zhou dynasty, the favored courtier Dongfang Shuo of Emperor Wu of Han, the minor official Ma Mingsheng of Qi, the aristocrat Yin Changsheng of the Han, the recluse Wei Boyang, the thaumaturge Zuo Yuanfang, the Tang dynasty scholar Lü Chunyang, the Yan dynasty prime minister Liu Haichan, the general Zhongli Quan, and the magistrate Zhang Sanfeng—all of whom, according to popular tradition, are acknowledged as immortals—were laypersons, not monastics. They were neither monks, nor Daoist priests, nor even followers of Confucius or Laozi. Later generations conflated the teachings of immortality with the doctrines of the Three Teachings, thereby obscuring the true nature of the immortals’ arts. It is as though one were to daub a fair, unblemished complexion with layers of pigment, believing it enhances beauty, only to invite ridicule from those of refined taste.

You have studied in America for many years, and your scientific mindset goes without saying; your knowledge of modern medicine is undoubtedly extensive. While others might grow complacent, you, by contrast, display ever greater humility. Having recognized that the great matter of life and death cannot be resolved by medicine alone, you have taken a further step to seek the arts of immortality, cultivating thoughts that transcend the ordinary. Who but one with deep karmic roots and innate intelligence could aspire to such a path?

Miss Chen Wuxuan of Baoying once asked me, “Among women who possess both good fortune and wisdom, who might be expected to achieve success in cultivation?” I replied, “Those who combine good fortune and wisdom are exceedingly rare. Among such women, those who also love the Dao and may be expected to achieve success are even harder to find in today’s world. I have been diligently seeking them.” Now that I have found you, perhaps there is hope that you may meet the criteria.

At present, your medical duties still burden you, and you have not yet reached the stage of dedicated practice. Thus, you should first focus on studying the underlying principles. Below, I briefly outline the essentials of women’s cultivation for your perusal. I will also publish this manuscript in *Yangshan Journal* for the reference of all those across the nation who love the Dao, thereby fulfilling my long-held wish.

The study of immortality prioritizes longevity. The concept of longevity has existed since antiquity. Laozi said, “Deeply root and firmly secure [the stem]”; Zhuangzi said, “Guard the One and abide in harmony.” The *Suwen* states, “The True Person’s lifespan spans heaven and earth; the Perfected Person accumulates essence and preserves spirit; the Sage’s body does not decay.” Yet while such theories appear in the classics, the methods are not detailed in the records, leaving scholars disappointed. Only with the emergence of the *Cantong Qi* and the *Huangting Jing* did the arts of immortality’s cultivation and nurturance gain dedicated texts. Since the Tang and Song dynasties, alchemical scriptures have proliferated, yet their esoteric language and variant names create confusion, while divergent branches and winding paths lead practitioners astray. If even the methods for men’s practice remain unclear, how much less is known of the secret essentials for women’s cultivation?

Master Shangyangzi said, “In women’s cultivation of immortality, the breasts are the seat of generative qi. One must first accumulate qi in the breasts, then establish the furnace and cauldron to practice the Great Yin method of refining the body.” Alchemical texts often state, “Men cultivate to prevent seminal leakage; women cultivate to prevent menstrual leakage.” Yet when asked how qi can be accumulated or how menstruation can be prevented, they never provide explicit answers. The *Huangting Jing* states, “The teacher who transmits and the disciple who receives take an oath, hand in hand ascending the mountain to sip the elixir’s fluid; only then may the golden script and jade tablets be revealed.” The *Cantong Qi* says, “To express one’s thoughts on bamboo and silk risks leaking the celestial tally,” and further, “The Three and the Five unite as the supreme essence of heaven and earth; this can be conveyed orally but not transmitted in writing.” Thus, the secretive practices of cultivation schools did not begin today.

The reasons for the reluctance to transmit oral instructions lightly are fifteenfold, as detailed in the successive installments of *Oral Instructions: Unveiling the Profound* published in *Yangshan Semi-Monthly*. I will not repeat them here.

Here follows the translation:

In brief, there are six principal reasons:

First, life and death are the constant workings of creation, yet the study of immortality places utmost importance on longevity and transcendence of death—contending with creation itself. If lightly divulged or recklessly discussed, this may invite calamity and misfortune. (Though modern minds may dismiss this as superstition, the ancients were indeed governed by such concerns.)

Second, the distinction between orthodoxy and heterodoxy is finer than a hair’s breadth. “When the unorthodox practice the true method, the true method becomes wholly unorthodox.” Oral instructions are not recorded in writing lest they fall into the hands of the unorthodox.

Third, what is gained with difficulty is transmitted with equal difficulty—this holds true for all arts, and especially so for the elixir’s secrets.

Fourth, the Dao may be made known so that the world recognizes its existence, but its techniques must be guarded with reverence to preserve the master’s dignity and authority.

Fifth, the world lacks the Dharma-eye; who can discern yin and yang? If not deeply concealed, the teachings are easily slandered.

Sixth, the original intent of transmission is to ferry across those with karmic affinity. Yet if, through a moment’s misjudgment, the recipient treats the oral instructions as a rare commodity to be hoarded or bartered, this contravenes the transmitter’s purpose—hence the reluctance to transmit lightly.

The above reasons for secrecy pertain to the true method. As for those wandering alchemists who falsely assume the mantle of the Dao to amass wealth, they fall outside this discussion.

Profound in sudden enlightenment is the deep sorrow for those who, chasing after the form-bound, forget to return. Thus, there arises the desire to breach the ancients’ barriers and reveal their secrets, that extraordinary and untrammeled scholars—rich in lofty thought—may not misapply their wisdom and fall into peril. Yet affairs often thwart the heart’s intent, leaving only an unfulfilled aspiration. Here, I can but select what may be spoken and speak it.

Let us first discuss the schools of women’s cultivation. Since ancient times, alchemical formulas have relied on oral transmission rather than being recorded in texts, and this is especially true of women’s alchemical formulas. To trace their origins and exhaust their details, thereby forming a systematic study, is no easy task.

Examining the methods by which Daoist schools have traditionally been classified: some divide them by person, such as Qiu Changchun’s “Dragon Gate School,” Hao Datong’s “Mount Hua School,” and Sun Buer’s “Pure and Serene School,” among others. Others divide them by region, such as the “Northern Seven Perfected School,” the “Suo Five Patriarchs School,” Lu Qianxu’s “Eastern School,” and Li Hanxu’s “Western School,” and so on. Yet such classifications are ill-suited to women’s alchemical formulas. Moreover, they are divisions of doctrinal appearance rather than scientific categorization.

In my humble opinion, the schools of women’s alchemical formulas should not be divided by person or by region, but rather by method. This would lend interest to research and facilitate the seeker’s inquiry. Below, I tentatively list them as follows:

1. The Central Ridge Old Matriarch School

1. The Northern School begins with the refinement of sword techniques. There are two types of functions: the "Dharma Sword" and the "Dao Sword." Their origins and development are briefly recorded in *The Complete Works of Patriarch Lü*. In modern Daoist circles, two verses on internal refinement through sword techniques are still transmitted, offering a glimpse into this tradition. However, as the method is exceedingly difficult, few in the present age achieve mastery. Nevertheless, this approach constitutes a distinct lineage within immortal Daoism, and those who study these schools cannot disregard it. (Mount Zhongtiao is located in Yongji County, Shanxi.)

2. The Danyang Chen Matriarch School
This school emphasizes the cultivation and ingestion of Heavenly Origin Divine Elixir, along with talismans, registers, and summons. *Danyang* is a place name, and *Chen Matriarch* is a person’s name. Wu Meng of the Jin dynasty was originally the teacher of Xu Xun, but later, Xu Xun fully inherited the transmissions of Chen Matriarch. Wu, following the Matriarch’s command, again became Xu’s disciple. Perfected Lord Xu composed *The Stone Casket Record*, while Perfected Lord Wu wrote *The Bronze Talisman and Iron Contract*; both texts discuss the Heavenly Origin Divine Elixir, which was transmitted by Chen Matriarch. These two works represent the supreme teachings of elixir methods. Yet those who study the Dao in the world generally fear their difficulty and dare not attempt them. Since the Ming dynasty, with figures like Zhang Sanfeng and Shen Wansan, there have been few true adepts.

3. The Southern Marchmount Wei Lady School
This school focuses on "refined contemplation" and "visualization," revering the *Yellow Court Scripture* as its orthodox text. Since Wei Lady transmitted the *Yellow Court Scripture*, many female perfected ones of later generations have cultivated according to it, such as Lu Miaodian, Cui Shaoxuan, and Xue Xuantong. My humble work, *Lectures on the Yellow Court Scripture*, offers a fragmentary glimpse; if you have leisure, I invite you to peruse it briefly.

4. The Xie Ziran Immortal Maiden School
This school begins with "grain avoidance" and "qi ingestion," taking the *Central Yellow Scripture* as essential reading. Later, one may consult various qi formulas and methods for grain avoidance and fasting. It is suitable for the young and robust, but for the elderly or infirm, practicing this method exclusively may not be advisable.

Xie Ziran began her Daoist studies as a maiden of little more than ten years—how rare is such a one in ancient or modern times!

5. The Perfected Cao Wenyi School
This school begins with "clarifying the mind and reducing desires," "preventing the spirit from scattering outward," "focusing qi to attain suppleness," and "internally circulating primordial harmony." From start to finish, no other methods are employed—it is most simple and most accessible. For details, see the *Yangshan Semimonthly*, issue seventy-seven, "Song of the Great Dao of the Numinous Source."

6. The Primordial Lady Sun Bu’er School
This school practices the *Cultivation of Form through the Supreme Yin*. It begins with "severing the Red Dragon," which is the formal method of female cultivation. For details, see my humble work, *Annotations on Sun Bu’er’s Female Elixir Poems*.

The above six schools encompass the entire range of female cultivation methods from the Wei-Jin period through the past seventeen hundred years. Though scholars should be aware of the strengths and weaknesses of each school, as well as comparisons of difficulty and superiority among them, such discussions are not urgent for the present and may be deferred.

Additionally, there are methods such as "harmonizing *xun* and *gen*," Xia Ji’s "nourishing yin" techniques for "filling the flesh," and Feiyan’s "inner visualization" arts. There are also "secret chamber arts" and the *Su Nü’s Legacy Scriptures*—these involve "language that is not refined" and "matters of hidden intricacy," and thus are not suitable for open discussion.

Next, we consider the relationship between female cultivation and age. The *Plain Questions*’ "Discourse on the Heavenly Truth of High Antiquity" states:

The Yellow Emperor asked: "When people grow old and have no children, is it because their strength is exhausted, or is it determined by Heaven’s decree?"
Qibo replied: "In women, at seven years, kidney qi flourishes, teeth are replaced, and hair grows. At fourteen, *tian gui* arrives, the *Ren* vessel flows, the *Tai Chong* vessel flourishes, and menstruation begins at regular intervals—thus they can bear children. At twenty-one, kidney qi is balanced, the wisdom teeth emerge, and growth reaches its peak. At twenty-eight, sinews and bones are strong, hair reaches its fullest length, and the body is at its most robust. At thirty-five, the *Yang Ming* vessels begin to decline, the complexion starts to wither, and hair begins to fall. At forty-two, the three *yang* vessels decline above, the face is entirely withered, and hair begins to turn white. At forty-nine, the *Ren* vessel is depleted, the *Tai Chong* vessel weakens and diminishes, *tian gui* is exhausted, the earthly path is obstructed—thus the form deteriorates, and they can no longer bear children." (This describes the natural principles of the human body.)

The Emperor asked: "Yet some in their old age still bear children—how is this?"
Qibo replied: "These are those whose heavenly lifespan exceeds the norm, whose vessels remain unobstructed, and whose kidney qi is abundant." (This describes exceptions in physiology.)

The Emperor asked: "Those who follow the Dao may live to a hundred years—can they still bear children?"
Qibo replied: "Those who follow the Dao can ward off aging and preserve their form. Though their years may be long, they can still bear children." (This describes how those who cultivate the Dao can reverse the course of nature.)

According to the *Plain Questions*, this discussion seems to refer specifically to bearing children. Yet whether one follows the natural course to become human or reverses it to become immortal, the principle is the same—it depends solely on how one applies it. Thus, in female immortality cultivation, age makes a great difference:

1. Cultivation in Maidenhood
This refers to girls of around ten years who have not yet begun menstruation. At this time, primordial qi in the body is abundant, undivided, and whole; the spirit is unified, and desires have not yet arisen. If such a girl possesses innate wisdom and can devote herself to the Dao, her accomplishments will come easily—several times faster than those who begin later. For maidens, cultivation may bypass the foundational work of "building the base" and proceed directly to grain avoidance and qi ingestion, or to stillness and non-action through calming the spirit and seated meditation, as in the case of Xie Ziran.

2. Cultivation in Young Womanhood
This refers to girls from fourteen or fifteen to their early twenties who have begun menstruation but have not yet lost their virginity. At this stage, one should first use methods to refine and halt menstruation, restoring the state of maidenhood before proceeding with further work.

3. Cultivation in Mature Womanhood
This refers to unmarried women from twenty-two to thirty-five. The body’s physiology has reached its peak and is about to decline. At this time, menstruation may be regular or irregular, timely or untimely; there may be leukorrhea, flooding, or other ailments causing indescribable distress. One must first employ medical and hygienic methods to dispel stagnation, harmonize qi and blood, and invigorate the spirit—only then will cultivation yield results. This is more difficult than for young women.

4. Cultivation in Later Womanhood
This refers to women from thirty-five to forty-nine who have maintained their chastity without marrying. At this time, *tian gui* is nearing exhaustion, and the body’s vital qi daily weakens. Though they may have remained unmarried their whole lives, the depletion of their form is no different from that of married women. This is akin to men who never marry yet still cannot avoid aging—the principle is the same. Thus, the first essential in cultivation is to nourish and replenish the body’s deficiencies; there is no need to rush into "severing the Red Dragon."

5. Cultivation in Elderly Yin
This refers to women from forty-nine onward, up to sixty or seventy years. At this time, menstruation has ceased. One must daily practice cultivation, drawing in the generative qi of nature to replenish the generative qi within the body, gradually restoring menstruation to the state of a middle-aged woman. Then, through subtle practice of the mysterious work, one gradually refines it away until it resembles the state of a maiden. At this point, the bones and marrow become firm, qi and blood harmonize, the complexion glows with redness, the voice is soft and clear, white hair turns black, and fallen teeth regrow—this is called "reversing aging and returning to youth."

This practice may sometimes require twenty or thirty years to complete. (In the Eight Trigrams, *dui* represents the maiden, *li* the middle-aged woman, *xun* the timid woman, and *kun* the elderly Yin.)

6. Cultivation for Young Married Women
This refers to married women from sixteen or seventeen to twenty-six or twenty-seven years of age. At this time, their passions have just awakened, and desires are strong; the bonds of marital affection and family obligations are particularly constraining. Such conditions are most unsuitable for cultivation. Even if the woman herself is determined to cultivate, her husband will surely resist. A wife from a poor family need not envy those from wealthier ones. If she has neither in-laws above nor children below, and her household is prosperous with ample leisure, even if her husband is unwilling to sever worldly affairs, should she possess unwavering resolve and receive true transmission, she may secretly apply the technique of reverse circulation during her daily routines. This neither disrupts worldly affairs nor hinders progress on the immortal path. Though she may not immediately transcend the mundane, she will surely preserve her youthful countenance. However, those who have not yet mastered the "Severing the Red Dragon" practice are unfit to attempt this.

7. Intensive Cultivation for Middle-Aged Married Women
This refers to married women from twenty-six or twenty-seven to forty-six or forty-seven years of age. At this stage, they are burdened by household cares and preoccupied with their children, making cultivation seem entirely out of reach. If their husbands oppose it, there is no hope at all. But if husband and wife share the same aspiration, they may mutually agree to abstain from worldly affairs and each devote themselves to practice. If there are young children requiring nursing, a wet nurse must be hired, or substitute milk powder or semi-solid foods used; one must not feed the child with one’s own milk, lest it impede progress in cultivation.

8. Cultivation for Widows
For those who have married and been widowed, with no children or whose children are already independent, this is an opportune time to embark on the path of cultivation, filling the solitude of their later years. In the era of old Confucian ethics, widows were bound by reputation to maintain chastity; since the Republic, the custom of widow chastity has been overturned, yet remarried women still face societal disdain. Why not instead devote oneself to the study of immortal arts, finding solace for the spirit and joy in bodily health? Success would be ideal, but even if not fully attained, the results will be beneficial, sparing one from wasted effort. The initial practices differ little from those of unmarried women.

The above outlines the general paths of cultivation for women, which have seldom been openly discussed by past masters. Today, I reveal them to you in full.

Herein lies the same intent as when, twenty years prior, I composed the *Annotations on the Female Alchemy Poems* for the lady Lü Bicheng. Later, for reasons unknown, she returned to the Buddhist path. Who can foretell her next life? I fear she may no longer aspire to liberation in this present existence. Nevertheless, had it not been for that karmic connection, the *Annotations on Sun Bu’er’s Female Alchemy Poems* might never have been completed. To date, no fewer than two or three thousand copies have circulated both domestically and abroad. (*The Annotations* first appeared in *Yangshan Journal*, with two thousand copies distributed per issue. Later, woodblock editions were printed as standalone volumes, selling several hundred more.) Among these readers, some must have attained liberation through this work. Tracing its roots, the one who initially requested the annotations played no small part. Moreover, with the emergence of the *Thirty-Six Questions*, the gateway to female cultivation has advanced further. Readers will reap even greater benefits. Though she failed to liberate herself, she first liberated others—upon hearing this, Lü Bicheng would surely find solace. Yet what I expect of you surpasses even that. Lü’s merit lay solely in benefiting others; today, you should seek mutual benefit for self and others, achieving true perfection.

The supreme path of cultivation prioritizes attainment in this very life. Do not procrastinate or grow indolent, abandoning the present in pursuit of the illusory blossoms of future lives—this is my earnest hope. When Yizhu returned home, I learned of your eagerness to begin in earnest. Is this not most excellent? However, the subtleties of principle and method defy expression in mere words. When circumstances align in the future, I shall delineate the stages and discuss them face-to-face. For now, I offer this reply, with wishes for your well-being.

**Essentials of Female Alchemy from the *Recorded Sayings of Leyu Hall* by Master Huang Yuanji**

1. Shuduan, chaste and solitary in her suffering, aspires to cultivate the Great Dao—truly an extraordinary woman. I deeply pity and praise her. In essence, the study of the Dao requires nothing more than purifying the heart and cleansing the mind, emptying it to lay the foundation. Emptiness gives rise to numinosity, and numinosity reveals the true heart, from which the original nature emerges. This is but one facet of "illuminating the heart and perceiving the nature." Know that this is no arduous task. When one’s thought returns to the cinnabar field and the intent is used to observe it, the initial stirring of thought is the heart. Illumination means illuminating this—nothing more. Before thought arises, there is but a vast clarity, empty and undifferentiated, devoid of all things—this is the nature. Perception means perceiving this—nothing more. If one can truly illuminate the heart and perceive the nature, it is akin to the moment when, amid the congealing yin, a single yang stirs—suddenly, the light turns inward. This is the ancient saying: "At winter solstice, yang is born; at midnight, the living zi hour arrives." Here, one redirects the light to the breasts, where the water source is purest, to refine the immortal elixir. Beginning with dispelling illness and prolonging life, it culminates in sagehood and transcendence—all rooted in this single moment.

Yet, fearing that women may struggle to grasp such profound teachings, I offer a simpler method. When beginning practice, sit upright and composed, serene and tranquil. Be diligent and self-motivated. Reflect that nothing in this world is truly one’s own—even children and spouses dissolve in an instant, like travelers parting at dawn, each going their separate way. You are you; I am I. When the eyes dim and the breath ceases, this body becomes mere dust, leaving only the heart-nature. If one cultivates well in life, the spirit remains clear and luminous, free from torpor—ascending to heaven. Upon rebirth, with such clarity of heart and numinosity of nature, who would willingly descend into the ranks of beasts? Thus, those who nurture their heart-nature well reap its benefits for myriad lifetimes. Clearly, who would choose to be reborn into poverty and suffering? One must select a favorable birth—this is both principle and sentiment. For the uncultivated, death brings only confusion, like a blind man stumbling without regard for peril. If entangled by karmic debts, they may fall into the three evil destinies or be reborn into hardship—an inevitable fate.

2. Xianzhen and others, devoted to this path, have recently endured trials and tribulations, surely recognizing the illusory nature of worldly attachments and refusing to cling to them further. My teacher advises: even the greatest worldly wealth and love last but fifty or sixty years before parting. How much better to cultivate the Dao within, enjoying boundless, imperishable bliss? Seize this moment to see through the red dust, shatter the web of karma, and leap free with resolve. Day and night, focus on observing the breath within the breasts—counting each inhalation and exhalation, allowing it to flow naturally. As you exhale, let it rise without disturbing the head or eyes; as you inhale, let it descend without agitating the water palace. Let it proceed gently, unhurried and at ease. Whether a hundred or a thousand breaths, let it follow its course without counting. Simply concentrate the spirit in the breasts, regulating the breath there, following its natural rhythm of ebb and flow. This is sufficient.

**From the *Complete Writings of the Quanzhen School*: Patriarchal Admonitions for Women Cultivators**

**Patriarch Ma Danyang’s Gift to Sister Ma**

Jade Maiden, fairy of jasper, wears a jade gourd,
Amid lotus fragrance, she plays with the whirlwind’s might.
Within the *Moon over the Western River*, she gathers the sprouts of lingzhi.

Ninefold refinement complete, she enjoys eternal joy,
The Three Fields’ treasures congealed, she roams at will,
A guest of immortals, ascending to the azure heavens.

**To Sister Sun**

I urge you, Sister Sun, to cultivate the Great Dao,
Ever sweeping the field of the heart.
Slay the Three Corpses and the Six Wastes,
Free from affliction—
Ever pure, ever still, you shall know the profound mystery.

Ask not of strange names for furnace or hearth,
For harmony rises and falls, inverting high and low.
Lead and mercury naturally form the supreme treasure—
How wondrous!
Clouds of radiance escort you to Penglai Isle.

**To All Sisters**

Listen, sisters, listen well,
Learn from Ma Gu’s utmost purity,
Follow her wondrous goodness.

Be not clever, be not clever,
Outwardly simple, inwardly awake,
Your work complete, you ascend to Jade Capital.

**To Sister Ma**

Jade Maiden, fairy of jasper, wears a jade gourd,
Amid lotus fragrance, she plays with the whirlwind’s might.
Within the *Moon over the Western River*, she gathers the sprouts of lingzhi.

Ninefold refinement complete, she enjoys eternal joy,
The Three Fields’ treasures congealed, she roams at will,
A guest of immortals, ascending to the azure heavens.

**To Chen Gu, the Recluse of Cave Clouds**

Pure and still,
Delving into the profound,
Gazing at heaven, you discern the root-source.
Spreading and weaving the net of the Three Powers,
All phenomena transmit the sun and moon’s legacy.
Emulating Nüwa’s craft,
Gathering emptiness—
Five hues renewed.
Refining with fire,
The great work perfected,
Whole, without lack or excess.

Attaining unity, you dwell in serene earth.
Acting without contrivance,
Naturally, eternally enduring.

In the depths of profound obscurity,
She conceives and bears a celestial embryo—
This is the original face,
Ever luminous, though without mouth to speak.
When the time arrives,
A golden youth comes to announce:
She shall ascend to audience at the Primordial.

**To Yang Wuyi of Lingkou**

Yang the Maiden awakened to the Dao,
Her resolve fierce beyond compare—
Shattering the mirror-tower to pieces.
Seeing through the foul and putrid skin-sack,
She vowed never to groom or wash again.
With single-minded devotion, disheveled and unkempt,
She dons a straw cloak, wields a staff, and wears a bamboo hat.
She severs all cunning and wisdom,
Renounces the karma-fire of ignorance,
And dulls all sharpness of self.

How does this compare to men?
It surpasses even the soul-snatchers,
The life-demanding living ghosts.
Wealth and glory she casts aside,
No longer giving them a thought.
Abiding in constant purity, constant stillness,
Dwelling in non-action and the natural principle.
When merit and practice are complete,
She shall claim a place among the immortals
On the Ten Isles and Three Mountains.

**To Li the Maiden of Little Hu Village**

Maiden, in your cultivation,
Hear my earnest counsel:
First, sever all afflictions.
Capture the mind-monkey and intent-horse,
Lest they reverse course.
Like a resolute man,
Do not hesitate or act carelessly.
Swiftly take up the task,
Rely on utmost bitter resolve,
To avoid facing King Yama.

Cut away the myriad worldly ties,
Attain true joy and true bliss,
Gradually penetrating the profound mysteries.
Guard closely the non-action of pure stillness,
The natural Dao.
Responding to things with true constancy and ease,
Qi and spirit conjoin to form the elixir-treasure.
When merit and practice are complete,
Ride the auspicious clouds
To return to the Three Isles.

**To Wang the Maiden of Chang’an**

Marvelous is the sword of wisdom—
Formless and traceless,
Of pure steel, cleaving iron and severing nails.
It hews asunder the mountain of affection,
Strikes down the dust of love and desire.
It exterminates the Three Corpses and Six Bandits,
No need to brandish it—
Even gods and ghosts tremble.
Ever grasp it firmly,
To subdue the tortoise and serpent,
Ensuring peace beneath your feet.

This sword, all possess,
Yet only those who single-mindedly turn toward the Dao
Will witness its power unfold.
Further, with constant purity and stillness,
Its blade gleams bright.
Naturally, it penetrates the profound and wondrous—
Why then fret over an unenlightened nature?
When merit and practice are complete,
You shall surely ascend,
Bearing clouds to Penglai and Yingzhou.

**To the Two Maiden Sisters of Jingyang County**

Marvelous is the supreme principle—
Ever pure, ever still,
Easy to know, easy to understand, yet hard to practice.
To become an immortal,
You must take this as your foundation.
Cease seeking the profound or wondrous;
Simply settle the mind, dispel desires, and forget emotions.
Without attachment,
Without hatred or love,
Without contention or strife.

Once awakened, though touched, remain unmoved,
Aware that within the boundless,
All returns to tranquility.
Sun and moon share the same palace,
Day and night unite in radiance.
Not divine, yet divinity manifests—
Riding auspicious clouds,
Hastening to Penglai and Yingzhou.
Paying homage to the Jade Emperor,
Displaying true merit,
The Dao of pure stillness is accomplished.

**To Wang the Auspicious Recluse of Chang’an**

The matter of life and death is pressing—
What concern are sons and grandsons?
Blithely leap beyond the household gate.
Roam carefree beyond the mundane,
Dwelling in a tranquil pavilion free of affairs.
Ever settling the mind and cutting off thoughts,
This is called capturing the horse and seizing the monkey.
Sever worldly thoughts,
Awake and unburdened,
You shall comprehend the profound words.

The subtlest and most wondrous realm
Lies where wind neighbors moon, and companionship,
Where the roots of clouds take root in your feet.
In stillness, you know the cave’s interior—
A heaven and earth apart.
How can destiny be left to heaven and earth?
Shield your numinous clarity,
Return to the origin.
Return to Penglai Isle,
Where there is neither birth nor death, ever abiding.

**To Wei the Illuminated Recluse of Lingkou, Afflicted by Wind**

Though a woman in body,
Her will rivals a man’s—
Awakened, she leapt beyond the household gate.
Seeking the Dao,
She forsook her beloved daughter and handsome son.
Her nature like a solitary cloud or wild crane,
Unentangled by worldly dust.
Truly liberated and untrammeled,
She may well be called
A great man.

Recognizing the original purity,
Why trouble to acknowledge
Tiger-child or dragon-child?
No need to arrange
The maiden of Kan and Li,
The infant and the girl.
The great work of non-action is accomplished,
Bearing a formless child.
When yang is perfected,
She shall become
A child of the Most High.

**To the Assembly of Maiden Disciples**

Averse to wind and sun,
Cherishing your appearance,
You find comfort in the depths of painted halls.
Before the mirror, you groom,
Adjusting your golden-threaded silken robes.
Yet hearing of the immortal’s molting like a cicada,
You admire true cultivation,
Resolute and unwavering.
Forsaking splendor,
Disheveled and unwashed,
You take refuge in plain cloth.

The spiritual root, once afflicted,
Now demands your hands-on healing.
Circulate the sun and moon within your body,
Directly to the ends of the earth.
Naturally, wood and metal become treasure,
Revealing the divine pearl,
Shimmering in limpid streams.
Only then shall you know
These wonders
Are beyond thought or speech.

**To Lu the Maiden**

A wisdom-lamp soars to the Milky Way,
Auspicious smoke of the Eight Trigrams billows forth.
Ninefold golden elixir refines the jade nature,
The jeweled path to the Ten Isles lies smooth before you.

**To Xue the Recluse of Pine Stream**

The great taboo of cultivation is extravagance—
Shatter all attachments, refine wheat and hemp.
Reversing yin and yang, penetrating creation,
Naturally, the furnace congeals the great cinnabar.

**To Chai the Maiden**

Non-action, non-effort—great cultivation.
Intent pure, mind clear, the wisdom-lamp shines.
Illuminating the Nine Passes, traversing freely,
On Kunlun Mountain, you ascend.

**To Yan the Recluse of Frost Stream**

With single-minded devotion to the Dao, never turning back,
Eight-flavored nectar irrigates the great elixir.
The Nine Luminaries harmonize the true jade nature,
With merit complete, you ascend to the immortal altar.

**To Liu the Maiden of Hu County**

A single grain lodged within the abdomen,
The secret transmitted, transforming the vulgar stream.
Fire mutually engenders, preserving life and nature,
Penetrating nature, she is called Immortal Liu.

**Matching the Rhyme of Cao Jieyuan’s Wife, the Non-Action Recluse Jia Wu’er**

Fire in the same palace, two intermingling,
By the moon’s light, brilliance shines forth.
Awakened to the Dao, worldly dust is cast aside,
Timely settled, never sinking into the desert.

**To Old Zhang the Maiden of Hucheng County and the Assembly of Maiden Disciples**

Extraordinary ones act extraordinarily,
Wielding the Three Wonders and Six Xin.
The jade tree ever flourishes, ever watered,
The jeweled blossoms never fade, untouched by spring.

**To Wang the Maiden of Hu County and the Assembly of Maiden Disciples**

Leisurely and serene, nurturing the true person,
No need for wine, meat, or pungent flavors.

**To Maiden Zhao of Huxian County and the Assembly of Maiden Disciples**

Three cups of ghee bestow a thousand autumns,
One peach of Penglai grants ten thousand springs.

**To Maiden Sun the Second**

In non-action, purity refines the elixir-maker,
Spared from the ten thousand sorrows of red dust.
The Mystic Garden yields the fruit of no-leakage,
The Water Realm’s withered spring leaves no trace.

**Master Wang Chongyang**
**Maiden Zhang’s Inquiry**

Nine-petaled golden flowers ever unfurl,
Eight channels of jade waters freely flow.
Seven gates, each attained, all lead through,
Four images, bright and clear, wholly still.
One grain of divine elixir returns to the true path,
Two silver threads bind the primal beginning.
Radiant and resplendent, know the transmission—
Fruit realized without remainder, medicine in surplus.

**To Maiden Sun the Second**

Maiden Sun was the wife of Ma Yu. The Master twice halved a pear to share with the couple, wishing their transformation together. Yu followed the Way for over a year, and Sun also left home to embrace the Dao.

Ten divisions of the pear—years past,
Heaven grants the season, nature’s decree.
Why not depart then and there?
Only to await the golden lotus’ bloom.

**To Maiden Sun**

The Second Granny still clings to her estate,
Yet who knows wealth will ruin her coin?
Had she kept home as before,
Master Ma would have no share in immortality.

**To Maiden Sun**

At home, she was but "Second Granny,"
Once departed, the hearth’s fire is gone.
If she masters cultivation and refinement,
Others will forever call her "Immortal Maiden."

**Tune: "Bu Suan Zi"**
**Jiao the Maiden’s Request**

Hearing the gate of exposition unsealed...

**To Yang the Maiden**

Extinguish the worms, the hearth and stove stand firm and secure.
Refine the pure and constant, gaze beyond the void,
Utterly clear, utterly still—thoughts and cares dissolved.
Press down in frequent forgetfulness,
Bind the golden elixir,
Pierce through to the Hall of Light.
The axe’s long blade wields a steel-edged sword,
Offering the true origin,
Straight to the path of Penglai.

**Master Tan Chuduan**
*Presented to Yang the Maiden*

Eighty Daoist nuns of Yang’s Investiture into the Mysteries,
A single blossom of jade flower, fragrant after autumn’s fall.
The jade chalice brims with water of purity,
The golden censer must burn incense of wisdom’s spirit.
Now you have secured blessings for the days to come,
Henceforth, what doubt remains of past misfortunes?
The silent, profound, empty hall is free of hindrance,
Naturally, the jeweled tripod reveals rosy clouds.

**To the Three Sisters of Marshal Wang’s Residence**

If you wish to cultivate amidst the worldly,
First extinguish the distinctions of self and other.
In softness and weakness, guard poverty’s clarity,
In steadfastness, remain unchanged from beginning to end.
True goodness—true goodness—
Only in harming the self to benefit others lies true convenience.

**Master Wang Chuyi**
*The Wife of the Surveillance Commissioner Seeks a Cure for Illness*

Heaven births, heaven nurtures—follow heaven in cultivation,
Disregard the dusty world and its mortal bones.
How can the Four Borrowings ascend the Phoenix Terrace?
How can the Three Corpses reach Yingzhou’s isles?
Awakening to truth, inward illumination forgets new attachments,
Returning to the root, the heart’s embers extinguish old sorrows.
The formless true emptiness transcends the other shore,
Steadily riding the great divine vessel of ease.

**To the Abbess of Weizhou’s Abbey, the Grand Lady**

Who has encountered a living immortal?
Certainly, one who nurtures the numinous and bright, piercing the azure sky.
From the jade treasury, true water and fire are drawn and replenished,
In the jasper palace, auspicious clouds and mist congeal.
Form and spirit both wondrous—who can fathom?
Void and color both dissolved—how can the principle be conveyed?
The four seas turn their light in unison to revere the teaching,
Naturally, merit and virtue fill the three thousand realms.

**Sent to My Mother**

Mother and child cultivate the true and leave the household together,
Embodying heaven and modeling the Dao as our livelihood.
Wherever we transform conditions, the gods and spirits assist,
Exhorting goodness again and again, blessings and longevity increase.
Worldly ties and affections are no longer discussed,
The mysteries of the Dao’s gate and its virtue never err.
When the inner spirit ascends and transforms, it merges with the true,
One day, our merit complete, Penglai’s isles will be praised.

**To My Aunt and Mother-in-Law on Cultivation**

Aunt and mother-in-law both speak of the heart’s devotion to the Dao,
Yet do not indulge in extravagance, lest it all come to naught.
First awaken to true cultivation, forgetting self and other,
Do not let external seeking send you wandering east and west.
Qi and spirit must be made to attend the Ocean of Origin,
The monkey and horse must be locked within the Crimson Palace.
Subduing the host of demons without obstruction,
Freely and at ease, you will complete the immortal’s work.

**To Daoist Nun Liu Zhimiao of Huang County on Daily Practice**

Scrutinize daily practice with painstaking care,
Where the eye falls, turn the light—all deeds perfected.
Refine the self to temper the true and magnify the great Dao,
The path ahead will naturally extinguish all transgressions.

**To the Cypress of Longevity and Protection at Fushan**

From ancient times to now, true compassion establishes great merit,
Only after three heavens does one encounter trust in haste.
Gradually, joy and glory reveal the profound mystery,
Attaining unity, you know the Dao is shared by all.

**To Jiang the Maiden of Fushan on Cultivation**

Studying the Dao is like smelting gold in fire,
True gold, once refined, gleams with deep purple light.
Within the mysterious palace, employ the three treasures of the body,
A single orb of perfect clarity comprehends ancient and modern.

**To Sui the Maiden on the Mind-King**

The mind is the great Dharma-King of all dusts,
Do not let it be inverted, chasing after drifting things.
Naturally, inner and outer unite in true emptiness,
Then return to Penglai to recognize your homeland.

**Sent to *Cloud Record*, with This Quatrain Presented to the Lady of Gold and Purple**

Pure tones and wondrous conduct, without private bias,
Even a drop of water’s kindness is hard to forget.
Secretly, from the immortal’s palace, ascend the purple paths,
Your name is already prepared in the *Cloud Record*.

**The Surveillance Commissioner’s Wife Seeks Instruction Due to Illness**

Birth and nurturance are the Dao’s constant way,
Yet how is it that people all turn their backs on this path?
Not cherishing the original, pure, and tranquil master,
How can this colored body ever attain peace and health?

**To the Female Assembly of the Hall of Cultivation**

Regardless of age, engage in true cultivation,
Each unfolds in tranquil leisure, reaching the shore’s vessel.
Light fills the ten directions, freeing from suffering and peril,
Forever dwelling beyond the heavens—most refined.

**To Gao the Maiden of Laiyang**

The mind is the bodhi, the great Dharma-King,
Do not become inverted, chasing after waves in haste.
Naturally, a single point of numinous emptiness coalesces,
Then return to Penglai to enter your homeland.

**To Zhang the Maiden of Dizhou Seeking Instruction**

Born of the great Dao, fragrance is natural.
With a true master, examine carefully.
The path is open, immortal tidings arrive.
Only when the ashes are cold does the nature stretch and lengthen.

**Excerpts from *The Heart Talks of Two Lazy Ones*: Women’s Alchemy Section**
*(Editor’s Note: This compilation reveals the secret of "retaining the gaze to forget and advance the fire," a method hitherto unrecorded in the alchemical canon. First retain, then forget—retain at "a certain place," then forget at that same place, and true fire will naturally arise there. When true fire is born, the turbid yin-qi of illness dissolves of itself. Yet the bellows must be unobstructed for the practice to avoid error. The method: first direct intention to a certain place and still the mind. Stillness deepens into softness, softness into release, release into forgetting, forgetting into deeper forgetting—then true fire ignites like flame.)*

**Preface**
These two individuals—of unknown origin and unrecorded surname—appear, from their *Heart Talks*, to be adepts of the nurturing arts, poised to embark upon the Southern Palace. I copied their words with delight, for their speech is shallow yet profound, coarse yet refined. Their intent seems purposeful, far removed from the obstinate recluses of this world, and their tastes align with my own. Thus, I have excised the unorthodox and preserved the essential, naming the collection *The Heart Talks of Two Lazy Ones*. One styles himself "Lazy Old Man," the other "Great Lazy One." The character for "lazy" (懒) here takes its sense from the heart, not the woman radical, implying reliance on the heart in learning—akin to Su the Lazy Old Man. For they are those who engage with the heart of Heaven and the heart of the Dao.

Respectfully transcribed and annotated by Xiao Gen on the fifteenth day of the eleventh lunar month in the *wuyin* year of the Jiaqing reign.

**Encounter**
You drift like duckweed in the southern skies, I in the northern earth. This autumn’s sudden meeting felt as natural as a lifetime’s acquaintance—destined, indeed! Your teacher was Li the Barefoot, mine the Venerable of Great Emptiness. Though no cause bound us, our masters’ silent accord brought us together. You recently befriended the Master of the Northern Wall, who received transmission from the Stone Mirror Mountain Daoist. What you may not know: his master’s master, the clear-sighted Jin Huaihuai Wang, is also your master’s master. Thus, your teacher, Red Master, and my Stone Mirror Mountain Daoist were fellow disciples under the same master. I once studied under Jin the Cup-Holder, having been introduced by the Venerable of Great Emptiness. You and I are kin in lineage, and at first meeting, we blended like water and milk. Our school is one, our practice identical—our tastes naturally harmonize. What Dao did the Master of the Northern Wall impart to you?

**The Heart Talks of Two Lazy Ones**
*Question:* What have you learned?
*Answer:* Only the single aperture of the Mysterious Pass and the union of heart and kidney.
*Question:* Whether the Mysterious Pass is open is not difficult to discern. But if the method of opening it remains unknown, how do you propose to unite them?
*Answer:* I seek instruction.
*Reply:* We are of one family—how could I remain indifferent? For now, we are but drifting duckweed, but one day we may together dissect the profound mysteries.

**A Good Question**
Great Lazy One asked: The ancients said the subtle mechanism lies in the eyes. I wish to fathom its wonders.
Lazy Old Man rose solemnly and said: An excellent question! The entire body belongs to yin, and what transforms yin back to yang are the two eyes. This is the first oral instruction for entering the Dao. Since you already know this, use it for inner illumination, and every step will be the Dao—the Mysterious Pass may yet open.
*Question:* Where does inner illumination begin?
*Answer:* Close your eyes and regulate the breath for a time. When the breath feels settled, begin by gathering intention and spirit in the brain *(Editor’s Note: This is a general term for progressive stages. Beginners may practice the Brahma Heaven "Ī" seal, using spirit-intention to envelop the entire surface of the head, with the root of the nose as the foundation. Advanced practitioners may enter the brain’s interior; the most advanced unify inner and outer, merging the five senses.)*. With the eyes’ light, glance slightly toward the crown.
